THE RED PAGE
The Best Australian Prose
Although the number of authors draw*
upon for this competition is not great,
the passages received are numerous and var-
ied, and many of them are of nearly equal
merit.
It was uot expected that our young litera-
ture would afford any of those jets of ele-
vated diction which arise naturally out of
the good prose of English masters. Several
of the paragraphs sent in. however, were the
showy imitation purplb, the attempts at line
writing which looked highly-colored against
their drab surroundings.
Style is a volatile spirit which escapes de-
finition. The text books on rhetoric will ex-
plain what it isn't; hut let us^ay—vaguely
—ja good style is that which appeals most
strongly to the leader's imagination. It may
appear in short, stabbing sentences, or long,
billowy periods; it may be ornate, like Bus-
kin’s, archaic like Morris’s or Hewlett’s;
crammed with metaphor like Meredith’s, or
as simple as Huxley’s. Whatever it may be,
it is not a garment that anyone else can re-
move his own substance, for it is of
the very texture of the thought itself.
A sentence should not exceed the normal
length which enables it to be carried safely
on a single breath of the speaker. A para-
graph not always be a fair test of an
author’s quality; but, as a rule, the writer
can add grace to force in its compass, and
display himself at his best. These being
granted, and the maxim de gustibus being
remembered, it appears from the competitive
paragraphs that Marcus Clarke is the writer
of the best prose of Australia. Of the many
paragraphs taken from his novels and minor
writings, this is selected as the best: —
In the great crisis of our life, when, brought
face to face with annihilation, we are sus-
pended gasping over the great emptiness of
death, we become conscious that the self
whiebi we thought we knew so well has
stwwnge and unthought-of capacities. To
describe a tempest of the elements is not
easy, but to describe a tempest of the soul
s impossible. Amid the fury of such a tem-
pest, a thousand memories, each bearing in
its breast the corpse of some dead deed whose
influence haunts us yet, are driven like
feathers before the blast, as unsubstantial
ind unregarded. The mists which shroud our
self-knowledge become transparent, and we
are smitten with sudden, lightning-like com-
prehension of our own misused power over
our fate
Marcus Clarke —For the 'Term of His
Natural Life.
The prize, therefore, goes to the sender —-
Mrs. Howe of the Brisbane Girls’ High
School.
Here follow some of the other paragraphs
which best stand printing alone:—
THE WOOING OF THE BUTTERFLY.
When the pair meet, what time the *fan-
cies of the butterflies lightly turn to thoughts
•f love, he swoops impetuously towards her
and rises in a graceful curve, seeming to
enchant her with the display of his 'colors.
She forthwith amends her staid behaviour,
and begins a quivering, fluttering flight, ris-
ing and falling with gentle and rhythmical
grace. He, hovering near with rapid wing
movements, harmoniously responds to her
undulations. Still maintaining her coy con-
tours, she floats away over the tree tops, or
descends among the ferns and bushes, past
the burnished blue berries of the native gin-
ger, while with quaint courtliness he pays
his compliments and bewilders by his
audacity. As the amorous dalliance pro-
ceeds, he flits in brilliant spirals round and
before her, and again resumes his tremulous
flight, consonant with her emotional flutter-
ittgs.
C. It. Banfield— Confessions of •
Beachcomber.
Would it have been the Soul of Man and
none higher that gave spoken and written
word to the noblest precepts of human na-
ture? For the deeper you sound it the more
noble it seems, in spite of all the wrong, in-
justice; sin, sorrow, pain, religion, atheism
and cynics in the world. We make laws
for the protection of Society—or Property,
or Beligion, or what you will—and we pay
thousands of men like ourselves to protect
those laws and see them carried out, and we
build and maintain expensive offices, police-
court-houses and gaols, for the pro-
tecting and carrying out of those laws, and
the punishing of men-—like ourselves—-who
break them. Yet, in our heart of hearts,
we are antagonistic to most of the laws, and
the Law as a whole, which we regard as an
Asa, and to the police magistrates, and the
judges. And we hate lawyers and loathe
spies, pimps and informers of all descrip-
tions, and the hangman with all our soul.
For the Soul of Man says, Thou shalt not
refuse refuge to the outcast and thoy shalt
not betray the wanderer.
And those who do it we make outcast.
Henry Lawson —"Wanted by the Police.”
If ever men fought giants in the Olden
Times, men still fight giants to-day. If
miracles came to pass, they come to pass
still. If there was adventure in the old
days, if brave deeds were done, if kind and
courtly actions, noble thoughts, gentle w r ords
were common, they are still so. We are too
close up to our own lives to see all that is
in them. If you look at a far-off range of blue
mountains, you will see their whole shape
and form in a way that is impossible when
you are close beneath them. There is still
splendid adventure in this world of ours;
there is still room for the old brave deeds,
the old kindness, courtesy, high thinking,
gentleness. There is not much change,
when you come to think about it. Men and
things, day and night, summer and winter,
are as they were a thousand years ago. We
live differently in details; but we live only
while we breathe, as our fathers lived, and
we die as they died, when we can no louger
breathe. We are just the same, bones and
blood, ‘body and soul. If we are changed,
only the good God knows it.
J. H. Abbott —William Dumpier.
Faster and fiercer now fly the sun-shafts,
and heavier is the fall of the dissolving glory
of the'jewel time. A quiver along the tree-
tops. rather as of something departing than
coming, and a momentary collapse of all
keener delight. The radiant glory of that
day is past. Few such days in any year of
any life. And it has been sadly illusive.
The deepest soul inspirations have taken in
but driblets of the full volume.
Francis Myers—“A Bush Symphony” in
The Lone Hand.
The Fizzer was due at sundowm, and for
the Fizzer to be due meant that the Fizzer
would arrive. The Fizzer is unlike every
other type of man excepting a bush mail-
man. Hgrd, sinewy, dauntless and endur-
ing, he travels day after day,, and month
after mouth, practically alone —"on me Pat
Malone,” he calls it—with or without a
black boy, according to circumstances, and,
five trips out of his yearly eight throwing
dice with death along his dry stages, and vet
at all times as merry as a grig and as cliir-
ruppy as a young grasshopper. With a
light-hearted “So-long, chaps!” he sets out
from Katherine on his thousand-mile ride,
and with a cheery, “What ho, chaps. Here
we are again!” rides in within five weeks
with that journey behind him.
Mrs. Gunn--—ll'e of the Never-Never.
The Australian autumn plays an original'
duet upon our moods, wherein, while Nature
pipes her full, joyous soprano, ancestral
Memory, reading from the score of Old
World literature, Weaves a sad contralto of
minor chords. Right out in the bush,
autumn is only the spring returned, but
arbund the busy haunts of men she wears
the northern garlands of the dying year.
The bronzing creeper of Virginia flashes here
and there a gold and crimson leaf, premoni-
tory of its scarlet flare for the year’s sunset.
In every orchard the trees, with yellow, ban-
ners, are waving a brave adieu to the scenes
•f_ their beneficent year’s toil, straight-
ening their unburdened limbs for the winter's
repose. The last of the apples and the great
golden quinces gleam out a reminder that
the melancholy of the northern autumn is
for the season of plenty past. But with us
autumn’s imported pageantry of woe can
arouse no feeling more personal than an
.•esthetic shade of sorrow, “sad as the last
line of a brave romance.” For how can it,
when the plenteous autumn rains have
wakened again the music of water along all
our thirsty creeks, when the ploughman is
out with his team the whole day long to get
the. moist earth delved, and the soft light of
their dumb pleasures shines in the eyes of
the cattle as they nibble once again the
sweet siiceulence of the velvet herbage?
The Age (Melb.), 19/4/’O3.
Amongst the other passages received were
extracts from Kingsley’s Geoffrey Hamlyn,
novels by Ada Cambridge, Rolf Boldrewood,
Louis Becke, A. Dorrington, and stories in
The Bulletin Story Book and recent Bulle-
tins —most of which depended upon their
context. Also extracts from Barton’s His-
*orV °/ N.8.W., a speech by Parkes, Rev. J.
J. Malone’s article on Gordon in Austral
Light , “The Wattle River” in Heart of the
Rose,” Amy Mack’s Bird Calendar, Bean’s
On the Wool Track, and a number of news-
paper articles. Nearly all of these were too
long. One enthusiast sent the whole “Death
of Judy” scene from Ethel Turner’s Family
at Misrule.
A few writers of fine quality were neg-
lected by the extractors. Still, sufficient has
been done to show that although verse has so
far attracted the best of our imaginative
writers, and for a while the prose of
Marcus Clarke stood alone, there are now a
good many writers of prose that is well-
balanced, clear and sometimes beautiful.
Plays for an Operating Theatre
Eugene Brieux, member of the French
Academy, is not well known in English-
speaking countries, as his plays are too un-
pleasant for their theatres. Brieux is a re-
former. He uses the drama as a sword to
slash at abuses. Since 1890 he has produced
15 plays—one exposes the evils of employ-
ing provincial wet-nurses for Parisian chil-
dren, another is an arraingment of divorce;
one attacks the French judicial system, an-
other explains the futility of organised char-
ity—and so on. Some of the later plays are
merely pathological discussions. They don’t
mind that sort of thing in France, hut in
Britain plain-speaking on social diseases is
barred. Mrs. Grundy reigns, and has decreed
that in drama or fiction Society’s drainage
system niust not be mentioned. Accordingly
the British Censor of Plays refused permis-
sion for the performance of Brieux’s plays.
Thereupon Bernard Shaw, also a writer of
“unpleasant” plays, waxed indignant, and
vowed that he would have some of Brieux’s
plays translated and published, with the in-
dispensable preface by himself.
G.B.S. has made good, A. C. Fifield
(London) sends the volume —Three Plays
hy Brieux, with a Preface by Shaw. It is a
book that will startle the average reader,
and probably do him good. This French-
man Talks at him relentlessly about his cant,
his immorality, his diseases, and other things
the Comfortable man prefers to ignore.
The three plays translated for this vol-
ume are Damaged Goods. The Three Daugh-
ters of .1/. Du gout, and two versions of
Maternity. The last is a counterblast to
those who have shouted at the poor to multi-
ply and increase the supply of food for
powder.
Dr. Hourtin : I only ask that the poor
should have few children and the degenerate
none. No child ought to be brought into
the world handicapped by illness or want.
Brignae: And as the result of your pre-
cautions our country would fall in point of
population to a fifth or a tenth-rate Power.
Hourtin: History teaches us that not even
military supremacy belongs to the largest
nations. M. de Marigny’s reflection, not
mine. . . .And when you have a moment just
consider how Society behaves to the mothers
of whom it demands children. You’ll find
that an entertaining subject, unless it makes
you cry.
Damaged Goods is a lecture on syphilis in
relation to marriage, spoken through images
with phonographs inside.
There may come a time when people will
go to the theatre for instruction as the stu-
dent goes to the university, when society’s
diseased members and institutions will be
placed on.the operating-table, and the cancer-
ous growths exhibited by the dramatic sur-
geon. Brieux will then be out of date, but
honored as a pioneer of clinical drama. If
such a time should' come, it will not last
long, as the reaction in favor of “legs
and tomfoolery” would begin quickly.
Shaw’s preface to these plays contains
wilder assertions than usual. He proclaims
Brieux as “the greatest writer France has
produced since Molibre.” The majority of
the French people, he says, are not only in-
capable of fine art, but resent it furiously.
What they like to read is the police intelli-
gence, especially the murder cases and di-
vorce cases. The invented murders and di-
vorces of the novelists and playwrights do
not satisfy them because they cannot believe
in them. To produce this belief the writer
must disguise and even discard the arts of the
man of letters, and assume the style of the
descriptive reporter of the criminal courts.
As an example of how to cater for such
readers we may take Zola’s Bete JHuniaine.
It is in all its essentials a simple and touch-
ing story like Provost’s Manon Lescaut.
But into it Zola has violently thrust the
greatest police sensation of the nineteenth
century, the episode of Jack the Ripper.
To such readers, he says, the formula of
tragedy had Come down from ancient times
when, in order to make life seem tragic,
something dreadful had to happen, and some-
body had to die. “The tragedy of modern
life is that nothing happens, and that. the
resultant dulness does not kill.” Brieux
threw over the traditional forced catastrophe
uncompromisingly
He was as scientific, as conscientious; as
unflinching as Zola, without being in the
least morbid. He was no more dependent
upon horrors than Molihre,. and as sane in
his temper.
He may be all that, but in faithfully por-
traying “the resultant dulness” he has failed
to make drama. Magnificent as a muck-
raker, but no artist.
The name of Molihre suggests barbed
satire that wounded Evil and rankled there,
laughter that blew pretentiousness out of
existence, bright, witty dialogue and dramatic
action. Brieux, brought into momentary
comparison by this book, suggests only dul-
ness and ugliness.
This book emphasises the remark of J. M.
Synge in his preface to The Playboy of the
Western World : “on the stage one must have
reality, and one must have joy, and that is
why the modern intellectual drama has
failed.”

This is the day of the cheap book. That
“Home University Library” referred to here
a few weeks ago is selling like fiction ; and
the first volumes have arrived of a new series
of shilling books issued by Methuen and
Go. Stanley Weyman’s tinder the Red
Robe needs no advertisement; it is now in
its 23rd edition. Some volumes to come
are Wilde’s De Profundis and Selected
Poems, G. Balfour’s Life of Stevenson, and
an excellent view of The Condition of Eng-
land, by C, F. Masterman.
Sunny Australia
Archibald Marshall, who visited Australia
in 1000 as special commissioner of the London
Daily Mail, has expanded his newspaper art-
icles and given them a little more permanent
form as a book — Sunny Australia (Dym-
ocK’s).
It does not pretend to be more than a
journalist’s' impressions and recollections of
this country, but it would have been a better
book if Marshall had arranged his material
in some logical order, and cut out the repe-
titions. lie says on p. 211: “I have written
nothing yet of the hospitality of Australian
clubs,” although the reader has met on
p. 9 and elsewhere with glowing eulogies of
Australian clubs. The chibs treated him so
well, in fact, that he saw Australia through
a rosy mist created by dinner parties, motor
picnics, and other entertainments. Every-
thing in Australia is lovely:—
Australians are commonly reported to
possess some faults to balance their obvious
virtues, but I can unashamedly confess my-
self blind to them. After my experience I
can see only the virtues.
Australians have a positive genius for
hospitality. 1 have never met with anything
like it in the world,
He is more than generous in returning
thanks. Besides the numerous individual
hosts who are mentioned, the waitresses, the
squatters, the children, the women, the in-
dustries, the roads anu the Bush all receive
lavish praise.
It is roses, roses all the way with only one
or two thorns of criticism. The Bulletin.
it seems, offended the dear man because of
its dislike of ceremonial and “honors from
the crown.” Alas, it will take a procession
of Carlyles to/clear the British mind of cant
about such things, and fragments of the Bri-
tish atmosphere still hang about the clubs
from which Mr. Marshall viewed Australia.
He is inclined tq think that we are still in
the grossly material stage, and are not cul-
tivating the finer feelings. Although “the
fact that man does not live by bread alone
has taken no great hold of the imaginations
of Australians,” we are fond of poetry “of a
sort,” and he mentions the huge popularity
bf Gordon and Patersoii.
Superior. I think, in quality is the work
of Mr. Henry Lawson, who writes both
verse and prose, and will, unless I am much
mistaken, he accounted the greatest of all
the Australian writers up to the present
time. -> \.
If Sunny Australia is read on the Other
Side its favorable account of our industries
may induce intending emigrants to come this
way.
Songs from m Hammock , by Dunstan
Shaw (Penfold and Co., Sydney), are too
slight to deserve publication in book form.
The idea in “To a Wattle Tree” is good-
Yesterday
A nymph in ancient loveliness
I came across —-but you would say
A wattle tree! ,
but clumsily handled, and squashes itself in
the last lines: — ' . . <
Oh, be a Greek! Oh, be a Greek!
The gods are living! lam sure
Within, our purple bush they seek
Tlieir ancient empery restore.
An,airy trifle or two suggest possibilities ;
this, for instance: —
A moon came out of a mist
As they kissed,
And the moon was new.
They kissed, and whispered a boon;
And the moon
In a mist withdrew.
Dunstan Shaw should now try Songs from
a Deal Box, or something firmer than s a
hammock.

“A.V.” (Q.) suggests that Australian
teachers should include Australian books
among their prizes to school children, and
mentions these':—
Banfield : Confessions of a Beachcomber.
Le Souef.: Wild Life in Australia.
Bean: On the Wool Track.
They are suitable for boys in senior classes.
In N.S.W. schools many of the teachers
present as prizes for girls
Mrs. Gunn: We of the Never-Never.
Amy Mack: Bushland Stories.
Emerson : Santa Claus and the Sun-Dial
and stories by Ethel Turner, Lilian Turner,
Mary Grant Bruce and Winifred James.
The only Australian books for boys that have
been used are those of Alex. Macdonald.

“S.T.S.” : Since the man who pretended to
know Sydney asked, “Who keeps the pub
there now?’’ nobody has come along with
such a quaint idea of it as one Andrew
Soutar shows in a new novel, The Island of
Test. His hero landed from an English
steamer on the “landing-stage,” where friends
awaited him with horses, and they started
straight away on “a 30-mile ride, over pretty
stiff country,” to Dingo Flatts. The genial
host told the new-chum never to argue with
any station hand. “Hit hard, on the chin
for preference. We are pretty close to the
primitive out here.” (And the story is quite
modern.)

and send for this medicine.
The following is a letter received from Air. fohn L. England, iBj a Cuba
Street, Wellington, New Zealand, and though the subiect of it lives far away
it is none the less the true story of a great physical resurrection from the greatest
form of ill-health to one of splendid strength. All sick men or women should
read this testimony, and benefit by the reading :
Clements Tonic Ltd.
“I feel it my duty to mankind in general to let you publish this, what I consider
as a remarkable cure. Three years ago I was badly crushed in an accident on the
wharf in Wellington, and I never earned or was fit to earn a week’s wages for a
period of 16 months. After that time I commenced to earn a little, but my life was
a complete misery. Eat I could not, nor sleep, and I suffered awful pain; I would
double up and could not straighten my back. At last I wasted away over 281 b. in
weight, and got that nervous that I could not follow my occupation. In the mean-
time no one would credit the amount of pills and medicine and tonic that I had taken,
some of which would give me a false start, but after a few hours I would be as bad
agai About six weeks ago I saw an advertisement in the Wellington Evening Post,
describing a case similar to mine. I procured a bottle of your Tonic, and, before I
had finished the first bottle, I felt a new man. I commenced to eat a hearty meal.
I commenced to sleep seven or eight hours at a stretch. I could feel the elastic step
and the straight shoulders coming back again, the eye that could not bear the light or
the sun, brightening again. My weight increased in four weeks from 9st. bib. to
10st 111 b and after taking 10 bottles of your wonderful tonic, I feel a new man, and
shall never be without it; not forgetting Fletcher’s Pills, which you sell with the
tonic and which I found very useful for my liver, from which I suffered great agony.
I give you full liberty to publish this testimonial to the whole world, as it is abso-
lutely true. (Signed) JOHN L. ENGLAND.”
Men who suffer from Brain-fag, Loss of Nerve, Weakness, Loss of Sleep, Indigestion,
Low Spirits, Constipation, Weak Kidneys or Sluggish Liver, and who want to get
well and feel young again, should get this medicine. It Is as good for women,
especially for Anaemia or Broken Rest, Biliousness, Sick Headache, Melancholia, or
Constipation. ALL CHEMISTS and STORES sell it everywhere.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He "Went to Earth.
“My soul goes Heavenwards”: he spoke
Poised on the brink of Death’s dark firth.
Swift from its slieli nis spirit broite
And found, not Heaven, but the Earth —•
A cave more deep than furthest delved
And litten by some hidden light,
Streaming from where the granite shelved
To deeps beyond the reach of sight:
He saw the molten riverfall
Of gold, poured down in flaming rains,
And, ebbing through the milky wall,
The shimmer of the opal-veins;
Heard the quicksilver-ripple cold
Sob at his feet, and far apart
The slumber-belling pulse that told
The murmur of the planet’s heart.
He dreamed of Paradise.. .and here?
Lo, in this iron hull profound
The great Earth, mistress of the sphere,
Awaited Kim... and spoke no sound.
And gazing on that face he saw
Dark strength, far deeper than surmise:
There was no creed of god or law
Could quell those planetary eyes.
Silent, she led him through the deep
Across the gulfs of fire and mist,
Where the flame-dragons of the steep
Sprang at her cheek as gods who kissed ;
Until they came ’neath human hives,
And saw the great roots striking down—
Life-roots, whence sprang in growth of lives,
The teeming trouble of the town.
Then, then she tossed her mantle free —
And bared her head, and spoke in scorn:
“From tiniest creatures of the sea
My children, those 1 love, were born.
* : .> v
“I brooded o’er them, tender, mute:
I led their minds through Twilight-land,
On, till they sundered from the Brute —
The beings who could Understand:
“Yea, have I toiled to make them speed
Long, since the journey from the sea;
And I have squandered on their need
The patience and the heart of me.
“But you -—you spurned me and my bests:
I spread the vines for you in vain:
Sweet women caught you to their breast:*—
You tossed my bounty back again;
“You scorned the Earth ; you strove, and built
Sick fancies, worlds of saints and wings,
And banned with the remorse of guilt
The sweet sane health of primal things.
“Here, where the roots of life press round,
Give answer; your own path you trod ;
Further in Life should soul be bound
That casts out Beauty... for a god ?
“How, granting Life, could Life condone?
No bud of mine, no leaf, no tree
Craves your marred spirit for its own,
For now you have no part in me.
“For now you have no part in me ... ”
The fading echo died, and through
That silence of eternity
The moments dripped like silver dew.
The soul hung hovering as at death,
A flame that flickered to eclipse;
Then vanished at the stroke of breath,
Like a wan taper, of Her lips.
N.S.W.
HUGH McKAY.
Leaving Time.
When you’ve left a ship at Sydney with your pockets
full of “tin,”
And with men of doubtful kidney you have done
your “good stuff” in;
When you’ve been abused and chided ’cause your
“rent” is overdue,
And you’ve just about decided that the shore’s ho
place for you—
Then it’s time to get a hustle on you, sonny!
'J ime to look around for work amongst the ships !
When the pubs and boarding-master have your
money,
Say good-bye and kiss your sweetheart on the lips!
When you’ve dallied oft with Daisy and you’ve spent
some time with Sue,
Till this one thought drives you crazy: if your best
girl only knew!
And you know that when you greet her, you must
make things smooth with lies,
And you feel ashamed to meet her, and you fear her
trustful eyes—
Then it’s time to get a move on, do not tarry!
Time to make a few more long deep-water trips!
Empty pockets don’t encourage blokes to marry,
Say good-bye and kiss your sweetheart on the lips!
When you get a fit of brooding and you haven’t
much to say,
And you feel that you’re intruding in the hash-house
where you stay,
And you hatch out fine romances in a morbid re-
verie,
And you talk of vanished chances and the things
that ought to be —-
Then it’s time to pack your bag and do a hurry!
Time to think no more of long three-finger nips!
You must leave behind your ham-and-eggs and curry,
Say good-bye, and kiss your sweetheart on the lips!
When you’ve done a bit o’ graftin’ and you’ve saved
a little pile,.
And the murmur of the shaftin’ has been with you
all the while, <
And you have a sort o’ notion that you’d like to
“rest a bit,”
And you hate the name of Ocean and the things that
go with it—
Then it s time to hustle down the gangway, sonny 1
To the girl who loves you to her finger-tips!
Time to think of how you’re going to spend your
money!
Say Good-day!” and kiss your sweetheart on
the lips!
VICTORIA.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. ‘‘The Editor, The Bulletin," is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

fused.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-

form of Political, Social or other Artieles, Verse,

The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, JUNE 22, 1911.
THE WANE OF THE ECCLESIASTIC.
On the date borne by this issue of The
‘Bulletin, George V. is due to be crowned
in London. The Coronation has no practi-
cal significance. George was as much King
and Emperor the moment his father died as
he ever will be. It is a religious pageant,
meaningless to the general public, though it
has a certain historical interest for those
who appreciate its symbolism. The Corona-
tion provides, among other things, one of
the infrequent red-letter days that the high
ecclesiastic ever enjoys these times. For a
brief though glorious space the Archbishop
of Canterbury becomes the most prominent
member of the realm. The very King bows
in humility before him. It is his strange
task to inquire of the audience in Westmin-
ster Abbey whether “they who have come
to do their homage are willing to do the
same.” He has the privilege of anointing
the Head, the Breast, and the Palms of the'
Sovereign with Holy Oil. Having waved
aside the Ampulla and the Spoon, he clothes
the King with the sacred vestments, and
hands him the Sword, the Orb and the
Sceptre, administers the Oath, and puts the
Crown on the Royal head —all amid a wealth
and riot of ceremonial. It is a crowded
hour for the Archbishop and his henchmen.
But it passes; and then the prelates must
shed their gorgeous old-world clothing, and
get back to the drab garb of everyday life,
and stay out of the public eye, perhaps for
years to come.
What a tragic commentary it is on the
decline of the religious caste! The cleric
who was once all powerful is in no demand,
politically, nowadays, unless it is to be a
figurehead at Coronations, Jubilees, and so
forth. The modern prototypes of the men
at whose words nations rose and fell rarely
get. into the national limelight, and when
they do the joy is a fleeting one, and pain-
fully suggestive of the barren glory that
accrues to the “super” Duke in a costume
drama. The Archbishop of self-respect and
pride who looks back on the ancient glories
of his order, and then considers its present
status, must feel much of the bitterness of a
once-beautiful woman reviewing her lost
youth. In the dawn of civilisation the wis-
dom, knowledge- and power of the com-
munity were centred in the priests. They
were the directors of labor, the doctors, the
writers and artists, the teachers, the generals
and politicians. Since they monopolised the
great offices of State it was to the priest-
hood that the best brains of the nation gravi-
tated. Egypt, before the Bedouin inva-
sion, was a benevolent despotism controlled
entirely by priests. Thenceforward the
world saw in Moses, Mahomet, Augus-
tine, Wolsey, Cromwell, Richelieu,
Penn, and a thousand others, men who were
great religious leaders and, in addition, sol-
diers, statesmen and reformers of the highest
order.
■* * * *
The supply appears to have ceased. It
used to be said of W. E. Gladstone, by his
least embittered political opponents, that he
was hopeless as a statesman, but would have
made a most satisfactory Archbishop. This
was not intended as a slight on Archbishops.
Yet it amounted to something like tliat. It
signified tnat Gladstone, though an elo-
quent speaker, a scholar and an impressive
personality, entirely lacked that constructive
ability and that stark, bold resolution which
make for success in the world. The con-
trast between the religious leaders’ authority
in the present century, and that of many of
their predecessors, is astounding. In com-
paratively recent times, as history goes, to
defy the heads of the English Church con-
stituted a deed of unthinkable audacity. One
can almost see the historians shudder as they
tell the dreary story of mediseval countries
groaning under the displeasure of their
spiritual leaders. In those black days chil-
dren went unbaptised, the dead lay unburied
in the streets, and the people wandered
about in mourning, praying that the insuf-
ferable burden might be lifted from their
shoulders. That was an epoch when
Bishops made and unmade Kings, and when
every hedge-priest carried a Prime Minis-
ter’s portfolio in his cassock. No longer
does the world evolve clerics like that Bishop
of Ptolemais, who was not only an author
and diplomat of note, but who, on his native
town being invaded, raised an army and
drove the enemy out at the point of the
sword ; or like Laud ; or even like Talley-
kand, who, a bare century ago, was a
political power in a country that was ruled
by the most virile of sovereigns.
The twentieth century ecclesiastic has
about as much influence on the big questions
of the day as an infant. Indeed, he hardly
concerns himself with them. The most agi-
tating problems that ever beset him are
how, with the minimum of unpleasantness
and scandal, to induce some stiff-necked
curate to desist from genuflecting or using
incense in his church. His intellect is not
exercised with imperial expansion, or the
exploration of foreign lands, or the machina-
tions of foreign chancelleries, but with mat-
ters like the revision of the Prayer Book
and the raising of funds for a new taber-
nacle or Sabbath-school. If he goes to war
he does not march in the van, as of yore, but
shoulder to shoulder beside the newspaper
correspondents and other non-combatants.
The ecclesiastical fighters who affect uni-
forms and military titles have the latter
printed between inverted commas by a scorn-
ful press; their active service is waged on
metropolitan streets under the protection of
the police ; the enemy is the Devil, who. as
years of experience have taught, never hits
back.
It is not the fault of the age that the
cleric is feeble and ineffective. The oppor-
tunities for distinguished service are as rife
as ever they were. The trouble is there are
so few who are equipped to grasp them. In
Australia there are scores of social evils
calling for extirpation, some of them as
grim as those of the Middle Passage itself.
The men who killed tfje slave trade in
blacks could kill the whife slane business in
short order if they were alive to-day. But
in place of a WilberEoece our Noncon-
formist Churches are most conspicuous for
their weird jests, like Wore all of Victoria,
and Dill Macky of Sydney. The social
and political life of the country could well
do with some brainy religious leaders—men
after the fashion of those who have done so
much to make France and England great.
But the bulk of the ecclesiastical politicians
in sight possess an ill-veiled impatience with
the aspirations of the “lower orders,” and
their conception of statesmanship consists
mostly in frothy talk about the Flag and
the Empire, and kindred topics. When W.
Judkins, of Melbourne, complacently sub-
mitted to be crowned with ti-tree leaves in
a patch of suburban scrub the other day,
what time the company at a Pleasant Sab-
bath Picnic acclaimed him as a Saviour of
the Race, he unconsciously typified the con-
dition to which the whole caste has de-
scended. Allowing for a few honorable ex-
ceptions it is not taken seriously. Its
members can no longer punish and humble
those who contemn them, and the thought
fills them with petulance. Many have not
even the sense to keep their exasperation
with the changed state of things to them-
selves. Thus, one who was greeted with
the cry, “Hullo, Wowser,” as he sat in a
Sydney tram recently, wrote a long letter of
protest on the subject, to a daily-paper edi-
tor —a total stranger, completely uninter-
ested in his troubles. But whether the
cleric is a diverting fool or a person of in-
telligence, or a knave, or a good and kindly
soul, he is, practically always and every-
where, quite futile. When one recalls the
tremendous part he used to play, and con-
siders the paltry one he plays now, and when
one thinks of all the useful things he might
be doing if he had the time to spare from
wholly negligible practices, the symbolical
appropriateness of his share in the Corona-
tion ceremony becomes apparent. It stresses
the fact that the great religious leaders are
shut away from the practical side of life’s
struggle for ever. The race of clerical
giants has died out.
THE GEEBUNG CROWD’S FIGHT IN
A NEW DITCH.
The further the case proceeds against the
N.S.W. Government’s proposal to resume
possession of the State’s Government House
and park, the thinner it appears. The Op-
position relies mainly on the preposterous
argument that the suggestion is a further ex-
ample of Labor’s “vandalistic tendencies,”
and amounts to a deliberate plot to “destroy
a great historical asset.” As a matter of
fact, the Government proposal is the exact
reverse of all that. There is no intention to
demolish Government House. But a his-
torical asset which is permanently locked
away in a cellar is of little value to the pub-
lic. And that has been the position of this
Government House for years and years. At
the best, the line building, leased for some
time past as an occasional residence of the
Gov.-iieneral, is only available to stimulate
the imagination of an infinitesimal section of
the people during a fraction of the year. The
vast majority of citizens who find the money
for its upkeep have never seen more of it
than the chimney-pots, and, while it is put
to its present use, have no hope on earth of
ever doing so. Now, it is an axiom with
Governments all over the civilised world
that the public shall not be excluded from
State buildings which are not put to a
reasonable amount of use. That is why the
Sydney Tory, when he goes to London, is
able to wander at will about the Tower,
1 lampton Court, and such-like places; and
it is because he appreciates the justice of
that policy, so far as foreign nations are
concerned, that he does not howl “Vandal-
ism” when he finds that the Paris Louvre
and the Luxembourg, the Palace at Versail-
les, the Doge’s Palace in Venice, and count-
less other places of the sort, are open to any
respectable citizen. A rational nation in
every case of this kind puts to itself tbe
plain business question: Is it worth while
keeping this place shut? The proposition be-
fore N.S.W. may be as simply stated: Is it
better that the State should have a Gover-
nor-General —who is rarely in residence, any-
how—monopolising its greatest historical
building and its best harbor site, or that it
should house him elsewhere and put that
building and site to some constant nublic
use? The common-sense of the community
should not be long in arriving at an answer.
The use to which the building should be put
stands out as obviously as a burning moun-
tain. N.S.W. already has a couple of ade-
quate National Libraries ; and there is the Na-
tional Gallery. But the State still lacks a
National Portrait Gallery and a museum de-
voted exclusively to Australian historical
relics and mementoes. It is impossible to
over-estimate the educational value of insti-
tutions like the Musee de Carnavalet in
Paris. Here may be seen those things
which speak to the Frenchman of all creeds
and every shade of political thought of his
country’s struggles, triumphs and sorrows.
It is a sort of working-man’s university, and
a place of endless entertainment as well, and
the crowds which flock there on fete days
afford a revelation in the matter of intelli-
gent interest. London’s National Portrait
Gallery, which stands close to and is, in a
sense, the complement of the National Gal-
lery, is a worthy model for this country to
follow. There is a natural affinity between
a National Portrait Gallery and a National
Museum of Antiquities. They should be in
conjunction, and there is no place more suit-
able to hold the nucleus of what must one
day be a collection of supreme Australian in-
terest than the edifice which has so far only
held Governors.
* * # *
There remains the curious complaint that
Sydney’s “prestige” will be affected in some
unspecified fashion, if this Government House
is put to any other than its present use. In
the eyes of the title-chasing type of Aus-
tralian and the remittance-man type of Bri-
ton, this may be so. But these folk do not
count. The world does not measure a city’s
importance by the people of rank who visit
it, else would Aix and Monte Carlo be held
of more consequence than Birmingham and
New York. Far from sacrificing prestige,
Sydney, by acting intelligently in this mat-
ter, has an opportunity to add to that asset,
in a very material way. It can never be-the
administrative centre of the Commonwealth.
Geokge Reid saw to that 10 years ago.
Neither can it ever hope to be the 6ne com-
mercial centre of Australia. But, with its
Mitchell Library holding as it does a col-
lection of old Australian records which can
never be duplicated, and with such a Portrait
Gallery and Museum as are here suggested,
it can and should be the Art centre, and
the place to which every student of Aus-
tralian historical matters would need to re-
sort. And there is the consideration that
with Government House open to the people,
and its grounds transformed into a public
reserve, Sydney will have between St. Mary’s
Cathedral and the harbor about as superb a
combination of glorious park and decorative
public buildings as exists in the world ; and
these are factors which operate more chan
the presence of many officials to give a city
prestige. It is not strange that Sydney’s
small Geebung malcontents should be enraged
at the Holman proposal. They always do
fight bitterly against any scheme which is
touched with imagination, and which looks
ahead further than the middle of the coming
week. But here it is to their own interests
as well as that of the general public that
they should not prevail. By blocking the
Government’s present proposal, they will do
their native city—the city which they have
always considered before either N.S.W. or
Australia —the same kind of bad turn that
they tried to do it when they struggled to
defeat Federation a decade ago.
THE CURSE OF BEING TOO DIFFIDENT.
Every time the mysterious meeting of
potentates, called the Imperial Conference,
fills a small corner of London with speeches
and food, the truth is shown afresh that
the Australian has been too sinfully modest
to recognise the value of his own plans
and proposals. The military and naval
schemes towards w r hich Australia yearned
furnished a surprising example of that.
Every Australian had the firm conviction
that, in order to defend itself on land,
the Commonwealth ought to train all its
youth to arms. But people in Britain, and
people imported from Britain, and people
who aped Britain, cried out with vague and
idiotic horror against the Australian no-
tion; and with his usual apologetic depre-
ciation of himself the Australian drew back.
For many years he scrambled along with a
wretchedly exiguous militia force. Then
Joseph Cook and Alfred Deakin, in
tremulous and fear-stricken hesitation, es-
tablished a sort of soldiers’ crbche by train-
ing youths 19 years old ; but, being afraid
of what they had done, they asked Her-
bert Kitchener, a man who was supposed
to know something about army organisa-
tion, to give them his valuable British
opinion. His verdict was that the Austra-
lian plan had been the right plan all along,
and that the Deakin-Cook policy had
merely fiddled with Australia’s military
necessities. Then, and only then, did the
self-effacing Australian get courage to go
ahead with his own ideas. The ultimate
result will probably he that Britain itself
will follow Australia’s example, and that the
disastrous Territorial Army will be aban-
doned, in spite of the fact that S’ John
Fuller, Victoria’s imported Governor, is a
captain in it.
For many years, moreover, Australians
saw that they could not be safe without
some navy of their own. They perceived
that the alleged “ocean supremacy” of Brit-
ain was a mere fiction in the Pacific, in
which sea Australia’s likeliest enemies are
to be found. They knew that the Marin-
ers of England would inevitably be called
back to guard their native seas, and that
Australia and the Pacific would be left to
look after themselves, with nothing but the
Japanese Alliance as a pretence of security.
This view also was gravely reprobated by
the imported pundits and their imitators;
so that the Australian, distrusting him-
self in his customary boyish fashion, de-
layed for 10 years the first instalment of
his plans. Again the event has proved
that the Australian was right, and that
the critics who gnashed their teeth were
wrong. To-day Britain is in one of the
bluest funks of which her history holds a
record. She wakes up in the night after
dreaming that her throat' has been cut by a
German Uhlan ; and she never retires with-
out looking under the;bed to make sure that
Kaiser Wilhelm is not there. This august
Mistress of the Seas, who once told Aus-
tralians that any fleet which she might
raise would be Useless, if not an encum-
brance, has now actually asked Australia
to build a fleet in order to help in the
maintenance of ocean power; and during
the present Conference she will probably
be found asking, either publicly or secretly,
for a good deal more. The Australian was
right, as usual; and nothing but his sinful
modesty stood in his way.
* * * *
There are other beliefs to which the Aus-
ralian is clinging, in spite of his tendency
to acquiesce when other people tell him that
he is. mistaken. One of them is, that a
maritime Power is in a bad way when it
depends upon Dagoes, other foreigners, and
niggers for the manning of its ships; and
so far as his powers go, he is trying to
keep clean white vessels in his own clean
white country. The Australian, too, can-
not fall in love with the piebald glories of
an empire which is mostly nigger, so that
King George, when he is’ crowned, might
fittingly cork his face like the gentleman
who sits at the corner of the stage in the
Tivoli. Alliance and co-operation between
white British races appeal to the Austra-
lian ; but he sees only one disaster for an em-
pire which is proudest of India among
all its members, and which shakes hands
with the Jap, because the Jap. promised to
keep its gun pointed towards India. Time
will undoubtedly show that the Australian
is right ; and, meantime, the best that he
can do is to get rid of his blushes, as far as
possible, and insist upon his own ideas to
the farthest limits of his opportunities.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Site for Government House.
Various small State insects are buzzing
furiously with regard to the housing of the
Governor-General, having been stirred up by
the Sydney proposal to turn over the Har-
bor-side residence and park to the people
who own it. Among others, Willie Watt,
of Victoria, lays it down that wherever the
G.-G. goes on “official business” there ought
he to have a Government House to accom-
modate him and his staff of lady and gentle-
man helps. Nobody has yet explained what
this “official business” is that takes the G.-G.
all over the continent. No doubt his Ex.
may want to go to Melbourne at Cup time,
and to Sydney dux-ing thg Easter frivols;
also he may care to roost in the hills outside
Adelaide during part of the summer, and in
winter he may care to go north to Townsville
to find some warmth. But if he does these
things he does them partly for his own pleas-
ure and partly because “Society” expects him
to buy food and drink and entertainment for
the people who are the best able to buy it for
themselves. The Commonwealth pays the
G.-G. to do certain fairly well-defined' things
—and the entertainment of a mere handful
of the population isn’t one of those things.
His “official business” lies where his Minis-
ters are; he is the link between them and
the British Government. And it has been
decided that the Ministers shall have fheir
official quarters at Yassberra, where, more-
over, the Parliament is to meet —the Parlia-
ment which the G.-G. must open and close
according to hoary precedent. Yassberra
has been declared to be good enough to be
the capital of Australia; now, when it is
naturally insisted that the Governor-Gen-
eral of Australia shall live there, the whole
shocking conspiracy over the selection is ex-


posed by the people who are
the wildness of the proposal to bu •
ment House there. The truth, of course, is
that those who engineered the con ®P^ d J
deliberately chose a place where nobody
would want to live, and where they
pected nobody would live if he coul P ,
live in Sydney. It is, however, n° P
the Commonwealth Government s duJL
proceed on that assumption. It mus
that the Parliament was honest ; and_ its
sole obligation, the capital site having been
chosen, is to provide a Government Ho '.
at Yassberra. That must be the home of the
Governor-General —until the site is chan a .
And any Government which, having ap-
proved of the capital site, attempts to dodge
the consequences of the selection brands 1
self a hypocrite in this matter and a conspir-
ator against Australia.
The “ Horrible Understanding.”
Sydney Telegraph and Melbourne Leader
have been blunting their teeth on that tough
proposition, “the honorable understanding.”
It happened in this fashion. April, which is
a critical month for people on the land,
chanced to be a “dry” month in most parts.
Farmers and squatters began to be alarmed,
and there was talk of a drought. Immedi-
ately the price of pollard and bran was
jumped up—all over Australia —by 15s. per
ton. This audacious thing was done at a
time when wheat was actually falling in
price. The millers, however, with one eye on
the weather-glass and another on the public,
saw a chance of “making a bit” ; and, had
the dry weather continued, the consumers of
bran and pollard would have had to go on
paying through the nose for their supplies.
Fortunately, rain fell —and so did the price
of bran and pollard. The fall of the latter was
so sudden that the bang woke up the two
journals referred to, and they began to ex-
amine into the matter. The net result of
their inquiry was that, “though there is no
combine amongst the millers” within the
meaning of the Act, there is an “honorable
understanding,” which amounts to practically
the same thing. Of course, this is not new.
In Melbourne the price of flour, pollard and
bran is openly published as “Millers’ Associa-
tion rates.” and little or no secret is made
anywhere of the fact. Both the Telegraph
and the Leader wagged their heads solemn-
ly, and said that it “shouldn’t be so.” Yet
when the Fisher Government asked a few
weeks ago for power wide enough to reach
“honorable understandings,” both these mud-
dle-headed journals urged everybody to vote
“No”; and their loudest shriek was directed
to the farmer, who was told that voting
“Yes” meant ruin and general desolation.
Here, however, is a plain case of the farmer
being chewed at both ends, in a manner of
speaking, by the very beast he was told he
mustn’t kill. While the price of his wheat
was being: depressed, that of the bran and
pollard he wanted for his stock was being
raised skyward. Still, why complain/ Ap-
parently the farmer likes this sort ot treat-
ment. If he doesn’t, then The Bulletin
gives him up as a bad puzzle.

“Backers Fare Well.”
A race meeting was held at Epsom (Vic.)
the other day. Melbourne Argus remarked
next day, apropos the afternoon’s sport,
“Backers fare well.” As a matter of fact,
backers did fare well —comparatively speak-
ing That is not to say, however, that the
layers suffered any financial damage.
An analvsis of the three events in which the
public “got home” on “the Ring” is instruc-
tive in its way. In the Jumpers’ Flat Race
there were 15 starters. Six to four was the
best price quoted against the favorite. A
typical book on this event would pan out as
follows: —
£ £ £
100 60 to 40 Prelude.
99 77 „ 22 Norley.
98 84 „ 14 Quedong.
100 874 „ 124 Brownlock.
99 88* „ 11 Altar Boy.
99 90 ~ 9 Benburb.
The most the bookmaker could be asked to
pay out was thus £lOO. Then add up the
third column of figures, representing the
money collected from the public, and it will
be seen that Christian Noses held £lOB
10s. to pay £lOO. In other words, he was
assured of a certain profit of £8 10s. before
the race started; and, in addition, he had a
field of nine unbacked horses running on his
behalf for the entire contents of his bag. In
the Steeplechase, the event where anything
may happen, the favorite (which lost) went
out at 5 to 4. The financial position may be
stated as follows :
£ £ £
180 100 to 80 Paralock.
180 120 „ 60 Southport.
180 144 ~ 36 Ivy Rock.
179/17/- 163£ „ 16/7/- Waxene.
179/11/- 171 „ 8/11/- Pilot.
The book held, in this case, £2OO 18s. to
pay £lB0 —a sure profit of £2O 18s. In the
Epsom Handicap the betting was even
money Chauvelin (which lost) ; 5 to 2 the
winner, Bambra. From the Ring’s stand-
point, the Epsom Handicap worked out
thus: —
£ £ £
210 105 to 105 Chauvelin.
210 150 „ 60 Bambra.
210 168 „ 42 Gunpowder.
210 168 „ 42 Wirregah.
£249 to pay £2lO = a sure thing of £39 for
the book. And this was one of those days
when “backers fared well,” and Christian
Noses pulled a long face, and told dreary
tales of the hard shifts an honest bookmaker
is driven to in order to earn a crust!
Luckily for Noses, it is not always thus.
There are gleams in the darkness. For
example, at the big meeting at Randwick
last April, 28 horses went out for the A.J.C.
Cup. The winner was a 60 to one chance.
Broadsword, the winner of the Doncaster,
started at 12 to 1. Of the 24 races that were
run, 16 were won by the rankest outsiders,
and the meeting closed with the victory of
Reg, a 100 to one chance. These were
days on which backers admittedly did not
fare well. In order to get a line as to how
the books fared, backers and other asses
only need to consider the tables supplied
above (indicative of “the Ring’s” position
when it is “hit”), and then draw them-
selves aside and do some quiet, heart-to-
heart brooding. If the result doesn’t impel
them either to swear off racecourse betting
in N.S.W. and Vic., or, in default, to howl
at their legislators for immediate tote legis-
lation, they deserve the merciless financial
skinning that has been their lot in the past.
Overboard Goes the Competitive System!
Certain Melbourne men of affairs have
lately been observing the common or side-
gate variety of “Milk-o,” how he starts out
at 4 a.m., rushes his vehicle up and down
20 or 40 streets, delivers half a cupful of
milk at one or two houses in each, chases
stray customers, new tenants and “bad 'lays,”
and then hurries his beast home to the
stable —often miles away—and repeats the
performance in the afternoon. Having pon-
dered this business, these men reached the
conclusion that there is here a sizeable waste
of horse-flesh, horse-shoes, vehicles, cans and
man or boy. not to mention lung power,
whip and axle grease. So they are forming
a syndicate to control the whole trade;
they propose to receive the cow-juice at a
few widely-placed depots, and distribute it
from convenient sub-depots with a mini-
mum of travel, wear and tear, splash,
churning, street sweepings, and general up-
roar. The idea is so good that so long as
the syndicate doesn’t come to an “honor-
able understanding” with itself, it will be
entitled to all the profits it expects to make
—until a Greater Melbourne arrives and
undertakes the sole distribution of milk,
along with the distribution of other perish-
ables —ice, fruit and fish —to such house-
holders as care to join in an optional adden-
dum to the scheme. One of the amazing-
follies of modern civilisation is the need-
less amount of beast and boy and
cart spent in humping a city’s sup-
ply of fodder about. The average suburban
street with 300 houses will on a rough esti-
mate harbor 100 youths and men, each with
two or three customers apiece, to whom he
delivers a few drops of milk, a small loaf, or
a pound of chops, whereas three men could
do the whole thing comfortably. Nobody
would be a penny the worse off if the other
97 were paid to merely twirl their thumbs,
or play two-up, or sit at the feet of Jud-
kins, "and, if that were done, 97 horses
could be turned out to grass and 97 carts
could be turned into firewood, for which
there is a good demand at present. Cer-
tainly these 97 youths might be put to much
better use, and so might the 97 carts and
horses; and the present is a good time to
try the experiment, in view of the alleged
great scarcity of youths, and the alleged
considerable scarcity of carts and horses.
Under the Dog Act.
Randolph Bedford is annoyed:—
The Australian desires in a vague sort of way
that his country should represent the apotheosis of
liberty, where there will be no policemen, because
the Australian will have learned that for his own
copifort and safety he must not affect the comfort
and safety of others; that Australia should elim-
inate more and more the occupations which are
mere drudgery, from which the people can derive
no pleasure; that Australian cities should be perfect
places for healthful living, where the jerry builder
shall not be, nor the terrace, nor the square-
cornered room, nor the house not connected with a
municipal vacuum-cleaning system, nor the street
that harbors rat, cat, dog, Norfolk Howard, or
blatta Orientalis, which is the fancy name for cock-
roach. Above all, he will exclude the rat, the cat
and the dog, and with them most of the people who
substitute the cat and the dog for healthy interest
in a healthy infant.
The cat owes most of its popularity to the fem-
inine habit of cuddling up to people, and its soft-
ness, and its pleasure in,, warmth. When very
pleased it bursts into music of the bellows kind,
its pleasant habit of rat-catching means that it be-
comes a city of refuge for the fleas of the rat.
Everything in nature is interdependent, and it
passes on the fleas to the softness of the little child
that nurses it, or the lingerie of the lady who
likes to share her bed with a blue Persian cat called
“tortoise-shell,” because it is made of fur, green
eyes, malevolence and meat. Man’s appreciation of
the dog is more deeply rooted. In the days when
he had to pursue his meat instead of buying it from
the Meat Trust, at prices shockingly high, the dog
was useful in pursuing the game to the game’s
weariness, and pulling it down for the final blow
of the hunter’s club. The dog has a greater
capacity for affection than the cat, is more trust-
worthy and sincere, and so it is a bad habit more
difficult to be rid of. A dog occasionally saves a
child from drowning, but more frequently his
owner is fined because the brute attacked the child.
Dogs often stand guard over children in the absence
of their parents, and bite the wrong man in the
person of the curate who has called to deliver a
tract entitled, “The Reformed Bookmaker.” And
dogs have been known to be faithful to their trust
even to the exclusion of meals. But this only
proves the dog is valuable, not in civilisation, but in
unsettled conditions. The sentimentality attaching
to the dog has been fostered by such pictures as
“The Faithful Hound,” and such poems as “Gelert’s
Dying Yell.” But in Australia the faithful hound
has been known to eat his dead master, and if
Gelert breathed on the infant he saved from the
wolves, and left it a legacy of hydatids on the
liver, his dying yell wasn’t worth a hill of beans,
and is only dragged in like the “curtain” of the
third act in the melodrama. And all this indi-
cates only that the Faithful Hound and Gelert
should not be allowed inside houses.
The dog is still a useful and good friend in many
ways, but never in the household. He pursues ver-
min, like the rabbit and the fox, but he gets more
wtdp than nursing. The collie’s intelligence in
drafting sheep and cattle makes him a servant
sufficiently valuable for the drover to carry him on
the saddle when he is foot-sore, or to prompt the
making of boots to save the dog’s pads in very
stony or spear-grass country. But he is valued
mostly for his commercial use. To call a man a
dog or a dog’s bootmaker is an insult. “Cur” is
also a word provocative of strikes. But outside of
his uses, and especially in the household, he is, at
his best, a kindly carrier of disease; at his worst
he is a filthy and pampered brute. I knovv of one
English household where the siclc-diet of a dog
already dying of overfeeding was beef-tea and cham-
pagne' mixed. .
The habit of keeping dogs in houses is stupid
and filthy and British. What man forgets the
night to midnight function of many London house-
holds, when the overfed dog is led into the street,
and the result causes the healthy man from open-
air countries to curse all Britishers, from Alfred
the Great, who burned the calces, down to Vic-
toria, who exaggerated the British stodginess, and
belonged to the time of horse-hair furniture with
swollen mahogany legs ! .
Australia has been clear of the dog habit for long,
because, until the present generation, the men were
workers and the women were mothers, and there was
no leisured class. But with the increase of easily-
gotten wealth, and the second generation of people
whitened by laziness, the dog habit becomes as
filthy a thing as it is in London or New York.
There is in a Sydney suburb a family which com-
pels the domestics to speak of a common dog as
“Miss Fido,” and the other day the papers had
reports of a “christening” of a dog! The cele-
brant of the baptism is reported to have been an
officer of the Australian forces, and, if this be so,
one wonders why, in the interests of Australians,
the Japs should not invade Australia immediately.
“Binkie Spencer Ernest” is the dog’s baptismal
name. “Binkie was dressed in white and had a
regulation baby’s feeding-bottle to soothe him before
the event. There were many guests. A side-table
groaned under the delicacies for dogs. The christen-
ing cake had a huge sugar dog on top, and Binkie
cut this himself after his muzzle had been whetted
for the ceremony. The christening gifts included
an eiderdown quilt for Binkie’s head, a new collar,
a toy dQg full of chocolates, a ball, a cake of dog
soap, and a cup full of chocolates.”
The dog “had a regulation baby’s feeding bottle
to soothe him!” Why not a regulation baby to
lick ? Or a regulation baby to eat ? Babies —regula-
tion or otherwise —are cheap enough, and unwelcome
enough, and there were hundreds of them hungry
while “the table groaned under delicacies for dogs”
and Binkie Spencer Ernest Reginald cut the christen-
ing cake.

Sugar and a Yellow Peril.
“Labasa” hoists a danger signal
Fiji has a new Governor, who till recently helped
to handle the reins for J. Bull in Hong Kong.
Since his arrival in this group he has been spreading
himself on the virtues of the Chow, and appears to
favor the importation of Chinese labor throughout
Fiji, in place of the indentured Indian coolie, who,
owing to good seasons in his own country, and the
strictness of the Indian immigration authorities, is
becoming harder and harder every year to obtain.
The fat men of the Fijian community who are now
putting up for the local Legislative Council, all
favor the yellow agony, and seem to have the sup-
port of a big bulk of the community ; so it is more
than likely an attempt will be made to follow the
awful example of Tahiti, and promote Chinese immi-
gration. If the Commonwealth and Maoriland get
busy right away, the scheme may be nipped in the
bud, as the Colonial Office, after its little experience
on the Rand, will think hard before adopting the sug-
gestion, especially if Fisher and Ward bark loud
enough the moment the matter crops up. Failing
this, something else will have to be done. The
bulk of Fijian sugar is consumed in Maoriland, and
the imposition of a high tariff by the windy islands
in favor of white-grown Australian sugar would
settle not only the Chow question here, but almost
everything else as well, and would, at the same
time, open up a vast field for State mills in Queens-
land and N.S.W., where cane lands in manv in-
stances are from 20 to 50 per cent, more fertile
than those of the Western Pacific Islands. Austra-
lia, in return, could comfortably grant Maoriland a
free market for oats, flax, etc. That, however, is
a secondary consideration. The thing to be done
immediately is to get Fisher and Ward to have a
word to say on the question of bringing Chinese into
British colonies in the South Pacific; and as the
spokesmen for the great white communities in those
parts, as well as the representatives of the white
countries with which the yellow “indentured” labor-
ers will come into competition, they have every
right to speak. In fact, they have no right to
keep silent.
The N.S.W. Government has at last tabled
a Bill to create a Convention of Citizens of
Sydney to discuss all things necessary for a
complete and up-to-date Greater Sydney Coun-
cil. This Convention will consist of about 40
delegates, elected from the Federal elector-
ates in the Greater Sydney area. Went-
worth, East, West, South, and North
Sydney, Cook, Parkes, Dailey and Lang
will elect five each ; and part of Nepean and
Parramatta will elect two each. The Con-
vention will discuss (1) the territory of the
new council. (2) the powers it will exercise,
(3) the services it will take over, (4) the
control it will have of existing services, such
as Water and Sewerage, Harbor Trust,
Trams, etc. Also, it will handle the finance
question, but will be debarred from discuss-
ing Unification versus Federation (the form-
er is the Labor party’s policy), or the fran-
chise, another policy matter. Already the
aldermanic animalculae in the suburban
council areas are beginning to squirm, and
shrill yells to the Legislative Council will be
heard in the land and over the waters of the
harbor, like foghorns and bad-weather sirens.
If the Legislative Council.refuses to straight-
en up Sydney’s tangled civic government, or
to allow the citizens to do it themselves,
then the inevitable fight for the Council’s
own abolition will begin. It could not
come on a better question; for even the com-
mercial community is beginning to find
divided control and a swarm of helpless
authorities intolerable.

A Commission is to inquire into the “sweated
trades” in N. S. Wales. That the conditions
of the “white workers” who toil at home, the
hair-workers, artificial flower-makers, and a
number of others are infamous is as true as
anything can be. But the trouble is to
prove it. The people who are sweated
are so crushed that, in most cases, they have
not even the courage to join a union; and as
for giving evidence before a Commission, in
the face of the boss, they would rather die
than do it. In spite of all the declarations
to the contrary, they know that workers who
have given evidence before Wages Boards,
Arbitration Courts, and so forth, have been
penalised ; and, since they haven’t a day’s keep
put by, they feel that they dare not take a
risk. Fortunately, Minister Beeby, who has
charge of this Department, has been through
the mill. He appeared for the shophands
in the memorable case before the Arbitra-
tion Court, and must have personal know-
ledge of how some of the witnesses were
victimised afterwards. Yet if the workers
themselves do not give evidence, how is the
truth to be brought to light V There is a
way, and it is better by far than the exam-
ination of shivering, half-starved workers
speaking with the terror of the sack before
their eyes; and that way is to go straight to
the books of firms suspected of sweating.
Then the public may see to what extent it
ought to sympathise with some manufac-
turers in their howl about the shortage of
labor.
O
A beautiful illustration of how the “No”
policy works out for employer and employee
was provided by some evidence given before
the N.S.W. Country Shop Assistants’ Wages
Board at Wagga the other day. A Corowa
storekeeper pleaded for exemption from the
award on the ground of the fierce competi-
tion of Wahgunyah, Rutherglen and Yarra-
wonga, on the Victorian side, where no
Wages Board had jurisdiction, and business
was sunk in the slough of cut-throat com-
petition and sweating. On the Victorian
side, grocery assistants were getting from
£2 down to a wretched 15s. (Evidence
showed that the ruling minimum for grocers
in Wagga and thereabouts was £2 55., while
men with more experience got anything up
to £3.) Girls in the rag department were
toiling for the half-a-crown a week which
used to disgrace the Sydney fluff trade. The
Corowa employers said they knew it was
a shocking state of things, but they were
helpless until John Weaky’s Cabinet de-
cided it was unsafe to procrastinate longer
and granted a Wages Board for the Victorian
border districts. And even then there was
no guarantee that the conditions imposed
would correspond to the N.S.W. award.
It was, they added, a situation clamoring
for Federal legislation. Yet probably most
of the Corowa employers went along in
right-thinking indignation and voted “No”
on April 26, while the Corowa employees
were too tired or dispirited to vote, or got
lost on the way.
+
It is alleged on good authority that there
are not now, in the whole Melbourne metro-
politan area, eight firms employing more
than five journeymen tailors each, and that
of the firms which used to employ from 12
to 40 tailors, not 25 per cent, now employ
more than three. The firms turn out more
work, but women do it—for less wages.
The Board that fixed the minimum wage
failed to discover the relative value .of
tailors’ and tailoresses’ work, and, after giv-
ing the men, in most classes, a wage of £2 15s.
a week, blundered badly in fixing the pay of
a competent adult tailoress at only £1 Is.
a week. A disastrous result of this anomaly
is that the employers are training no new
tailors. Instead, they are crying aloud for
the importation of more female labor, which
is a futile remedy anyhow. Female labor
is, or should be, a temporary thing at best.
The State wants mothers in its homes, not
middle-aged spinsters in its factories; and
Parliament should insist that the ratio be-
tween men’s and women’s wages be so ad-
justed that the woman shall not only get
equal pay for equal work, but that her mini-
mum wage shall be so high as to discourage
her employment, and encourage her to be-
come the wife of the man who will displace
her.
Fkom a Melbourne critic, concerning the
Felton Bequest and its art purchases:—
The dissatisfaction expressed with many of the
purchases made by the administrators of the Felton
Bequest is hardly likely to be allayed by the three
recent additions of Old Masters —the Reynolds
(£5000), the Hofpner- (£2000), and the Mor-
land (£440).
In surveying the results of the expenditure of
some £45,000 since Mr. Felton's decease, the
first point that strikes the observer is the absence
of any sound policy or business principle underlying
the investment of the income from this handsome
endowment —an income which at present amounts
to about £7OOO, and will ultimately be between
£lO,OOO and £12,000 per annum. Firstly, the
purchase of imperfect, damaged or incomplete ex-
amples of the dead Masters —often at fancy prices
based solely on the name—is an entirely mistaken
policy for a young country to pursue. It is futile
for Australia to expect to acquire by purchase any
important works, truly representative of the finest
achievements of the great Masters, in competition
with the wealth of Europe or of the American mil-
lionaires. Earlier discarded works, often imperfect
or unfinished, which are traded in by dealers on
account of the market value of the name, only
serve to give a false conception of the artists’
greatness, and are unworthy of a in a Na-
tional Gallery of true Art. The most we can
aspire to obtain are good copies of original master-
pieces by the old Masters.
It is difficult to conceive any argument that
could justify the expenditure of a thousand guineas
on a faded water-color —albeit the handiwork of
the immortal Turner himself —which in another 20
or 30 years will cease to possess any value at all.
To those who know the works of the • real Turner-
in the London National Gallery, there' is nothing
in this little sketch that affords the least concep-
tion of the greatness of his genius. Even the com-
paratively modern Corot —the most important work
of the dead Masters yet purchased—is relatively in-
significant, and but poorly represents the achieve-
ment of that incomparable artist. And (he highly-
lauded Watteau (£3500) would never be recog-
The Song of the Heathen.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We must not kiss in the gardens,
We must not sing in the street,
We must not jump with a joyous shout
When a long-lost friend we meet.
We must not race by the sea-shore,
We must not sit on the sand,
We must not laugh on a New Year Night,
For this is the Wowsers’ land.
Oh, this is the Wowsers’ land,.
And the laughing days are o er,
For most of the things that we used to do
We must not do any more!
We mustn’t be glad on a Sunday now,
For fear of a fine or gaol;
We must not eat the fruits of the earth,
Nor drink of the ginger ale;
We must not go to a picture-show,
No matter now pure it he;
On a moonlit night, when the stars are
bright,
We scarce dare stroll by the sea.
We scarce dare stroll by the sea,
For the plain clo. “John’s” about,
And the she-male’s nigh with an evil eye,
And the Wowseradite is out.
We dare not walk in the park by dark,
We dare not sit on a seat,
For the kill-joy is there with a baleful glare,
And the night “cop” off his beat;
We dare not pause to talk to our girl,
At her door we scarce dare halt,
Lest she be fouled by a gimlet bird,
And we run in for assault.
And heavily fined for assault,
For the thing is everywhere,
With its rusty coat and scraggy throat —
Oh, the holy bird is there!
The little hoys mustn’t play marbles,
They mustn’t trundle a hoop ;
We must not cheer for the Senate,
Nor yet for our nation whoop !
Oh, tell me in cursing anger,
Oh, tell me in grief and tears,
Oh, tell me if this is Australia
I wake to after the years!
I wake to after the years—
The land that I thought was free ;
Oh, tell me whatever in Heaven
Or Hell can the matter be!
Oh, glorious skies above us,
Oh, spring-green earth beneath,
Oh, kindly gods that love us,
Oh, the open air we breathe;
Oh, song of the mighty Bushland,
Oh, light of the wild bush-flowers,
Oh, the surge of the grandest ocean
At the gate of this land of ours!
At the gate of this land of ours
By religious mania cursed;
Say what has brought clouds upon usV
Who brought the germ in first?
If this be the end of our striving,
The reward of the fighting years;
If this be the goal of the Commonwealth,
I go with the Cavaliers ;
I go with the drinking fighters,
With laughing rakes and carls —
If this be the work of Cromwell,
Bide hard ! Ride hard for King Charles!
Ride hard ! Ride hard for King Charles,
Nor think of the useless slain ;
Then back to the world and nature —■
The truth and the devil again!
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

nised by the connoisseur as representative of the
tar greater achievement of this artist in the best
period of his career. That it must have been one
ot his earliest efforts is exemplified in the cramped
drawing of the figures, in which—both in elegance
and refinement—he afterwards particularly excelled,
were Watteau alive and confronted with this
work, he would certainly turn it to the wall.
Again, what value as a work of art can anyone
discover in Monticelli's half-obliterated study
entitled “Fete Champetre” (£500)? Part of the
original painting appears to have been damaged or
destroyed, and what remains of the artist’s original
conception is scarcely discernible to the most prac-
tised eye. Surely this discarded fragment need not
have found its last resting-place in our capital!
The examples cited afford sufficient evidence in
support of my contention that the administrators
are working on the wrong lines, and that num-
bers of the selections are unworthy of the ob-
jects of the benefactor. Instead of dissipating the
valuable income on damaged or inferior works, hav-
ing at most an academic interest, we should as a
business people seek to enrich our Art collection
with the masterpieces of living masters, or of
those but recently deceased, whose works would in-
crease in value as the years roll by, and which in
after times could, if deemed advisable, be realised
or exchanged at an increased value on their orig-
inal cost. Thus an excellent opportunity to acquire
a masterpiece of exceptional merit occurred when
Lefebee’s “Chloe” was offered for sale by the
executors of the late surgeon Fitzgerald. But the
Felton Trustees were outbid by a private buyer,
who gave £BOO for this chef d’oeuvre, which now
hangs on the walls of a hotel. It is quite possible
the Trustees may some years hence purchase it at
a considerably increased figure, as in the ease of
Mackennal's “Circe,” for which they recently paid
double the price ( £1200) at which it had been
offered to them a few years previously ( £600).
Would it not be more advantageous in the long
run to devote, if needed, the whole of one year’s
income to the acquisition of one genuine master-
piece, than to distribute it broadcast in petty
amounts on mediocre works of more than doubtful
merit ?
It is a matter of national importance that so
splendid a legacy should be administered under
such conditions as will build up for future genera-
tions of Australians a noble collection of works of
art, which any young nation would be proud to
possess, and worthy the public spirit of the late
Mr. Felton. It is, however, painfully evident that
the Trustees have been guided by no higher ideal
in their estimation of Art than that afforded by the
virtue of a name, and that the expenditure of the
funds has been accomplished with conspicuous disre-
gard for the commercial aspect and importance of
the question.
A somewhat wily deputation, consisting of
Pecksniff, Stiggins and co., last week
waited on the acting Chief Secretary of
N.S.W., and complained that the opening of
fruit and refreshment shops on Sunday was
being sadly abused. Vegetables, tobacco and
other instruments of sin were sold by these
establishments, and the law was being griev-
ously fractured. The deputation was reason-
able, and had come in a broad spirit. The
opening of fruit shops might be a good thing;
but as the license given them was being ter-
ribly abused, the only remedy was to close
all shops on the Sabbath. These places em-
ployed assistants, and the unfortunate people
had to work seven days per week. And,
owing to the way the law was being broken,
more and more shops, the owners of which
would gladly keep the “seventh” day holy,'
were being forced in self-defence to keep open
too, and make their assistants work. It was
dreadful, and the deputation thought rhat, in
the interests of the workers, who had to bear
the brunt of it all, these places should be
closed right up and the law enforced without
any exceptions. It was a neatly-displayed
trap ; but the Acting-Chief Secretary con-
ceals under an angelic exterior a consider-
able knowledge of how many beans make
five, and shrewdly guessed that it was not the
woes of the over-worked assistants that wor-
ried the deputation. At present, Coogee,
Bondi, Manly and other seaside resorts are
crowded on Sundays with working people
and their children. Whole families make
for the seaside; it is the one outing that
thousands and thousands of families can
have; and to these thousands the open re-
freshment shop is. practically indispensable.
If they were not indispensable, thev would,
indeed, not worry the Wowser. His real
objection is, not to the open shop, but to
the outing; and, after the manner of his
kind, he seeks to attack it, not from the
front, but by climbing up the back-stairs. So
when the Chief Sec. asked whether the
deputation would stop the Sunday tram and
train —which would carry the reform to its
logical conclusion —only one parson had the
honesty to say straight-out that he would. The
rest smiled smugly, and said they were not
there to discuss that —they were there
merely to ask that the law should be en-
forced without exemptions or exceptions.
But Mr. Flowers refused to bite. He
promised if information were given as to
the selling of cabbages on Sundays, and
other breaches of the law, action would be
taken ; and that was all he would promise.
So a dispirited deputation moved out and
off—no doubt to think no some other back-
stairs way of stopping the people from ob-
serving the Sabbath in any other way than
as a Day of Bust.
