A word in the ear of Trefle, N.S.W. Min-
ister for Buckwheat and Pumpkins
For 16 years there has been a so : called experi-
mental farm at Pera Bore, and the people are no
wiser to-day as to the agricultural value of the West
than they were 16 years ago, simply because no ex-
perimental work has been carried out. When the
farm was started a manager was imported. He
planted hundreds of trees —peaches, apricots, nec-
tarines, mandarins, oranges, lemons—and thousands
of grape vines. After some six or seven years, the
expert concluded that the West was only good for
( oranges , arid lgmons, and everything else was rooted
out. Since then no experiments with stone fruit or
vines have been made, though the results at manv a
wsstern homestead are distinctly encouraging. The
public, however, naturally knows nothing about these
small private experiments; and when settlers come
in hundreds to. this part—and that will be when the
Darling River is locked—they will be as much in the
dark as if there had never been any experimental
farm in existence, and a vast amount of money and
time will be wasted. Minister Tkefle should get
busy. N. S ; Wales has its biggest public estate out
there, and it wants to know how to make the last
pound of profit out of the possession.
Abo. debaters on the mental cure for
snake-bite should remember that to bluff one-
self cured of a real bite is a mighty different
proposition to frightening oneself dead from
an imaginary one. And. before undertaking
any risky enterprises of this or any other
sort, level-headed men should inspire their
wisdom by infrequent but enthusiastic inter-
views with D.C.L., the Whisky of the Wise.*

As a choir the Sheffield singers leave very
little to be wdshed for; the weakness of the
attack lies in the soloists. Oratorio singers
don’t, however, grow on every bush ; so it
was no doubt a question of weakening the
choir or strengthening the solo end of the
argument. Very wisely it was decided not
to weaken the choir. “The Messiah,” the
opening programme of the Sydney season,
might certainly have gained in massive ef-
fect if the number had been even greater;
but in the beautifully clear quality of the
voices, and in the perfect command which
the conductor (Dr. Coward) had over them,
the performance was so good that nobody
need expect anything better until the Celestial
choir strikes up. This opening perform-
ance in Sydney had the advantage also of
including a very fine bass —Robert Charles-
worth —among the soloists, and some of the
numbers of Miss Jennie Taggart were also
completely satisfactory. The leading tenor,
Henry Turnpenny, got much more satis-
faction out of the lighter role of Prince
Henry in “The Golden Legend.” This Sul-
livan music was sung on Saturday evening,
when almost as many people were turned
away from the Town Hall as were able to
crowd into it. Had the baritone soloist
been equal to his task, this might have
ranked as the best concert of the series. The
Legend was preceded by Bach’s “Sing Ye
to the Lord,” a piece full of difficulties; and
the performance amounted to a triumph of
the conductor’s generalship. To this con-
cert Arthur Mason also contributed two or-
gan solos, one of them a little thing of his
own, full of charm. The great Ninth Sym-
phony of Beethoven was undertaken on Sat-
urday afternoon by the Sydney Symphony
Orchestra, the choir supplying the final
burst of ecstatic song. The orchestra had
the help of P. S. Kelly, a returned Austra-
lian, in a Beethoven concerto for piano and
orchestra. This was his first appearance in
Sydney, after many European successes;
and a brilliant performance justified a re-
markable outburst of enthusiasm, though
Beethoven worshippers gasped at the intro-
duction of cadenzas in a composition of the
“master.” The cnoir is now hurrying on to
Maoriland; thence it will return to Aus-
tralia—but cannot, it is understood, pay a
return visit to N. S. Wales. On its choral
work during the season in Sydney it is quite
safe to say that it is going to earn three
cheers everywhere.

There will be less Girl and more Gore at
Her Majesty’s after Friday night. The
mystical business, hitherto supplied by a
Giant, will on and after Saturday night be
in the hands of a Ghost. Gravediggers will
usurp the interludes of Kenyon and Cannot:
the macabre waggishness of the churchyard
will replace the more robust wit which
enianates from bursting motor-cars. To
sink metaphor, the pantomime leaves Her
Majesty’s within 24 hours of the Corona-
tion, giving place to Hamlet, Prince of Den-
mark. H. B. Irving is to portray the horno-
Cidal foreign royalty, and Miss Dorothea
Baird will be his Ophelia,
Miss Florence Young has been shamefully
insulted in the Bohemian Restaurant at
Sydney Theatre Royal evei’y night this
week, and unless Judkins or some such
Moral Force intervenes, the outrage is likely
to continue for months. The dismissal of
the restaurant’s present powerful and
heavily-armed chucker-out, and the substitu-
tion in his place of a small but wordy Wow-
ser, might make for Purity. “The Balkan
Princess” management is hereby offered the
tin for what it is worth.

*# * *
“Starving, are you !” yells the top-hatted
second villain to the noble, crippled child.
“What right have you to starve ?” And seizing
the child’s hard-earned loaf of bread, he bowls
it down the alley. The incident happens in
Act 3 of “Driving a Girl to Destruction,”
the high-speed George Marlodrama which
transpired at Sydney Adelphi last Satur-
day. It bears a strong family resemblance
to other melos. Its nose certainly is a bit
redder, and it drinks harder and faster
than its relatives; but its ground plan
and front elevation are practically the
same. The three villainous characters only
last for three acts because the noble-hearted
crew of live are thoroughly stupid, and be-
cause the victimised typist, round Avhom the
plot riots, is always ready to accept a bou-
quet of drinks in lieu of an explanation or a
settlement of her lamentable affairs. Three
totally idiotic male characters transpire at
frequent intervals in improbable clothes
and create distracting uproars. They are
intended to provide the humor; but a police-
man-dramatist would have no hesitation in
arresting the three for being on the
premises without lawful excuse. The strong-
est feature of the play is its wealth of situa- •
tions, some of them fairly well-devised and
unexpected.
* * * *
Louise Hampton and Cecil Mannering
make their first Australian appearance as
Ruby Wright (the Driven One), and Robert
Ray (the Driven One’s sailor lover). Miss
Hampton is a sympathetic actress with a
quiet, effective style; R. Ray is a player of
the vigorous order, who semes to be subject;
to first-night nervousness. Nellie Fergus-
son makes the best of the ugly part of Ruth
Wright, the typist-victim. Ethel Buckley,
as Bessie Betts, is a cheerfully independent
barmaid who is never seen behind the bar
and insults the villain —the only person she
carries drink to. Essie Clay is Madame de
Meral on the programme, but she occasion-
ally forgets the fact, and relapses into un-
broken English. 11. W. Diver (in the play
Lucas List, of List and Co, Ltd., General
Ruiners) skilfully earns the hatred of the
house; John Cosgrove makes Octavius
Screwe a thirsty, giggling evildoer, who
seems to love villainy for its own sake, and
dresses as if he habitually forgets to collect
his wages of sin. Robert Inman has an
easy, head-patting role as the Rev. Christo-
pher Lovatt (a Clean and Healthy Soul).
J. P. O’Neill, Wilton Welch, and Arthur
Shirley, as the three irresponsibles, do their
best to deliver the laughs; and Miss Cecil
Bradley is the remarkably agile cripple boy
who climbs about like a leading seaman and
is robbed of the bread.

On Monday night the Religious Editor,
taking a hint from the advertisement curtain
at the Tivoli, asked himself to have a drink;
but having seen the first part already, he
slightly varied the curtain’s suggestion and
drank till half time. Then returning, he was
disgusted to find the theatre fuller than he
was—which is, no doubt, why a lot of others
don’t follow this same plan. The Zanfrellas
are the current draw; they defy the laws
of gravity, whirl in space and crawl around
each other in an amazing manner. The
Columbia Trio fold up their tuneful voices
at the end of this week. Keldine and Klimo,
trick cyclists ; Croxton, monologue entertain-
er ; the juggling Kavanagh boys; Delino the
wire-walker, and the Australian Dartos are
the other principal attractions.
** * *
At the J. D. Williams Lyric Theatre,
Sydney, the beginning of this week saw a
very fine film of local production, entitled
“A Ticket in Tatt.’s.” It was horsey; and
murder, greed and treachery combined to
point a moral and adorn an exciting tale.
It was filmed by the local concern presided
over by Gaston Mervale at the studio at
Queenscliffe, Manly; and the actors in-
cluded well-known mummers like Ilarrie
Ireland and Louise Carbassa. The pro-
gramme for to-day shows how the Italian
Army is exercised in the Alps, and some of
the horsemanship is bewildering even to an
Australian. “Amusements at Singapore”
tells how the gentle Oriental passes his
spare time. “Marozia” is one of the splen-
did costume plays for which the Pathe
Co. is famous; “Who’s Who at the Zoo”
is an extravaganza by the Gaumont Co. of
Paris, and is a sequel to one of the
same title shown last week; and there is
the usual dramatic love tale and a wild
farce —“Jane and the Sleeping Sickness” —
which cannot be described till it has been
seen 16 times.
** * *
At the Sydney Lyceum Spencer’s Theatre-
scope is about to make an energetic splash
in honor of the Coronation. The unveiling
of the great statue of Queen Victoria will
introduce the gorgeous business ; and, among
other things, “Under the Union Jack” will
provide an epitome of empire, or Imperial-
ism in tabloid form. For nine days there
will be special Coronation matinees for
ladies and children. On Friday Mr. Spencer
will show pictures of the previous day’s de-
monstrations, including the children’s out-
break at the Sydney Agricultural Ground.
In fact, it is quite within the range of possi-
bility that the energetic Higgins will have
his film ready to screen on Thursday night,
but there is no guarantee about that.
** * *
It is said that, in the event of the princi-
pals of “Our Miss Gibbs” being cast away in
a motor-car, or run over by a steam launch,
the members of the orchestra could step into
the various vacancies, and drift through the
parts without having to stop once to think
of a word. But as this would necessitate
the stage hands being called upon to whistle
the music, it is hoped nothing really serious
may happen in the long run. Familiarity
breeds content, and there is no urgent de-
mand for “Next, please,” at Her Majesty’s,
excepting among those who have to find some-
thing to say about the “Cryptos” opera as
fresh at least as last year’s eggs.

At Melbourne Royal “The Woman in the
Case” is bringing her career of crime to a
close, and will take her inflammatory wig out
of the public’s eye at an early date. Friday
night’s outburst will be the last occasion on
which Miss Foster will be called upon to
take excess of liquid nourishment out of an
urn, prior to having her head employed to
damage the elegant furnishings. On Satur-
day, 24th, another bad lot of the same com-
plexion arrives at the Royal, when Porter
Emerson Brown’s drama, “A Fool There
Was,” is to be staged for the first time here-
about. It is fondly hoped that this play
will lash W. Judkins to apostolic fury.
** * *
The second act of “Nobody’s Daughter,”
now providing wholesome entertainment to
Melbourne Princess’s patrons, is devoted to
frocks, frills, and mixed millinery; but as
an attempt to dramatise the dress, mantle
and hat departments, it falls short of an
artful success. The stage is filled with
bandboxes, and the ladies of the play de-
vote themselves to a long debauch of trying-
on. The spectacle offers no particular sat-
isfaction even for women. As non-combat-
ants, ladies in the audience find small joy
in the other person’s intemperate indulgence
in Paris hats. Minor personalities crowded
out of last week’s review are Greenaway’s
youthful baronet, who amuses his leisure
moments by falling out of an aeroplane—a
sketchy personage with a sandy atmosphere:
and Sweeney’s mechanic, the noble-hearted
British workman who is in love with No-
body’s Daughter, and likes her rather better
on learning that she is a little “illejim.”
This good young man does not contrive to
appear a splendid toiler making an irresist-
ible appeal, although that was clearly the
author’s hope.
** * *
These are the last appearances of Sousa
in the Victorian capital; so the people who
have not seen the champion or heard his
band are requested to visit the local Glaci-
arium at the earliest moment. Recitals are
now given twice daily, at 3 p.m. and 8 p.m.,
and the evening programmes are beginning
to include a lot of items included in response
to the entreaties of General Desire.

WOLFES
SCHNAPPS.
Principal of the new performers at the Opera
House is one Lamberti, who gives imperson-
ations of the masters of music.’ A. fresh
turn of great interest is supplied by Reichen s
amazing team of balancing dogs; while
Mdlle. Charmeroy, Parisian and _ piquant,
reveals herself as a singer and pianist. Other
Opera House people are mostly familiar
identities, and include Peggy Pryde, Black
and White, Caldwell and Verne, and the pop-
ular jester, Frederick Bluett.
* #■ * *
“The Woman of Pleasure” is the lady of
the moment at Melbourne King’s; and good
crowds are watching her procession of sensa-
tions with evident joy. There is a strong
Jingo sentiment about the play; but that
quality gets little recognition, through the
excitement aroused by a bloomered dame.
The lady trips into the plot in loud, check
knickers, and, though she affects a jaunty
air, she never anoears quite right. Some-
how these pants, that make some women
look like pictures, make others look like
puzzles.
** # *
During the past week “Thelma” has been
flying across West’s Melbourne screen in
several hundred feet of interesting film.
Corelli’s story makes a pleasant picture—
partly because its sentimental scenes are
subdued and rational. The same strong-
programme includes “A Tale of Two Cities,”
“Tontolini’s Clarionet,” and snapshots of
the Rome Exhibition in course of construc-
tion. Judging by the multitude of buildings
in this last production, Rome’s exhibition
will have the dimensions of a town.
m# # *
“The General’s Fate,” at the Praliran
Lyric (Vic.), pictures an episode in the
career of General Pichegru. The film, which
is handsomely tinted, also introduces the
young Napoleon, shown either raging like a
cyclone or rigid like a corpse. There is
plenty of excitement in “True to His Trust” ;
while “Pathos Gazette” regales the audi-
ence with photos, of hats and other recent
disasters and weird events. Respecting the
Lyric’s special films of famous operas, it is
announced that “Lucia di Lammermoor” will
be the next attraction.
* * *
Those who yearn for the intellectual
drama are getting a chance to prove that
their virtue is not a vain assumption. On
Monday, 26th, and three other nights, at the
Turn Verein Hall, Gregan McMahon’s new-
ly-organised Repertory Co., will present in
turn Hank in’s “The Two Mr. Wetherbys,”
Sheridan’s “The Critic” (Act II.), and Ib-
sen’s “John Gabriel Borkman.” The enter-
prise has received a lot of canvassing from
local literary quidnuncs, and Gregan Mc-
Mahon reckons that the first performances
will be found satisfactory to intellects which
are not too exacting.
#* * #
_At the Adelaide Theatre Royal the
Katherine Grey Co. has made a brilliant
success of the llodgson-Burnett “Dawn of a
To-morrow,” which is like “A Messenger
from Mars” without any magic, and with
religion substituted for morality. To say
that the characters mostly swear when they
are not praying, and frequently do both at
the same time, does not make the play
sound attractive; but it is full of fun, with
tears close behind the laughter. There is
a long cast, strikingly in contrast to the
brief ones of the co.’s American plays. Des-
mond and Mackay sail easily and gracefully
through what they have to do, and Miss
Grey makes the theatrical character, the
slum-girl, as little stagey as possible. Never-
theless, her artless profanities seemed more
lifelike in the book, where she was a mere
kid ; in the play she is made older, to allow
an unnecessary love-interest. Some fine bits
of acting are put in by Leslie Victor, as a
Johnnie with the beginnings of a conscience;
by Winter Hall, as a Fatman with nerves ;
by Atholwood, as the specialist who diagnoses
his case; and by Susie'Vaughan as a slum-
dweller, who prays hopelessly for her hus-
band’s reform, and “just throws in a os-
tridge-fevver” for herself. Miss Grey is
now showing her versatility in a double bill,
as Madame Butterfly and the serving-maid
in “Arms and the Man.”
From a confirmed member of the House
of Commons:—
Went to Whitehall Rooms to hear George Reid
discourse on “The Past, Present and Future of
Australia.” A rambling, discursive, unprepared,
ill-digested , address, As he himself confessed, he
was unable to “keep on the rails.” George is too
busy lunching and dining to find time to prepare a
proper and. worthy address on such a subject. His
interjectional jokes were of the poorest pattern.
“That- gentleman must be a squatter,” quoth
he, when somebody cried “Hear, hear!” to a
statement about the prosperity of the pastoral in-
dustry. George was followed by Lord Denman,
the new G.-G., who avowed a dense ignorance of
Australia, but added that he was learning as fast
as he could by daily attendance at the office of
the High Commissioner. McGowen, N.S.W. Pre-
mier, boldly defied all the rules of grammar for
five minutes; and burly John Forrest made a pain-
ful exhibition of himself for a similar period.
John Downer, of South Aus., proclaimed himself
a Conservative and a Unionist, and was promptly
sat upon and squelched by the chairman (Earl
Carrington) for introducing party politics. Alto-
gether a bad evening for the reputation of Austra-
lian oratory.

“Theorbo”: Armes Beaumont, for whom a fund is
being got together, has a peculiar claim to sym-
pathy ; he has been almost sightless for 4 4 years,
through a shooting accident, an unfortunate. affair
caused by one of Lyster’s brothers. Beaumont had
then been six years on the stage, and remained over
20 more. Pie could not see his music—-how wonder-
ful, then, was the great range of his characters! In
all he sustained about 80. Never a great tenor, he
had, however, all the advantage of a fine person-
ality and a good voice of the robust order. His
Don Caesar de Bazan, Faust, Thaddeus (“Bohemian
Girl”), Vasco d 6 Gama (“I’Africaine”), and many
other parts, will be remembered with favor in Syd-
ney and Melbourne. He made a snug little fortune,
but, dropping it in the boom, bravely turned to the
drudgery of teaching, with all his disadvantages.
Now, at 70, he deserves more than ordinary recogni-
tion.
For the Coronation opera shivoo at Covent Gar-
den, on June 26, the following charges were fixed:
Pit and grand tier boxes, 100 guineas each; front
and second tier boxes respectively, 60 and 40
guineas; orchestra stalls, 20 guineas; balcony, 10
guineas; first four rows amphitheatre, 4 guineas;
other rows, 3 guineas. There was a stampede for
seats, and the booking closed six weeks ago.
“A.E.G.” : “C. Ockney” is quite mistaken when
he states that Waller is the original “Beaucaire.”
The late Richard Mansfield played, it originally in
America for two seasons. So great was his success
that Frohman offered to take the full co. to the
Duke of York’s Theatre, London; but Mansfield
wouldn’t go, remembering his previous failure at
the Lyceum, as Richard 111., and in “Jelcyll and
Hyde,” etc. Had he gone Waller would never have
been seen in the part. I have seen them both,
and Waller wasn’t within coo-ee of Mansfield.
“Big Drummer”: W. S. Gilbert was actually for
a while a member of a tony West End club “for
noblemen and gentlemen.” Of course, he was never
happy there. When someone stole his new hat he
was unhappier than ever, and pinned up a notice
in the hall stating that he would be obliged if the
nobleman who stole his hat would return it, and no
questions would be asked. When called up to ex-
plain the insult, he remarked that as no gentleman
would steal a hat, it must have been a nobleman.
He neither dined nor wined there again.

it Wowserisin any more. It will be Flowerserism
for the future.
To Australians it seemed that Blarney, the fat and
flabby young tenor who impersonated an unemo-
tional Pinkerton in “Madame Butterfly,” was quite
out of place as an operatic artist. He sang satisfac-
torily in foggy tones, but his appearance and crudity
as an actor of heroic parts were fatal to the illusion.
Yet Blarney was engaged for the expensive Beecham
Opera Co. when he returned to London« and lately
he was appearing with a choice selection of the co
m an excerpt from an old Offenbach work at the
Alhambra. This was the first time that a handsome
scene from opera, with full orchestra and chorus
had been tried as a music-hall turn.
Edmund Burke, whom Melba has secured for the
grand opera tour of Australia, is a Canadian. Up
till 12 months ago he had been singing in opera at
the Hague for three or four years. He made his
first appearance, your Worship," at Covent Garden
when the Opera House was re-opened after King
Edward’s death. On that occasion he solemnly
warbled as the High Priest in “Samson and Deli-
lah.”
Beaumont Smith, late sec. to Bilanderson, has
been careering over Europe looking for a little
man named Ulpts. The chase led through
Rome, Florence, Milan and Lucerne; ultimately
Smith ran his quarry to earth at Vienna, and en-
gaged him on the spot to come to Australia. Died-
erich Ulpts is 3ft. lOin. high, and weighs 601 b.
He is a spruce, well-featured fellow of 50, married
to a lady of normal size, and the father of two
normal-sized children. He will bring with him a
co. of midgets, the smallest being Ludwig Mess
(height 25in.), who is a lightning cartoonist. Mrs.
Tom Thumb, who was in Australia years ago, will
possibly make the journey with her second husband,
Count Magri (height 3ft. sin.). Mrs. Thumb-
Maari is about 70, and has stipulated against Sun-
day shows.
“Christian B.”: Queer the fads and superstitions
of theatrical managers. Tom Pollard had the 13
superstition very badly. Also, he had an idea that
a gong was necessary to the success of any musical
production, and W. S. Percy can tell to what
lengths he’d go to get the implement introduced.
One manager regarded the fictitious name “Hugh
Power” as a mascotte. Programme collectors will
find the name in casts extending over a quarter
of a century, and it still crops up frequently. 11.
Power is the only actor on record guilty of playing
in three pieces in three different cities on the one
night. William Anderson has a similar supersti-
tion. For years he has insisted on the bogus actor-
name, “Frank Rossmore,” appearing on his day-
bills and programmes. Frankie should be inclined
to senile decay now; but, the other day, when
Anderson staged the Lilliputian panto., “The Old
Woman Who Lived in a Shoe,” he blossomed out
as large as life among the rest of the kids in the
cast.
“Floater”: New York, which turned down “Mrs.
Warren’s Profession” with horror, has given an en-
thusiastic reception to “Thais.” The heroine is the
lady who has been described as “the wittiest Flossie
of them all.” In the drama she drifts through the
marble halls and rose-strewn gardens in semi-demi-
robes, pursued by youths in the last throes of calf
love, hedonist philosophers, and, queerest of all,
a fantastic Christian seer. But after splashing round
one of the most lavish displays of artistic Flossie-
dom, the author saves the conscience of N’Yorlc by
converting the lady. Had Bernard Shaw made Mrs.
Warren “repent,” and then rung the curtain down
on her class in Sunday-school, N’York would no
doubt have taken that play to its bosom too.
Matheson Lang has gone to S’Africa with a co.,
but not to play the pieces which he tried in this
country. His partner in the spec is W. E. Hollo-
way, presumably a son of the Plain Bill (W.J.) Hol-
loway, who made his pile by exploiting Essie Jenyns.
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: Mrs. Emma Griffiths,
who died in Melbourne lately, was a daughter of
G. H. Rogers, a leading comedian of the ’4o’s, ’so’s
and ’6o’s. “Old Rogers” and “old Lambert” di-
vided the honors of Sir Peter Teazle, Sir Anthony
Absolute, and such characters 50 years ago. There
were two sisters on the stage, Emma and Fanny;
but the former on her marriage retired. The
first Mrs. Rogers was a sister of Charles Young,
a famous comedian, contemporary with Rogers. An-
other sister, Fanny Young, married the comedian
George Washington Daniels.
“Snell”: I consider it an error of artistic judg-
ment to put the blind, halt, or lame on the stage
without strong excuse; but the author of “Nobody’s
Daughter” has dragged in a young cripple, and keeps
him limping through three acts, for no reason but
to point an out-of-the-way moral of a stupidly senti-
mental kind. The father and mother of Nobody’s
Daughter, respectably married (though not to each
other), are grievously unfortunate in the matter of
multiplication. The mother has no children; the
father only this cripple. This is rubbed in as retri-
bution evidently; but as the unlawful offspring is
strong and lusty, the true moral is shockingly other-
wise, implying that if people want sound children
they had best have them on the off-theory.
After all, the rich squatter is much the same as
the cloakroom attendant. He merely takes charge
of the sheep’s overcoat.
Something new in picture shows is promised in
Melbourne. It is an exhibition of original adver-
tising poster designs, in aid of the Children’s Hospi-
tal. The persons whose goods the designs advertise
are to be asked to pay £1 Is. per picture for the
advt., and the public will be required to pay to see
them. The Gaud Mayor is pushing the scheme, but
success is expected.
“C. Ockney”: Who told “Cockaigne” (B. l/6/’ll)
that “Kismet” (Oscar Asche’s latest London pro-
duction, and a big winner by all accounts) was a
translation from the German? Certainly not the
programme, and decidedly not the critics. I have
read the notices of all the principal London dailies
and they, without exception, give the credit of the
authorship to Knoblanch, an American, who has
been in England for quite a while, and whose name
has figured on metropolitan programmes ere now
in connection with several trivial (and not too
successful) one-act pieces. In an interview, Kno-
blanch says he wrote the play 15 years ago. ’I sup-
pose that means it has taken him 14 years to get
it produced. He was lucky in hitting on a sub-
ject that would keep all that time. Most of us go
in for the modern play (which we are told every
manager wants). We spend a year writing it, and
another year trying to get it “placed,” and then
it’s clean out of date! I congratulate the author
(not the translator) of “Kismet” on his success,
and am grateful for his tip !
“S.S.”: I went to church last Sunday. I did it
to please her, and expected to be bored, but instead
found myself quite enjoying the monologue enter-
tainment. They talk about the union of church
and stage. Well, this parson was the best actor
I’d seen in seven days. He was sufficiently young,
extremely becoming, pale of face, long of liair, and
he preached with the subtle movements of a posture
dancer, and worked thin, taper hands with won-
derful effect. He seemed to be waving lilies
aloft. His voice was trained to the last possible
nuance, and he played his eyes for all they were
worth. There Was a fairly large congregation, two-
thirds women, and the performance was a tremen-
dous success. I believe it would have been a suc-
cess at the Opera House. Only perfect restraint
prevented the women demanding "an encore.

Melbourne Argus expresses its sym-
pathy with a millionaire named Joel:—
Why Mr. Joel should be unpopular is not clear. Now
that he has won the Derby, and is entitled to have his
name enrolled with King Edward and other illus-
trious persons as the owner of a Derby winner he
can afford to laugh at his detractors.
There’s “loyalty” for you ! Joel may be the
embodiment of all that’s dislikable in
gilded Jewmanity; but since his name was
enrolled with King Edward’s (not to men-
tion many “illustrious” lords and blacklegs)
Joel can afford to sit on his money bags
and bid defiance to unpopularity.
Apart from the Federal Ministers, the
only coronating Australian to get his name
prominently into the cables so far is N.S.W
Premier McGowen. His crawl through a
sewer seems to have done the trick; and, in
case that should have misfired, he stopped
in the Strand or somewhere and helped a
boilermaker do some riveting. None of the
others, State or Federal, appear to have hit
upon an advertising idea. Yet Murray had
his chance to challenge the Kingdom to a
sleeping competition, while Lewis might
easily have gained undying fame by attending
Covent Garden Market and dropping a large-
sized bomb among the agents who are making
fortunes out of the Tasmanian apple-growers.
JUNE 22, 1911.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To-day a matinfie will be produced
More wildly lavish in a thousand ways
Than Barnum ever dreamed of, or than Rome
Imagined in her richest, maddest days.
No spectacle in all the world has had
A fraction of its vast advertisement.
The plot, the cast, the costumes all are known
From hall to hut, from club to navvy’s tent.
The normal drama is “presented bv”
Or “under the direction of” some mere
Drab commoner. This is to be “put on”
By one who’s Britain’s premier duke and peer.
No slap-dash work’s allowable, and so
Last March each keen exponent of his art
Found on the call-board orders to rehearse
Three solid months before he played his part.
The small-part artist’s usually Low
(His opposite is hard to find as pearls) ;
Here is a show whose minor roles are filled
By barons, viscounts, marquises and earls !
No actresses, not e’en the wives of peers,
May take so much as supers’ roles —a pact
Arrived at, doubtless, for the reason that
No persons are required who cannot act.
This is a “one-day stand,” ho, more, and yet
Before a single syllable resounds
It will have cost (so swears the press) a sum'
Amounting to some twenty million pounds.
What wonder the dramatic scribes are dumb?
For once .the language fails them. There is dearth
Of panegyrics to discharge at this,
The Greatest, Most Stupendous Show on Earth.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.
OTHER TIMES, OTHER “LEADS.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The hero of “The Balkan Princess” is a frivolous
in fact, rakish—character. —News Item.
The hero whom we used to know,
A few short seasons back,
Has vanished like the choral show
Whose heroine was black,
Or brown. A person fills his place
Who is an ethical Disgrace.
No more the leading juvenile
Irradiates the stage, -
With virtue and the holy smile
Which makes the Wicked rage:
Convention now demands that he
Shall be as horrid as can be.
The baritone of yesteryear
Was strangely pure and good,
And, as he sang, a furtive tear
In ev’ry eyeball stood:
Each part he played he deified
And all “in front” were edified.
How different the modern “lead!”
No tuneful Wowser he:
His troubles cause no heart to bleed,
His triumphs bring no glee.
He is not kind and faithful, but
A most incorrigible “nut.”
For me, I speed the parting type,
And welcome in the new;
The old-time hero’s soulful tripe
At last has got its due.
Avaunt, dull effigy of wood!
All hail, blithe youth of flesh and blood!
N.S.W.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
The Rev. Cortie, S.J., now lecturing in
Melbourne on “The Heavens We See,” gives
the old mother-in-law joke another airing
when he talks of comets. A comet, he says,
is “a sort of astronomical mother-in-law
that comes in our system for a short visit
and stays for ever after.” But things have
changed since the mother-in-law became a
“prop” of the comedy-man. Under present
social conditions mother-in-law is mostly a
practical elderly angel who comes to nurse
the baby, or give Ethel a hand with the
housework, and her visits are all too brief.
The Rev. Cortie, S.J., doesn’t know what
it is to welcome the wife’s mother when the
rare, occasional hired girl has gone off in
the inevitable huff.

“Kaylee” : My friend Hikkup, filling in his
census paper, wrote the word “Wowser”
against the query re religion. _As Mr.
Knibbs has not proceeded against him, I infer
that Wowserism is now officially recognised
as a form of irreligion.
There evidently is a shortage of labor in
Australia, with high wages as a consequence.
Thus, in one capital, a “free laborers’ union”
has been formed, and the principal officers
are to get three hundred pieces of gold a
year. Some time ago, for somewhat similar
work, a man was paid in silver.
A peculiar scuffle on the Inky Way has
just closed at Auckland (M.L.). It appears
that a reporter of a weekly paper was, or felt
he was, tabooed by the other scribes to the
extent that they would not sit beside him in
the press-box at the Supreme Court. The
indignant man wrote a paragraph setting
out that his paper was informed that one of
the “cads ... was named Bullen, a fifth-class
flunkey on the Herald, who is said to have
been born a cad, and has remained so ever
since.” There were other amazing remarks
in the paragraph ; but the most amazing fea-
tures of it were (1) that it was printed,
and (2; that the writer got hold of Bullen's
name by mistake. Bullen put a fuse to the
libel law. and demanded £lOO. Then “Moor-
ish Maid” Birch, chief reporter of the
Herald, bore witness that, “in these times,
a journalist was chosen for inclusion on the
staff of the big dailies largely on ac-
count of his character as a gentleman, and
for good address, as well as for his compet-
ency. Consequently to say that a journalist
was a cad, and to publish that statement,
was very likely to block all avenues of em-
ployment to him.” Which so impressed the
jury that it gave a verdict on the spot for
the full amount claimed and expenses.
The threatened storm between Acting-Pre-
mier Holman and Works-Minister Griffith,
of the Ma State, has apparently blown over,
like a trouble last March between Holman
and Carmichael, which threatened to disrupt
the Ministry. When McGowen departed for
Europe his job was divided, Holman being
chalked up as Acting-Premier and Carmi-
chael as Acting-Treasurer. But neither
could decide where McGowen left off being
Premier and became Treasurer, or whether
he wasn’t Treasurer as a general thing and
Premier in his spare time. Anyhow, his
ship wasn’t far outside the harbor before
Holman had squatted in McGowen’s seat in
the Treasury building. Carmichael delicate-
ly hinted that that was his seat, and, finding
hints valueless, seized the furniture with the
first opportunity. Then followed a scramble
as to who could reach the room first in the
mornings, until Holman gave orders that the
Premier’s chair, staff and papers were to be
transported to the Attorney-General’s office
at the other end of Macquarie-street. Con-
sequently, one morning, the astonished clerks
saw their papers, books and desks being
seized by strong men and dumped into drays.
Joining in the procession, all hands trooped
up Macquarie-street, and had got half-way
before Carmichael rallied his forces and pur-
sued the procession with orders to return.
For the next few hours the members of the
Cabinet had their coats off pouring oil on
the storm, and when peace was declared
honors were even. Holman got the letters
patent of the entire Acting-Premiership, Car-
michael got the chair.
“Bark Holden” : Apropos memorials of the
S’African unpleasantness. Sale (Vic.) has
the most appropriate one in the country. It
is the usual fountain; the trickle comes from
a bore whose water is charged with sulphur-
etted hydrogen. The smell is just right.
REASON ENOUGH.
CURIOUS FEMALE : “ Why do you go barefoot , my poor man?”
GENTEEL JIMMY : “ Because , madam , I have not the appliances and means to boot."
The sturdy financial spirit of Scotchbyter-
ianism was displayed the other week in re-
gard to the last will and testament of a Vic-
torian landowner, who went down to the sea
in the bad ship Waratah. From his estate
of £230,000 the ill-fated passenger—Neil
Walter Black —had bequeathed some £8000
to various Scochbyterian religious institu-
tions, and the Vic. Full Court was asked to
decide what interest should be allowed on
those legacies from the date when the will
might have been proved (if the drowning of
Mr. Black had been known as soon as it
occurred) to the time when the money was
forthcoming.
The old row between the N.S.W. branch
of the British Medical Association and the
friendly societies is to be nosed into by one
of the Labor Government’s innumerable Com-
missions. The matter has been taken that
far by a rival Sawbones’ association,_ which
now numbers about 40 members. This asso-
ciation, which is branded “Blackleg” by the
B.M.A., alleges that its members are black-
listed for the sin of taking certain contract
jobs from friendly societies. It doesn’t ap-
pear to be a question of undercutting; any-
how, the new association alleges that some
of the B.M.A. men have taken friendly so-
ciety work for less money than some of the
alleged blacklegs are getting. So far as the
friendly-society people are concerned, the
most serious trouble is that, when the alleged
blackleg is in charge of a case, the B.M.A.
specialist—practically all _ specialists are
B.M.A. men—won’t come in for consulta-
tion. The Government can’t be expected to
have much sympathy for blacklegs, if they
are blacklegs; on the other hand, if it can be
shown that they are not blacklegs, or don’t
deserve to be branded as blacklegs, a lot of
friendly-society men who are good unionists
will feel much more comfortable about em-
ploying them than they do at present.
* * * *
“Jim Ballast,” from the stockyard : —
“Snaffle” (B. 25/5/’ll) says that, when the French
Brigadier quoted the French army remount officer’s
report re the deterioration of the Australian horse
through racing, his friend “urged what he could on
the other side,” hut naturally received in reply a
polite shrug. But what could “Snaffle’s” friend pro-
duce on the other side? The Australian racehorse is
only fit for racing, and nine-tenths of hint is only
fit for six furlongs, with 6st. up—somewhere there-
about, anyway. The whole lot of Australian racers
yarded and sold for utility purposes wouldn’t average
a fiver apiece. We’ve scarcely a champion race in
Australia, which is sufficient proof that the time-
rotten excuse for establishing a new race club—“to
improve the breed of horses in the district” —-is a
lie. And when it comes to considering the iniquity
of pony-racing, words fail, and not even a Frenchman
could possibly be polite.
The French Government has at its stud farms over
1000 thoroughbred stallions, especially bred for
utility. They are substantial in body while being
fine in bone, and are well able to drag or carry
heavyweights over long distances. Against these
“Snaffle” can only show the crossbred roadster, useful
enough truly, but in most cases lacking symmetry,
through the pronounced tendency to “throw” to one
side or the other of its breeding, and fre-
quently in certain parts of its frame only.
Many good breeders declare that no more than
one roadster in seven will prove reasonably sym-
metrical, and a glance at any page of photos., show-
ing “useful remount” types, confirms this statement.
No horse showing such defects could possibly be as
safe and reliable as one bred true to type, nor will
it prove so good a “doer.”
It is up to the State to have a say in horse-breed-
ing, for Private Enterprise, as usual, forgets to be
patriotic. There should be a vet. test throughout
the Commonwealth, the short heavy-handicap busi-
ness should be sat on hard, and pony-racing prohi-
bited. Decent breeders would welcome some legisla-
tion on the matter, for they know the truth as well
as the French officer did.
An army of J.P.’s waited on Acting-Pre-
mier Holman the other day to make a string
of requests with the object of raising its in-
dividual and collective “dignity.” Its main
objects were to acquire for J.P.’s. the right to
sit on the Metropolitan Bench in the ordin-
ary courts, and the right to boss the Chil-
dren’s Courts. Holman spoke kindly to the
deputation, but refused to concede the two
main ponts. The chief argument of the
J.P.’s was that the species in Victoria had
more powers than in N.S.W.; but remember-
ing some of the extraordinary samples of
“J.P.” Justice served up on the Victorian
Bench, that argument was like inviting a
torchlight procession to dance in a powder-
magazine.
The preliminary count of the census fig-
ures gives the population of N.S.W. as
1,648,000, and Victoria 1,315,000. These
figures are a triumph for Knibbs, the Fed-
eral Statistician, whose estimates are much
nearer the mark than the State Statisti-
cians’. In N.S.W. Knibbs is 16,000 out,
while the local Statist’s office, where the mar-
vellous Railway Superannuation Scheme
was hatched, is 53,000 out. But the.,grand
result shows that a large slice of the popu-
lation must have escaped the count. N.S.W..
Vic. and W.A. show among them a total of
60,000 fewer people than even the Common-
wealth Statist expected. There are five ex-
planations: (1) That the inhabitants have
planted their friends, like Butler and Deem-
ing did, (2) that tens of thousands have
stowed away in foreign-going steamers, (3)
that the lost 60,000 are in the other three
States. (4) that the 60,000 never existed,
and (5) that the census-man didn’t count all
the people. And No. 5 seems the most plau-
sible of the lot.
A FRESH DEAL.
[Foe The Bulletin.]
London, Thursday.—The railways are arranging

The cannon boom and the church bells ring;
The piled-up pack are watching the King.
This is the thought each knave has got:
“Wonder if he is a trump or not?”
N.s.w. THE JOKER.
* * * *
“D.” : “Do you not know,” said Mr. (say)
Jones, the Sydney lawyer, to a witness in the
Shearers’ Case, “that you can live for less
than 30s. a week in Sydney?” Of course
Mr. (say) Jones knows. Present writer and
he have the pleasure of lunching together
three or four times a week at a fourpenny
restaurant in Pitt-street; when we have ac-
cumulated a few empty bottles, we trade ’em
away for the price of entrance to a football-
match. The doorkeeper at the prize-fights is
an old schoolmate of (say) Jones’s, and w'hen
the boss isn’t looking he sometimes sneaks
the two of us into a back seat. The doss-house
(say) Jones introduced me to is not half
so bad, when you get used to —-to them.
Summer afternoons him and me have a
pretty good time in the surf, if we can raise
the tram-fares. Jones says he used to
live much cheaper before he made his repu-
tation as a lawyer; in those days his address
after 9 p.m. was the Domain. “I’ve
been on the Bench,” says he; that’s his little
joke. But he’s in steady work as a lawyer
now, and he’s making a good thirty-bob a
week; which (as he says) is plenty for a
man to live on in this town. You only
want to know how to do it; and me and
(say) Jones get along very well, without try-
ing to bleed our poor hard-up employers for
another bob or two.
AS THE SPARK FLIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Riverina and Cooma left Melbourne on W ed-
nesday, the former bound for Fremantle, the latter for
Sydney. On Thursday night, when these boats v ere
nearly 1000 miles apart, with the raging gale be-
tween them, the Cooma signals reached Melbourne
in the teeth of the gale louder than the Riverina sig-
nals with the gale. Melbourne was in a vortex of
storm, the wind howling, and the receiving aerial
dripping like a wet umbrella, yet, notwithstanding it
all, the messages of the boats went through.—Wire-
less operator in Melbourne Aoe.
Black night and a south-west gale;
The wind and the sea at grips;
Now pity them, steam and sail,
The hunted, blundering ships;
Like wallowing.brutes they fight
The furies that lie in wait,
Charging lions of the Bight
And leaping wolves of the Strait.
The rain drips down from the wires ;
The wind makes war on the trees;
Ashore by their glowing fires
Some dream of the hunting seas . . .
What call was that from the gloom
So sudden and faint and crisp ?
Through thunders of death and doom
You have heard the wireless lisp!
From cabin and masthead light
To crest of the seaward hill.
The long spark whispers aright
Whatever its master will:
Last w*rd of the outward bound,
Excuse of the overdue;
The messenger blind gropes round
Till it gets the message through.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

The lady with the horsewhip is still
abroad- Last week she turned up with her
man-taming appliance in a northern M.L.
town, and gave both weal and woe to a
local . storekeeper. According to her hus-
band’s story, the grocer gentleman had made
“certain statements” concerning the lady.
Whereupon: —
She asked me'to allow her to horsewhip him. I
agreed, and in the morning we went together and
bought a . Waitable whip Calling several witnesses
to follow us, my wife and 1 went to the shop and
she administered the premeditated punishment. I
then addressed the crowd.
Apparently the grocer took it lying down.
This merely shows the cunning of Eve. She
never does take .her green-liide to the hard-
faced citizen who will be liable to put her
over his knee and spank her with the thick
end of her argument.
“Vagabond”: The question is not, unfortu-
nately, whether the kilt is a suitable bag for
the body in Australia br not. As long as we
have the present voluntary system of enrol-
ment for adults the Jocks have only to say :
“If you won’t let us wear our kilts or kick-
shaws, we won’t wear a rifle or bayonet, and
be blowed to you!” and Australia must
crawl down. When the country makes up
its mind and plunks down a firm and immov-
able hoof, and says with a loud voice that
every man between 18 and 30 must bear
arms, then it can choose the uniform it
thinks most suitable, and the men will have
to get into it. Under present conditions, if
someone comes along and jolts our country
a rude crack on the jaw, it will have to be
glad to let the crowds wear anything or no-
thing—kilts or shirt-sleeves or surf costumes.
But by the Queensbury Rules of War, any
prisoner taken fighting, or with a rifle con-
cealed about him, and without a uniform,
will not only be liable to, but probably will
be, shot on sight.

“Horatio B.” : Some men contract the S.
M. Herald-Argus trouble very early in life.
I know one who started to say “The time is
not yet ripe” before he was 25. Still it
comes as a shock to see, H. G. Wells writ-
ing things like this: “I am anxious to influ-
ence thinking people between 18 and 30
years of age. There is not much use in try-
ing to get hold of people over 30. When
they have once taken on the burthen of life
they do not want to change.” So a keen
observer like Wells sets the limit; at whicl*
the average human being is willing to re-
ceive fresh ideas as low as 30. It is like
handcuffing Freedom before the lady is pro-
perly awake.
Things look promising for an improvement
in the Melbourne police service. After Chief
Commissioner O’Callaghan’s overcoat had
been stolen from Moonee Valley the other
week, a South Melbourne constable was
robbed of a suite of furniture which he had
placed in a house he was about to occupy.
Now that the inefficency of the force has be-
gun to find it out, and the sins of the system
are being visited on the officers, the way of
the evil-doer is likely to become harder. No-
thing like killing the captain if you want to
get protection for the private! For instance,
there was no talk of aviation regulations un-
til a flying machine fell heavily on the Pre-
mier of France and his late colleague. And
if only an Archbishop would allow him Self to
be chewed by a plague-stricken rat, every
alderman would declare war on the pest
within a week.
* * * *
The Mackintosh, a Highland chieftain who
owns most of Invernesshire, was asked last
month to explain the prevailing Bolt from
Scotland. He said :
The continual advertisement of the advantages of
our colonies and the United States, combined with
School Board education and the unsettled state of
the labor market, as against the high wages and
Protection in the adopted countries, are beginning to
tell.
While he mentioned these obvious factors,
the Mackintosh, as a great landlord, was coy-
ly silent about two little beasts that have
more to answer for than anything else —the
deer and the grouse. There are certain
duties which convention absolutely forces on
the socially-aspiring millionaire —English,
American, S’African or Australian —and one
of them is the purchase of a Scottish deer-
forest or grouse-moor or both. A Jew shoots
over what was once a Royal estate. Yankees
own vast properties like Balmacaan and
Skibo. These and a thousand other “shoots”
must not be cultivated. The crofter may
potter about with a few hens and a sheep or
two, but the heather mustn’t go, the nests
mustn’t be disturbed, and the stag mustn’t
have his native wilds interfered with. As a
result, the arable land at the disposal of the
Scotch yeoman, sturdy and otherwise, is now
nearing vanishing point. Provided the
world’s supply of millionaires keeps up, the
time must come when the country-dwelling
Scot will consist exclusively of (1) the land-
lord (mostly named Cohenstein or Silas K.
Jobson), (2) the ghillie, (3) the beater,
(4) the dog-boy, (5) the dog.
After a legal scuffle between the Whanga-
nui tribes and the Ngatimaniapoto Maoris,
which has lasted for over 14 years, the former
warriors have finally won the title to 4000
acres of totara forest country near Taumar-
anui. The first horsehair referee who was
called in took life easy, and gave each party
a helping; but that was no good to Ilenare or
Pita, and from the resulting appeal Whanga-
nui emerged with the lot. But Ngatiete war-
whooped till a Royal Commission was ap-
pointed, and, after a re-hearing, Whanganui
went into the soup-tureen. When the tribe
climbed out it tore round and appealed some
more, and once again collected the property.
Then the Ngatimaniapotos got their final
chance, and failed to make good. They fore-
saw defeat before judgment was announced,
for their chief witness, in passing back the
Book after being sworn, dropped it on the
floor. From that moment Ngatimaniapoto
knew their case was a coffin.
The owner of a new Sydney hash-house
reckoned that the new sign was a winner,
it caught so many eyes. Then an interfer-
ing person told him what was the matter.
The painter had put it up this way : “Dn-
der < New Management. 3 Coarse Meals,
Gd.” He sniffed in the doorway and de-
cided to let it go at that.
* * * *
‘‘Whaffor” : I recently made a trip up the
Murray from Morgan (S.A.) to Mildura
(Vic.), calling at the prohibition townships
of Waixarie, Loxton, Renrnark and Mil-
dura. The “prohibition” is a farce, or
worse; for this part of the Murray is the
home of the sly-grog seller. Instead of get-
ting a decent drink at a pub, you get poison
from a “bumboat.” Waikarie, which is a
rising agricultural settlement, has no accom-
modation of any sort for travellers. Neither
has Loxton. You have to camp under a tree.
If the Wowsers want prohibition, then why
don’t they erect some sort of dwelling for
visitors looking for land? It’s a scandal.
At Renrnark there is a Gothenberg hotel,
and several clubs. Further up, Mildura is
one of the heaviest drinkers I have yet
struck —in Victoria, at any rate. The Cof-
fee Palace has a wine license, and there is a
club where every sort of grog can be purchased.
Hunting for the sly-groggers, policemen pa-
trol the river dressed as swagmen; but cap-
ture is most difficult. And when there is a
conviction—well, it’s a fine, and then worse
grog still to pay for it.
A French physician has discovered that
smoking promotes, deafness, “by the well-
known effect of nicotine on the vaso-motor
system, which produces vascular hyperten-
sive phenomena which are not compensated
m the cavity of the tympanum.” As soon
as line Bulletin received the news it
threw its old cherrywood down the Wild
Cat shaft, and, rushing round to the chemist
next the Blue Anchor, asked for a remedy.
“Water,” said the druggist laconically, and
went on rolling up his breadcrumbs.
A tram ride in Sydney will never be what
it should be till the doors facing the oppo-
site lines are closed up, and people pre-
vented from riding on the footboards.
Women (usually the short dumpy ones who
are hard to see) persist in getting off the
wrong side, and invariably turn their faces
to the tail of the car, so that in the event
ot a spill they may have the better chance
ot a fractured skull. The killing of one
Holroyd on a recent day was another ex-
ample of the risks passengers take. Two
trams were travelling in opposite directions,
and standing on the inside footboard of each
of them was a passenger. One car was
slowing down, and one of the men went to
dismount without seeing the man on the
other footboard. They collided; and now
one is dead and the other is injured. A
very simple contrivance would enable the
conductor to lock the doors facing the oppo-
site line of rails at the end of each run;
and, if the footboards were hinged, the one
on the closed-door side could be put out of
action, and thus barred from being the hor-
rible menace to life that it is under present
arrangements.
To what use can a Governor be put when
there are no Parliaments or bazaars to
open? Victoria made an effort to solve the
question recently by driving its unemployed
Fuller through the rain-drenched wilds of
Gippsland to Wontliaggi, the coal township,
and there putting into his chilled hands a
locally-baked brick. According to Melbourne
Age, he “turned it over and over, and ap-
peared to be greatly interested.” Well, what
is a cold, polite man to do with a presenta-
tion brick except turn it over and over? He
dare not assume a startled expression, and
declare that it is the most original brick he
lias ever seen. One of the chief virtues of
a good brick is that it is exactly like all
other good bricks. From a brick point of
view, to be excellent is to be ordinary. An
abnormal brick is hateful to a bricklayer.
Brickmalcers, therefore, deliberately strive
after dulness. Hence it is very hard to say
something really graceful about a brick-'-
especially on a wet day at Wontliaggi.

The Vic. Government is convinced that the
official home of its newly imported and to-
tally superfluous Governor is inadequate to>
shelter his entire menage. Wherefore Watt
and his Cabinet are looking for a palace that
will meet the demands of an imitation Court.
So far, no suitable building has been found;
but should efforts fail to locate a dwelling,
it is understood, on the very worst authority,
that the Government will lease the Exhibi-

EARLY CLOSING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wee Willie Judkins is now agitating for the
compulsory closing of pubs at 6 p.m.
From early morn till midnight chime,
We hear the nocent Judkins spout;
There is no limit to the time
W T hen Woodfull rises up to shout;
Though weak men shriek, and strong men yawn,
The tribe may prate from dawn to dawn.
They clamor for the bolted door,
They battle for the shattered cup;
Each wearisome artesian bore
Aspires to put the shutters up—
Apostle of eternal skite,
He shuts up never, day or night.
The time is here! Awake, arise,
Free people, and put forth your best,
To agitate in earnest wise ,
For seven decent days of rest,
And one most necessary pact—
A Judkins Early Closing Act!
VICTORIA.
SNELL.
TWO ROSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This morn they blossomed, side by side,
Two rosebuds, white and red;
To-night one glows in ruddy pride,
And one is lying dead.
The red rose will not, when it dies,
The other’s honor share;
The white pleased best my lady’s eyes,
And decked awhile her hair.
VICTORIA.

ONE SCHOOL AND ANOTHER.
“My dear brother, I have started a school which will meet on the afternoon of every
Sabbath. ”
“No good to me, boss. I got cleaned out last week."
“11.5. M.”: Didn’t I hear some Abo-
fool say that no poisonous snake climbed? Well,
listen. I was out mustering sheep this morning,
and when riding under an old leaning dead tree, a
tiger snake 2ft. 6in. fell and hit my hat. It
landed on the pony’s back behind the saddle, and
wriggled under the pigskin. Luckily, I had a good,
thick cloth, and this no doubt saved the prad. I
slipped off, undid the girth, and slung the saddle
quick and lively. Then I settled accounts with the
cause of the disturbance.
“Kodak”: The grimmest invitation ever
nailed to a post-and-rail fence was the work of an
embittered selector who, after living and toiling
alone for years amongst the glum and wooden-
headed people of Dreary Plains, had been ruined by
the drought. “Them as Wants to Can see me Hang
Mesolf in this Paddick at 3 o’clock Tuesday. No
refreshments provided. I am Sick of this dam hole
and all in it.—-Joe M.” It was on view for a week,
and, although the district made no sign, at 2 o’clock
on the day fixed, all the able-bodied men for miles
round were sitting like crows on the fence of the
“paddick,” in the centre of which had been erected
a rough gallows. At 2.30, amidst dead silence, the
doomed man walked down from the house with an
empty kerosene case and a short length of soaped
rope with a running-noose at one end. Getting up
on the box he affixed it firmly to the cross-beam.
Then he got down, and, standing off a little dis-
tance, eyed it critically. Apparently satisfied, he
went off up to the house again, cutting tobacco and
filling his pipe on the wav. “He’s goin’ t’ have a
lasht shmoke,” croaked an old man, thoughtfully..,
The performer, ere he reached the house, turned and
surveyed the black line of dreary plainsmen on
the grandstand, like the business-manager of an
out-door entertainment. Then he went in, and the
audience settled down to wait. The fatal hour of
3 came and went silently, but the suicide did not
appear. Another half-hour went by without any-
thing happening, A small, impatient boy detached
himself from the crowd and wandered up to the
house. He looked fearfully in the back door, and
observed the desperate insolvent lying on his stretcher
—smoking. “Ain’t yer goin’ t’ hang yerself t’-day,
Joe?” he whined. “They’re all waitin’ for yer.”
The doomed one scratched his chin, and, after a
moment’s, thought, heaved himself off his resting-
place, and extracted from a cupboard a hammer,
two long nails and an immense bit of battered card-
board. “Nail that there cardboard t’ th’ gallers,
an’ mind yer get it right side up,” he drawled, and
resumed his horizontal meditations. The awe-struck
lad hurried out to do his bidding, and the “paddick”
soon resounded to the vigorous clouts of the hammer.
When the job was complete and he stepped aside,
the Dreary Plainsmen read in large black letters
the words “Sports Postponed.”
There are healthier ways of killing a dog
than drowning him, as a Timaru (M.L.) man is pre-
pared to solemnly take oath. AVhen the death sen-
tence was passed on his old retriever, the man procured
a light row-boat, the dog, a piece of rope and a good-
sized rock. With these implements he proceeded to
the deep water of the harbor, hitched his mongrel
to the stone and threw the combination overboard.
But the stone slipped from its noose, and the animal
started to clamber back into the boat. The owner
resented this so energetically that he fell overboard
himself, and when a passing craft rescued him he
was just reaching for the bell.St Death’s door, while
the dog was sunning himself on the gunwale of the
boat and yelping loud applause at his master’s dying
struggles. -
“Yalgun” contradicts all hands:—
This howl against child-slavery on cow farms
arises from foolish imagination. The kiddies in
reality have the foundation laid of industrious,
sober, thrifty lives, and they enjoy it. Milking,
feeding ,fowls, lighting the fire are nothing laborious.
Wang (B. 25/5/ 11) would have young girls on
dairy farms spend their time sleeping and dolling
their hair instead of learning to milk and rear calves
and manage a home. As for the average State school-
boy, whether it is his fault or the fault of the edu-
cation system I don t know, but it is seldom you
meet one worth his breakfast.

“Border View, 1v.C.,” for the defendant: —
[ beg leave to get a word in edgeways in favor
of the cow cocky, particularly on the count that he
looks upon his women folk as mere milking-
machines. “Curfew” (B. 11/5/’ll) and others like
him are either exhibiting cheap wit on that subject,
or they are extremely ignorant of the respect most
of these young eockies have for women. Very many
of them, when they get married, will not have their
wives in the milking-yard at all if they can help it.
Certainly, numbers of married women have to milk,
but to say that, and pin a charge upon it that the
dairy farmer is callous to the greatest of the natural
feelings, is an unfair assault on the instincts of the
native-born settler and the credulity of the public.

"C.D.0.” : Re blackfellow humorists.
Jacky Bundaburra, buried somewhere on the Lachlan,
figures in more Western jokes than most drovers
remember. Caught with a bottle of beer under his
shirt, he replied with a wink to the sergeant’s
question as to where he gathered it, “What! You
think I put ol’ Missis Dunn away! ” He was
nabbed “on the premises” one Sunday, and ex-
plained it: “Well, I walk alonga camp to Forbes,
t’at four mile; get lost li’ll’ while, ’nother mile;
now walk alono-a camp, four mile; get lost li’ll’
while, ’nother mile—lo mile altogether; Jacky
traveller then, same white pfeller. Mine’s pinter-
beer.” Jack was awarded a month hard by Butler,
P.M. “Can’t do it,” he announced from the dock.
“Mus’ get out Burrawang nex’ Monday for the
shearin’; or, by cripe, ol’ Misser Edols kick up plurry
row.” He got off.
“L. 8.” The 36ft. shark recently caught at
the Chatham Islands, though of unusual length,
may be swallowed without salt. For many years
the whalemen at Bampton Shoals, Chesterfield Reefs,
and thereabouts, were used to the sight of 40ft.'
sharks—few in number, certainly. Two or three
of these big fellows would sometimes tackle a calf
whale and make short work of it. The Polynesian
native when in the water does not fear these mon-
sters as much as the short, thick shark of, say, Bft.
to 10ft. (and he is not much scared of these except
it is night time and raining), for a very lengthy
brute is somewhat slow in' slewing, and can be
easily dodged by a good swimmer with plenty of
nerve. Probably the largest of all the shark
family, known to exist nowadays is the harmless
“bone” shark, so named because it has its mouth
fitted with plates of baleen; nevertheless it is a
true shark. Many whalemen assert that they have
seen these creatures over 50ft. long, basking on the
surface.
“Leo Lear” delivers a young lecture :-
Various writers have got into a bog re Australia':
mound-building birds. There are three of them
• —the scrub fowl ( Megapodius duperreyi), the scrub
turkey ( Catheturus lathanii ), and the mallee fowl
(Lipoa ocellata). The scrub fowl inhabits the tropi-
cal scrubs of Queensland, the Territory and W.A.,
and also the islands that fringe the coast. They put
more material into their nests than any other bird in
the wofld, but don’t do it all in one year. Starting
from a small mound in the first year, they add to
the heap till it approaches 10ft. in height and 28ft.
diameter at base. The nests are seldom far from
tidal waters, and are biggest near C. York. The
second bird-navvy is also a northern inhabitant, and
is not unlike the domestic turkey, though smaller,
and makes a good dinner. The nests are not as large
as those of Megapodius, seldom being over 3ft. high.
These incubators contain much decayed vegetation;
also snakes (not decayed). The mallee fowl pervades
mallee scrub, but is rapicjly disappearing inside the
imported fox. The fowls’ mounds are about the same
size as those of Catheturus, and contain a lower com-
partment of hot, wet, decaying leaves, over which the
eggs are placed. The young mallee chicks, unlike
their cousins, cannot usually dig themselves out to
daylight. But, as the parents have a habit of open-
ing up the nursery to air the blankets every morn-
ing, any youngster that is ready packs his swag and
takes to the scrub. A number of points are common
to all earth constructors. The eggs are laid at day-
break, at intervals of two or three days, and are
placed on end, small-ways down ; the temperature of
the nest is always close handy to 95 ; only one pair
of birds construct, lay, and give general attendance
to each nest; all specimens are shy, and seldom fly
unless disturbed, though at night they camp in high
trees. They share with emus the distinction of being
in the lowest order of our bird-life.
* * * *
“Sliprail” : The Wimmera Plains and Mal-
lee country (Vic.), the land of wheat, has been
scourged with another mice plague, the fourth in 12
years. The pests overrun house and farm, and
will tackle anything from a leg of mutton to a
marriage certificate. They play havoc with stacks
of hay and wheat, and pick the grain from the soil
as it sprouts. This season’s visitors developed a
disease, which shows in a white spot just above
the nose. Men working in mousey hay or corn con-
tract this plague, which breaks out upon the skin
like ringworm or eczema, generally on the hands or
face. A remarkable feature of the invasion is that
when the mice descend upon a district in hordes
they are fully grown, with splendid molars, whereas
if they were born of the flesh they should be in
all stages from the frolicsome youth to the toothless
veteran. Can any Bulletin naturalist explain the
peculiarity ?
WHEN THE JIM-JAMS NEST AGAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Night rolls on through scarlet hours and np one
heeds my yell,
For all are dead, including the marines ;
And I am here alive in til's menagerie of hell —
A life that slipped between the shifting screens.
The hang-faced fiend who welcomed me was blood to
elbow-bend —
The local butcher said his name was Cain.
He put me up at his own pub: “No charge,” lie
said, “my friend ;
We’ll square up ere the jim-jams nest again.”
lie handed me a blazing snake to light me up the
stairs;
A blue-winged vampire took me bv the hand ;
Blotched bull-dog slugs and centipedes marched on
ahead in pairs,
And wild blood-blisters danced a saraband.
We turned into the private bar; the boss, an octo-
pus,
Was feeding frogs with drops of molten chain.
“A quart of rum,” I cried to him, “and instantane-
ous—
I’ll pay you when the jim-jams nest again.”
At that his eye dropped on the floor, and, as he
stooped for it,
A stream of dead men’s teeth poured through the
hole ;
And some were sharp, and some were white, and
some were black and split—-
A green eel placed them all within a bowl,
And passed them to the octopus, who dropped them
one by one
A-down the gaping eyelet through his brain.
“The rum,” he snarled, and turned to me, “is far
too old to run;
Don’t hunt it till the jim-jams nest again.”
So here I toss upon my bed of cold, pulsating
snakes,
And watch the lampreys slime along the walls;
A mouthless devil combs my brain and raises it in
flakes;
Upon my limbs the hairy spider crawls.
Far oft' are rivers filled with beer, wide crystal
streams 'of .gin,
And when the snakes are dead, the devil slain,
I’ll take the shortest track to them, and soak my
burning skin —-
In the season when the jim-jams nest again.
N.S.W.

“Konini”: Can’t say anything discredit-
able about the Australian cuckoo, but the Maoriland
specimen, known as pipiwharauroa, ought to be
warned off the course. My particular objection to
him is that he is rapidly helping to put the sweet-
tongued riro riro out of existence. This little chap
builds a hanging residence with an open doorway,
and on returning in the evening from the musical
matinGe he as often as not discovers an egg in the
parlor which has apparently laid itself. As a
matter of fact, the egg was put there by a loafing
cuckoo, and, on being hatched, the gluttonous young
one wears out the life of the small riros in their
struggle to keep it in food. Finally, the growing-
stranger forces the gentle riros out of the home alto-
gether, and adds insult to injury by turning the bal-
cony into a gay bachelor’s flat, where all the cuckoo
chorus girls do high-kicking and other pastimes.
Then what the Wowser calls “the evidences of their
shame” are deposited in riros’ domiciles, and
so the process of extinction goes on.

“Jobber”: I want to start a society for
the suppression of the fiend who answers “That’s
right” to every remark, whether it’s right or not.
It gets on my nerves. There’s one here now, and
I’ll start on him as soon as you issue a license. This
is the sort of thing I’ve been having for the past
half-hour:—
Him: What sorter place is Queensland?
Me: Good place.
Him: That’s right. Can a bloke get a job easy ?
Me: Whips of work up North.
Him: That’s right. What sorter hours and pay?
Me: Good —in places.
Him: ’s right.
Me: If you go in crushing time you can knock out
a good cheque easy.
Him: ’s right.
And so on. Now, am I justified in outing the cow?
If you think it’s a fair thing I’ll get busy at once,
and life will run a good deal more smoothly for me.

“B. Nana” : Anybody who desires to keep
blowflies from a horse’s injured leg or body need
only apply a little turps, and the inhabitants will
leave immediately. Next, wash the affected part
with warm water and common soap—give it plenty
of wash. Finally, paint the wound over with a
mixture of Stockholm tar and oil (castor or
olive), one of oil to three of tar. I guarantee this
lot to abolish, not only the blowing pest, but every
other winged reptile.
“ Well, and what are you doing on my property?”
"'Sense me, guvnor, I fell out er my atroplane.”
“Eddyson”: Does the bush breed eccen-
trics, or do eccentrics take to the bush ? My ex-
perience of the wilds was not extensive, but I met
dozens of men who were beyond normal. Bates was
only an aggravation of a common type. His weak-
ness was for passionate and ravenous animals. His
dog, a cross between plain. bull and bull-terrier,
was the most vicious brute I have ever encountered,
not excepting zoo tigers and circus bears. It
never got loose without killing something, and
had to be muzzled to keep it from chewing up its
own home. Bates’s cat was a snarling beast,
with electric hair and the eyes of a venomous
snake. His magpie was a danger to every bare-
legged child within a mile, and he had a gander
that would attack intruders with the vehemence
of a spiteful terrier, in which undertaking it was
ably seconded by a game rooster that was always
prepared to fight anything from a cockroach to a
cow. Even Bates’s horse would bite, and his wife
was the most truculent female in five townships.
The tale told was that he fell in love with her
after seeing her beat the life half out of a Chinese
cook in a hotel kitchen. He proposed to her the
same night, whereupon she beat him. “My Lord,”
he said, “what a woman for Indian hawkers!’* And
he never rested till he had married her.
* * * *
“Montalex”: If I didn’t know Randolph
Bedford personally, I should say that he was
“wrathy” when he wrote last. And, the moment
that tone turns up in a discussion, the matter had
better be quietly dropped. Dialectical victory, should it
remain with me, would be too dearly purchased by
any diminution of an old-standing friendship. But
to “S. Q. Whyffe” (B. l/6/’ll) let me say that I
didn’t “contradict Bedford about the changing habits
of the English oak in Australia.” I admitted that
the leafing-habit of the tree had altered with cli-
matic conditions, but I denied that it would in
time become an evergreen. I do not know what
“S.Q.W.” means by “different kinds.” Quercus
robur is a well-marked and homogeneous species,
and any apparent “variety” must be a distinct spe-
cies, entitled to the label of Qwercus-something-else.
As a naturalist, I often find myself much hampered
in these friendly discussions by the current confu-
sion of ideas about the distinction between Family,
Genus, Species and Variety. All these are included
in an Order, which is itself a constituent of a Class,
which in turn forms part of the fundamental divi-
sion, the Branch.
* * *
“Pestle”: About “Tramp’s” advice (B.
11/5/Tl) to apply collodion to a horse’s sore leg.
Wonder he did not recommend court plaster or
goldbeater’s skin. Another horse physician orders
lysol, eucalyptus, etc. All bunkum. What’s the
matter with red gum ? Found anywhere in the
bush. Collect it off the stem of the tree, dissolve
it in hot water (as much as the water will take
up), cool, and apply to the wound. Red gum is
the finest healer and antiseptic in the world—and I
say it after handling scores of antiseptics for my
bread-and-dripping.
“Condamine”: Up here (Moonie Scrub,
Q.) “scrub-dashing” is not a lost art, and it is
worth watching —the gallop after the escaped steer,
the throw from the tail, the strapping of the hind
legs, all in about 11 minutes. The “coachers”
(quiet cattle) are then brought up, and an attempt
made to drive the captured scrubber along with
them. Should he break away again, he is once more
captured, a bell is put on him, and he is let go.
The instant he realises the presence of the bell he
makes a Dee-line for the mob, and never willingly
leaves it while the musical instrument remains at-
tached. It is a fine sight to see a powerful horse
dashing through the scrub, winding snake-like be-
tween the trees, the rider swinging from side to side
in the saddle to avoid the apparently inevitable
bump. The well-trained horse takes his own course,
and to make any attempt to guide him is to court
disaster. The conditions here are all totally dif-
ferent from those in the comparatively open foiest
ranges of the Upper Murray and Mitta. There the
chief requirements are a sure-footed horse and un-
limited pluck. Here a capacity for quick decision,
amounting to an instinct, is the indispensable thing.
» * * *
“E.P.” : I’m beginning to think there are
some possibilities about that ancient tale of Jonah
and the great fish, after an experience I had recently.
We had just bagged a gohanna, the length of which
a surveyor swore by all his gods was five cubits,
otherwise 7ft. 6in. During the post-mortem the
coroner discovered a full-sized kangaroo-rat, without
a hair turned, and with not a bone broken. While
we gazed with wonder on this phenomenon, the
blessed rat sat up, took a few gasps, and, recovering
his wind, hopped away, and is, I believe, going yet.
I once saw a rat hop out of a tied sheaf of hay after
going through a reaper and binder, but his experi-
ence was mild compared with the kangaroo-rat’s.
* * * *
“Billea”: A Cow-country Jay Pee reached
ibe sublimest heights the ether day when, having
heard all the evidence, he puckered up his forehead,
cleared his throat, seized his pen and announced,
“Sentenced to ss. hard labor in Gaol.” In
the same cow-afflicted parts an unstipended magis-
trate had a relative who was one of the local Bungs.
When a drunk was hauled in, the Beneh always
asked the constable the name of the hotel the man
was arrested from. If the poor wretch came from an
opposition shanty, 10s. or 24 hours’ was handed over
while 23. 6d. or ss. or the rising was sufficient if
he had had the good fortune to get tangled at the
right house. Writer has known the J.P. or his Bung
relative to pay the fine, so that the drunk might get
away while he was still parched, and “do in” the
rest of his cheque.

“North Cowst” : The half the grim story
of the Cow districts has not yet been told. Living
in this district is a dairy farmer reputedly worth
over £20,000. He works his dairy with machines
and his children. He does no work himself. The
census collector called at his home at a
quarter to 1 p.m. While the collector was
there the mother told the children to go
and get their breakfast. These children (some
of them grown up) have never been into
the neighboring towns. No riding horse or vehicle
is kept on the farm. No paper is taken in. Topsy’s
ignorance is deep learning compared with what
may be seen within cooee of a State school. The
kids spend from 10 to 12 hours every day in the cow
yards. This is not literature, but bald-headed fact.
“Matelot” : The 22ft. boa just acquired by
the Sydney Zoo from the Northern Territory, is
spoken of as the largest snake yet taken or killed
in Australia. A mistake. Ferdinand Sachs, ex-
bank-manager at Townsville, and once well known
in Sydney, shot one near Mourilyan measuring over
24ft. It was stuffed in Sydney, and Sachs after-
wards had it in his chambers in London. In the
curing process the skin shrank 18in. The Malayan
trepang fishers on the Northern Territory coast, it
is said (and there is no reason to doubt the story),
captured one of 28ift. It went to Sourabaya, and
from there to a museum or zoo in Holland. Apropos
of the ever-interesting creature that was the cause
of Eve’s giddiness, a late Kingston (Jamaica) paper
states that the deadly fer-de-lance has made its re-
appearance in Trinidad, where it has not been seen
for nearly 40 years. Martinique, St. Vincent and
Trinidad are the only islands in the West Indies
where this reptile has been found, though they are
plentiful enough on the South American mainland.
Some writers (who don’t know) confound the fer-de-
lanc-e with the small and slender coral snake of South
America and Central America, which is no larger
than our “bandy-bandy”—at least, so far as the
writer’s knowledge goes, and he has seen scores of
all three snakes, alive. The fer-de-lance is a com-
paratively big reptile—up to 4ft. and sft. Its head
is very like that of the copperhead, but broader.
A very much bigger snake of those parts is the
eamoudie, or water-snake of Demerara; it reaches
25ft., is as thick as a man’s thigh, and is highly
prized as a delicacy, not only by the Chinese resi-
dents, but by Europeans as well.
“Left Hand”: I don’t expect you to
believe this, but it is true. Two people were sitting
on an Adelaide tram. One asked the other to guess
the number of his ticket, arid, nearly burst with
astonishment when the latter said, without thinking,
27,286, or something like that, which was correct.
Then the guesser said: “Let’s take that ticket in
Tatt.’s!” But the owner of the tram ticket said,
“What’s the use!” and the matter dropped. When
Tatt.’s next result-slip came out this number had a
prize.
“Twigg”: The other day I noticed a
seemingly dead caterpillar being taken home for
dinner by six or eight little black ants, at the rate
of about 1.6 inches per 10 minutes. Arriving at
the front, door of their residence they unhitched from
the grub, and were wiping the perspiration from
their muscles when the tucker commenced to
wriggle. At this stage the Depart, for Home Affairs,
which had evidently been expecting delivery, tumbled
up from below, and, finding that the game needed
further killing, immediately commenced to finish it
off by dragging two pebbles, about - the size of
parched peas, across its equator. Now, what I want
to know is—Were those pebbles kept handy for that
particular purpose?

“Maranoa”: Has anybody ever taken
notice how long bees will camp without luncn?
One swarm that clustered on a dry tree in my
paddock stayed 17 days without shifting.
