“Roy W.”: Rangi rushed into Jackson’s
bar late in the afternoon and demanded a bottle of
brandy—“Te missus very bad ; she get the piccan-
inny. I pay all right.” Jackson shook his head.
“You always say ‘I pay,’ and that is the end of it.”
“But I pay you true, Jackson, this time, you tc
good man. My missus komati; she die!” Jackson
hesitated. “Well, I’m d—d if I want it to be said
that I refused any sick woman, brown or white, a
drop of spirits, but it’ll be the last straw you’ll
pile on my back. When is the piccaninny ex-
pected?” Rangi made for the door and unhitched and
mounted his hack, before he replied, “Oh, I dunno,
Jackson; I only marry te missus las’ week.”

THE COUNTRY OF BOUNDERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The coach was creaking up the hill, the straining
nags were nodding ;
Bi-weekly thus, upon its route, the Long Hill mail
went plodding;
The sunburnt driver seemed oppressed; he stared
ahead and muttered ;
Behind, unto a suff’ring friend, a damphool brayed
and stuttered.
“Bai Jove!”—amid the tall, hot gums the frightful
oath went pealing—
“ The boundahs in this country hyah don’t go in for
straight dealing !
That Land Tax now ! Bai Jove, you know, a chap
with money fioundahs!
One never knows what one’s about with these dashed
Labah boundahs!”
He brayed of “boundahs” all the way, no spur or
answer needing.
The driver waited till he saw a mob of grey ’roos
feeding;
With one wild whoop he sent them off in mighty
leaps astounding,
Then drawled, “Hey, Boss! them blankers there is
native ‘boundahs’ bounding!”
N.S.W.
E. O’F.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Anglican Bishop Mercer has consented to
be the chairman of Tasmania’s first Wages
Board. It will deal with the boot-trade.
A three-line par. in a London paper men-
tions the death of Lady Harberton. Fifteen
years ago the event would have been starred.
Deceased was the pioneer of bloomers. In
the ’9o’s, when she was blazing the lonely
track of trOusers-for-women, she was the ter-
ror of every genteel pub within biking dis-
tance of London. Her arrival in “rational
costume,” on a bike, caused the Rabble to
gather and jeer, what time the Upper Classes
fled, blushing hotly, through the yard. Var-
ious hotelkeepers refused to serve her,
whereupon she prosecuted them. Woman-
kind, after scrutinising her blue serge pants
and woollen hose, declared enthusiastically
for its old uniform; so Lady Harberton will
go down to posterity with the multitude of
reformers who bit off more than they could
chew.
W. J. Denny, S. Australia’s Attorney-
General, was originally weather clerk in Ade-
laide G.P.O. Then Jim Anderson, recently
K.C.’d, offered him his articles, and Denny
made a rapid climb. Recent shrewd mining
and other investments, together with a big
practice, are believed to have made him one
of the wealthier men of the Labor- party.

wang (N.S.W.). It was Mulhall who dis-
covered the remains of the victim done to
death in the Black Ranges by the notorious
Butler. The old man, who had put 77 years
behind him, had been in the Lachlan country
since the earliest bushranging ’so’s.

Thomas Talbot Wilton, who crossed over
a few days back, was for years head of the
Western (N.S.W.) Coal Combine. In his
younger days he was connected with the
Bathurst Times, and handled a scarifying
pen. He fell foul once of Edmund Webb,
M.L.A., and subsequently M.L.G., the rich
grocer of Bathurst. Webb had him gaoled,
but after a week or so relented, and sent his
carriage to the gates to fetch the vitriolic
scribbler away.
A Colonel Garibaldi lately distinguished
aimself as an officer in the insurgent Mexi-
can “army.” This Garibaldi is an Australian,
born at Brighton (Vic.), what time his
father, Ricciotti, son of the red-shirted
Italian, was a truant officer of the ATc. Edu-
cation Department. Ricciotti was also for a
time a clerk in the Melbourne Treasury. His
son, the Mexican Colonel, is a fire-eater like
grandpa, and, before going to Mexico, had
fought in Greece, S’Africa and Venezuela.

From a Roman journal:—
His Excellency the Prime Minister of Australia
(Mr. Fisher) arrives to-night in Rome with his suite,
which is composed of 17 people, en route for the

have been engaged for his Excellency at the Hotel

Seventeen people! The accuracy of this for-
eign intelligence!
Dan O’Connor, as the world knows, was
erstwhile N.S.W. Postmaster-General —the
Ministerial office generally given to the fool
of the family. He was, in Wordsworth’s
phrase, “a noticeable man with large grey
eyes.” One day he tendered the barmaid
half a sovereign, and forgot his change. Re-
turning from the doorway, however, he asked
Hebe for the money. She sighed, and, as
she slowly opened the till, gazed into the
translucent orbs of the illoquint Tribune and
said: “Pardon me, Mr.
darling, I
mean —but when you look
at me with those heavenly
eyes, how can I possibly
have a thought of sordid
things V” She kept that
change, of course. There
is a poetic and probably un-
true explanation of how
Dan became possessed of
those eyes. When in prac-
tice as a butcher in Wool-
loomooloo he was in the
habit of killing calves in his
backyard, and as the stuck
calf slowly passed away, it would fix its
long-lashed eyes in a reproachful, glassy
stare upon its assassin. Dan endured and
returned the stares of so many dying calves
that his eyes grew to he just like his vic-
tims’ —and his love, added his enemies, just
like calf’s love.

tions, died on the lltli inst., at Warragul,
from a smashing-up received a few days
previously in a football match at Moe.
Though only 25, McCarthy had twice stood
for Parliament, once for St. Ivilda and once
for Prahran. At the P’ran poll the shabby-
genteelers rolled up and voted against him
to a man, chiefly for the reason that at the
time of his candidature he Was employed as
a milk-carter. It is a dreadful thing to earn
an honest living.
Ex-Justice Hartley Williams is back in
Victoria. He has lately been in Eastern
Asia, and tells Australia not to be fool
enough to believe the yarns that Japan
doesn’t mean serious business. By the way,
Williams’s son is now CJ. Madden’s asso-
ciate- —which shows how well the hatchet has
been buried. The idea with Sliiels no
doubt was that Williams was too Demo-
cratic for the lift that he wanted, and that
the showier Madden got. Williams was bit-
terly chagrined, and took his pension as soon
as he could. His father was also Judge
Williams, one of Melbourne’s earliest barris-
ters, along with Stawell, Barry, Pohlman
and Croke.
A tale of the Long Arm: —
Years ago, when a mail-line ran from Australia
to Europe via Panama, the steamers touched at
Pitcairn Island to coal. On one occasion, after
the vessel had been loading for some hours, and her
decks were much begrimed, a nattily-appointed
whaleboat came off to land those passengers who ,
wished to see the island. Among these was a tall,
sparely-built gentleman with classically-chiselled,
features and of commanding mien, who, when asked
with the rest of the visitors not to step on to
the whaler’s speCkless white seats while wearing
boots blackened with coal-dust, did (perhaps ab-
sent-mindedly) exactly the opposite of what was
requested. Anyhow, the captain of the boat told
him that he was “no gentleman,” and said he would
mueh like to meet him man-to-man on shore. The
visitors landed and strolled about the historic island,
and when they returned to re-embarlc they found a
crowd assembled on the beach, forming a ring in
which two strong and skilful boxers, stripped to the
waist, were engaged in fierce fight. These men
were the whaleboat’s captain—a splendidly set-up.
thick-set fellow of
the seaman type—
and liis recalcitrant
military - looking
passenger. The lat-
ter, though he made
a valorous resist-
ance, was fairly van-
quished after bat-
tling with his heav-
ier opponent for an
hour and a-half. The
vanquished then
found that the vie-
to r was Fletcher
Christian, grandson
of the chief of the
Bounty mutineers,
and the victor found
that the vanquished
was no less than
Lieutenant Bli g h,
grandson of the
Bounty’s brave but
tyrannical comman-
der. Lieut. Bligh
still lives at Parra-
matta (he is often
seen in Manly) at a
venerable age ; at 80
he stands erect, and
is in appearance and
deportment as commanding and resolute' as when he
refought the old battle in the third generation.
Dr. Pomare, chief native health officer to
the M.L. Dovernmeait, after 10 years’ useful
work, has received notice that his depart-
ment is to be reconstructed out of existence.
So Dr. Pomare, revives an old intention of
becoming an M.P. for a Maori constituency,
and has let Pfenare Kaihau, the sitting mem-
ber for the Western District, understand (hat
there will be trouble in his vicinity next
election.
One of the three N.S.W. detectives who
helped to look after George V. when he was
touring Australia as the Duke of York was
Roeliaix, just retiring. And one of the
things that struck Rochaix concerning
George was his worried look when, with the
Duchess May, he went walking or riding in
the carriage. “No, no,” says the French-
man ; “I have no theory—only I think when
the Duke do not keep in step when they
walk he meet trouble half-way. I used to
say to myself: ‘Roc-haix, I would rather be
a policeman than a Prince so sad-looking.’ ”
Henry William Northcroft, another vet-
eran of the Maori wars, was decorated a
few days ago at Auckland with the M.L.
Cross by Governor Islington. For 10 years
Northcroft gave black brother a taste of
what Sheol is likely to be, and on three occa-
sions showed conspicuous bravery carrying
wounded comrades to safety. When peace
was finally declared, and the only danger to
the country was the pawnshop man, North-
croft took up the law and parcelled it
out from the magisterial bench in the Wai-
kato, Thames and Auckland.
Colonel Thomas Porter, of Gisborne
(M.L.), who was handed an Auxiliary
Forces’ “decoration” the other day, is an old
trader in gore. He started his apprentice-
ship with a midshipman’s knife over 50
years ago on the China Station, and early
showed a fine aptitude for carving pirates.
Subsequently he joined the 70th regiment in
Maoriland, serving with it for three years.
Then he became a cavalryman, and pursued
the Maori with deliberate intent. For 20
years he was more or less engaged in this
meat business, and received £lOOO reward
for the capture of that black hyena, Kereopa
Kaiwhatu, whose murder of the missionary
Volkner in 1865 is still a shuddersome mem-
ory. For a while, Porter was native land
purchaser, but when the Boer war was in
progress he sharpened up his old implements
and went after more meat at the head of a
M.L. contingent. For that he was handed a
C.B.—which may or may not have been a
delicate way of suggesting that the trouble
was all over Cohenstein's Cash Balance.
SENATOR ARTHUR RAE (N.S. W.).
“There seems to be a disposition here tv-
make something of a lion of Mr. Blank,”
says a London correspondent re a more or
less distinguished Australian Coronator: —
The process is a sorry one —
We’ve often seen it worked before.
We’yfe heard our poor Australian son
Put up his imitation roar;
They lionise him, but, alas,
Too oft they send him back an ass!
N.S.W. Premier McGowen is represented
in England as an Englishman. As a mat-
ter of fact, he was born at sea; but as
the incident happened aboard a vessel sail-
ing from the port of London, his arrival
was registered as if he had been born in
Stepney. This gives an extra Englishman
to the list of N.S.W. Premiers. The com-
plete tally now stands: Englishmen, 7
(Donaldson, Parker, Cowper, Robertson,
Parke's, See and McGowen) ; N.S.W., 4
(Farnell, Dibbs, Carruthers and Wade) ;
Ireland, 3 (Martin, Jennings and Wad-
dell) ; Scotland, 2 (Stuart and Reid).
Concerning Australia’s politest criminal-
catcher :
N.S.W. detective Rochaix has retired after 29
years’ service. Practically ever since he was born in
London, in 1851, the light-hearted Frenchman has
made adventure his daily meal and excitement its
sauce. He was in Paris when the Franco-German
war broke out, and saw the soldiers leaving “for Ber-
lin” ; enlisted, and to-day carries a medal for ser-
vices rendered to France in ’7l ; was back in Paris
when the Commune was starting to get in its fine
work, with all the ruffians of Europe putting a shoul-
der to the wheel; escaped from Paris ; went to Lon-
don and worked for three years in a lace factory;
sailed for Brisbane to gain health ; winked on a sugar
experimental station for Police Magistrate Day (so
Rochaix started with one Day in Australia and
finished with another) ; moved on by easy stages all
over Queensland ; sailed for the South Seas, and was
one of the crew of a “recruiting” vessel; returned
to Queensland and made up to the Gulf of Carpen-
taria ; then down to Sydney and worked with a
pick and shovel. It was while engaged at that harm-
less pursuit that the French Consul recommended
Rochaix to Superintendent Fosbery for the dangerous
post of tracker-down of escapees from New Cale-
donia, who were both numerous and wickedly busy
in Sydney at the time. From then until last week
Rochaix has sent dozens and dozens of escaped birds
back to New Caledonia, and in Sydney has had a hand
in other fine work calling for patience and brains —
mostly in connection with foreign criminals. It is
not his intention to spend the rest of his life at
Noumea. “Australia,” he says, “will be what you
call the good enough for me.”
A VISION OF KINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETION.]
Blare of trumpets and drums that beat —
To the right and left they swings—
And the years bend low at the mercy-seat
Of a crowned and sceptred king.
Tall years that hearken to do his will,
Proud years that shall tread him down ;
His name with theirs shall the pageant fill,
Till another shall wear his crown.
Another, another! The quick feet fly.
It is part of the measure they tread —
The kings that fall, and the years that die —
A dance of the crowned and dead.
A pageant of kings ’mid the files of time,
Tall kings that were bearded and strange,
Moving with measured steps that rhyme
To the heedless words of, change.
Conqueror, warrior, statesman, clown,
They march in their purple show,
Till the breaking sceptre and falling crown,
Are lost where the dead years go.
I saw them pass in their long array
By the mountain pillars of night,
Where my eyes in the midst of worlds at play
Were smitten with wonder-sight.
80, the first William! ’Tis Scnlac’s pride
That thrills where the harpers tell
Of the fougliten field where the Saxon died
And the dauntless Godwin fell.
Conqueror, plumed with the spoils of might
That the Doomsday records keep,
A flutter of years, and then the night—
Red havoc, and long, long sleep.
Deep in the covert the king’s deer hides —
Twang on the hard-strung bow—
Blow on the horn where the hunter rides;
But how should a dead king blow?
Book and sceptre 1 The scholar rules,
Heart-pierced with a father’s griefo—
Oh, shadows that war on kings and fools,
Throw dusk on the falling leaf!
Crown and sceptre, and sword and lance,
And fields with the red dew wet,
Where over the pages of large romance
Is the name Plantagenet!
lion hearts of the lost crusades,
And the roses red and white —
Henry or Edward —the long light fades
At the gauntlet challenge of night;
While a lordlier trumpet brays the fame
On the flying years’ short breath
Of the portly Harry, or shrills the name
Of the crowned Elizabeth.
Till the slight and courtly Stuart comes
To carry a sceptre thin,
As the fated years with their throbbing drums
Welcome the new years in.
And still to the silver fiddle of God
The feet of the dancers flew,
In the measure the kings and swift years trod
On the bridge of the old and new.
I saw them pass in their long array
With their lords of the Parliament,
’Mid the wonder-light of the worlds at play,
By the way that the old kings went.
Ah, was it a dance of the years I saw
Or the leaves from the forest shorn,
Where the gusts of the growing winter draw
The weird grotesques of morn ?
Long live the king! And who lingers long
In place, or in faith or fame?
Will the young years pause to sing one song
O’er the grave of the last king’s name?
Pomp of purple on robes of night—
One king shall have rule o’er all,
When the lhaves that yesteryear were bright
Are sere at the cold wind’s call.
Long live the king! And the thunders pass—-
Airs charged with an empty breath —
While the silent shadows lift the glass
To toast the one king—Death !
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.

This little bank has put itself in a better position
than it was 12 or even six months ago. its coin
and other liquid assets were then too small in pro-
portion to its liabilities. The comparison which fol-
lows shows, among other things, that liquid assets
have been substantially increased, partly by reason
of increased deposits, partly by reason of reduced
advances: —
May, TO. May, ’ll.
Notes in circulation.. £64.601 £07,139 Inc. £2,478
Bills, etc 14,247 12,477 Dec. 1,770
D'po-its at interest.. 267,065 268,024 Inc. 959
Depo-its, free.. .. 294,262 321,206 Inc. 26,944
Liquid assets .. .. 201,891 246,035 Inc. 44,144
Advances .. .. 621,921 611,313 Dec. 10,608
Premises, etc.:. .. 27,528 27,479 Dec. 49
fhe total debt to the public is now £668,846; and
of that amount 36 p.c. is represented by liquid
assets—which in this case means coin, bullion and
'■ash balances. That is a quite satisfactory propor-
tion. A high proportion of such assets puts a bank
in a position to meet any sudden demand, caused by
an unexpected scare. The sudden demand is, how-
ever, not the only sort of demand. There may, for
instance, be a steady shrinkage in the value of securi-
ties; and then other things have to be considered.
From the creditors’ point of view the important
thing, in that case, is to consider how much the
shareholders’ funds amount to in proportion to the
deposits and other borrowed money. Obviously a
bank which has borrowed £lO from the public for every
£1 of its own has much more to fear from a slump
than one which has borrowed only £3, assuming, of
course, that both have borrowed, not for the fun of
the thing, but to lend the money out again. Measured
by that foot-rule, this National of Tasmania is in a.
particularly strong position, because its borrowings
amount to only £3 3s. for every £1 of its own.
Scarcely another bank in Australasia owes so little
Of course that isn’t all gain. If a bank hasn’t the
nsk of losses, it can’t have the prospects of profits
either; and it is really surprising that the profits
in this case are as large as they are. Calculated on
the basis of capital and reserves combined, they pan
out as follows:
Nov., 1906.. £6 14 2 p.c. I May, 1909.. £8 7 2 p.c.
May, 1907.. 913 5,, Nov., 1909 712 2
Nov., 1907.. 819 3 ~ | May, 1910. , 7 1,9 11 ’’
May, 1908.. 9 2 0,, I Nov., 1910.. 716 JO
Nov., 1905.. 813 3 „ | May, 1911.. 716 4 ”
Under ordinary circumstances that should mean that
the bank hasn’t got any or many dead or unpro-
ductive accounts on its books; and that adds to the
respect with which this paper regards the co. Only this
paper hopes that the present div. won’t be put up
another fraction until the reserves are at least
doubled. The amount put by in these prosperous
limes isn’t what it ought to be.

I’hew! That will make some people feel pretty sore.
As a matter of fact, there was a deal of very peculiar
juggling over the reconstructions of this co. in the
gaunt years after drought had dried the coat off the
far-out squatter’s back, and the rabbits had generally
chewed his trousers right up to the limit of respecta-
bility. There were heavy debentures in those days;
but instead of the shareholders parting up to the full,
the “B” debentures suffered to the extent of 40 p.c.
Now, seeing that the debenture-holders don’t get any
of that 40 p.c. restored, while the shareholders are
getting some of their written-off capital put back
into their pocket, there is hound to be more bad
language. Some old shareholders, who are share-
holders no longer, will also be liable to pass remarks
which might displease Mr. Judkins. That is because
they were asked to load themselves with a heavy lia-
bility, which was subsequently reduced—after the
aforementioned shareholders had declined to take on
the liability, and had allowed their holdings to be
forfeited. However, all that is pretty ancient
history now; nevertheless, one is bound to" feel sorry
for these people; and so far as debenture-holders, at
any rate, are concerned, it ought to be a lesson to
them never to consent to a reduction of capital debt
unless it is expressly provided that the reduction
shall be made good contemporaneously with any
restoration of written-off share capital. The directors
of Goldsbrough-Mort’s, though they had no power to
restore the 40 p.c. of debenture debt, did one decent
thing. Under the last arrangement, the “B” deben-
tures were entitled to 4 p.c. so long as the share-
holders only got 3 p.c. ; if the latter got more than
3 p.c., the “B’s” were to get 5 p.c. The directors
• —the co. agreeing—-decided to risk it, and make the
5 p.c. a permanent thing.
The share capital is now £638,852; so the net
profit for the year amounted to 43 p.e. after paying
the debenture interest. This profit is shown after
making various deductions—£3B77 as bonus to staff,
£2839 for depreciation of plant and premises, and
£lO,OOO off premises account. The debenture debt
is now—“A” stock, £1,015,060; “B” stock,
£677,286. All other liabilities total only £85,464.
Capital, reserves and liabilities (including contin-
gent liabilities, £13,532) amounted on March 31 to
£2,898,889. These total figures are very much the
same as those of 10 years ago. The items have, how-
ever, suffered considerable change:—
1901. 1911.
Capital £55"',995 £638,852

The increase of capital is explained in the first table.
(The figures in the last comparison are, by the way,
as at March 31, before the appropriations have been
made. That explains why the reserves show up
higher than in the first table.) The comparison in-
dicates a much-improved position. And probably the
improvement is greater than the figures even re-
motely suggest. It is very significant that, on ap-
proximately the same balance-sheet totals, the profits
have increased fivefold in the 10 years. These ac-
counts are really only the aggregate of a large
number of station and other accounts; and it has
scarcely been possible to look at some station ac-
counts lately with the naked eye, they are so daz-
zling. The usual thing in pastoral book-keeping is
to take the stock in at one fixed price, which doesn’t
alter from year to year. Increases or decreases in
number must, of course, be taken into account; and
the proceeds of sales made in excess of the book
value —usually a pretty low value—are profit. That
partly explains how very big profits have been made
m the last few years. Prices for stock have been
very high; lambings and other increases have been
very good; and, instead of losirig stock by drought,
they have been got to the market, usually as fats,
and the surplus in excess of book values has been
shown as profit. No doubt also this co. has made
sales of properties; and the surpluses realised over
the written-down values have also been profit. But,
since they can’t sell the same properties again, the
profit from this source can only be pocketed once a
thing well worth remembering.
The visible position has improved; but the pub-
lished accounts only show us the surface of things
As mentioned just now, those accounts are the
aggregate of many station and other accounts; and
we don t know how those separate accounts are being
handled whether a policy of conservative writing-
down is being followed, for instance, or whether
every possible penny is being crowded into the co.’s
balance-sheet. If, however, the management is not
seizing the opportunity to make itself very safe The
Bulletin hopes that all the directors will have in-
somnia for a month. They know from experience
what an erratic business they are in; they know
what a mess was made of things before; they know
how creditors who never should have suffered were
made to suffer. Failure to provide for the inevitable
losses of the future would therefore be disgraceful

The income (less amounts written off) jumps about
m a mysterious way, according to that table. The
spring which , causes this jumpiness is the American
law providing for the annual valuation of securities.
Under this law the assets have to go in the accounts
a t their present value; and as this value may vary
to the extent of millions, the “income” may either
spring or slump by millions. Another and better
view of the situation is given in the next table:—

llie Equitable is one of the three great American
life offices which extended their business to Aus-
tralia, and proceeded to do things on the same lavish
scale as at home. The struggle was for big figures ;
how they were secured didn’t matter so much.
Ihings might have continued to go along in the bad
old hysterical way had not some of the directors and
managers put their finger into the political pie.
lhere was a howl—a very proper howl —at once;
funds which ought to be accumulated for the policy-
holders were going into the campaign funds of one
party. The other party began to tell what it knew ;
and no doubt the worst things were told about this
co. A legislative and domestic cleaning up fol-
lowed. The offices were held down while their wings
were clipped; they were forbidden to again attempt
to fly after such big figures. Expenses being re-
stricted, the struggle for new business stopped in the
places where it was most expensive. Australia was
one of those places. So the whole Australian estab-
lishment was disbanded, and David Fell and 00. were
appointed agents and attorneys. Fortunately that
9 111 J of accountants and auditors had a good name,
and their word carried weight with the Australian
policy-holder, who must have been sorefy tempted
to take whatever he could get and move out. At
that time this co. held about £3,200,000 of Aus-
tralian insurance.
As the second little table shows, the Equitable is
now rather more than holding its own in the matter
of income from business ; so the wholesale and usually
foolish lapsing of policies has been stopped. Pre-
miums from new business now once more exceed the
premiums from policies which have lapsed or be-
come claims. This shows the movement of total
premium income (including consideration for an-
nuities, though very little of that business is done) :
1904 £12,931,442
1905 12,386,891
1906 11,646,293
1907' 11,106,346
1908 .. .. 10,774,907
1909 .. 10,825,399
1910 .. 10,925,549
Thus the tide, after running out strongly, is coming
in again; and confidence seems to be gradually re-
covering. In 1907 surrenders accounted for no less
than 55 p.e. of the total claims; for last year they
were less than 26 p.e. That’s really one of the best
features of the accounts.
The expense rate has been got down to something
reasonable. Last year the outlay, equalled £l4 os. fid-.
p.c. of the total income from premiums and interest.
This isn’t a low rate by any means, and it compares
very poorly with the best Australian results; but it
is far better than the experience was in the days of
the boom. The saving, of course, means better bonuses.
(The bonuses last year amounted to £2,171,711,
plus a trifle on account of cash bonus deposits.) The
rate of interest is satisfactory; but the rate of in-
terest is really not so important in connection with
a life office as the quality of the securities. On this
point, at any rate, the American law does more for
the protection of the policy-holder than the Austra-
lian laws do. As usual with American offices, bonds
(mainly railway) and stock represent more than half
the assets (say, 53 p.c.); mortgages represent 20
p.c. ; loans on policies 13 p.c. In view of the ab-
surdly large sums spent by the Equitable on premises
in Australia, it is satisfactory to observe that the
total represented by “house property” and “landed
property” is under 6 p.c. of the total assets.

This is one of the two big co-operative distributing-
butter and produce cos. in N.S.W. ; it sticks ex-
clusively to distributing, while the other one does a
bit of manufacturing on its own account. It seems
to have two main objects—-to get as close to the
actual consumer as possible without going into the
retail business, and (which is partly the same ob-
ject) to promote direct exporting, instead of letting
the speculator have his cut. From July 1, 1910,
to May 31, 1911, the total exports from N. S. Wales
were 14,279 tons, of which this co. shipped 3039
tons, and the North Coast Co. 2156 tons. The latter
co. does its own exporting, though the Coastal
Farmers is its distributing agent in N. S. Wales.
The total profits from all sources, in spite of a bigger
turnover, were not as large last year as in the pre-
ceding two years. Lower average prices—which
mean, of course, smaller commissions—were partly
responsible. The average selling price for 12 months
to March, 1909, was 113 s. per cwt. in Sydney; 1910,
107 s. 2d. ; 1911, 102 s. 3d. That looks as though
increased supplies were pulling prices down; as a
matter oC fact, though, the 1909 prices were un-
usually high. The co. did unwisely last year
to distribute all its profits in bonuses and
dividends; but the co-operative idea isn’t yet
as firmly planted as it ought to be, and
the struggle for business is no doubt keen.
Hence the co. has been enticed to pay back
to suppliers some of the money which it ought to
have put by against contingencies. The paid capital
is practically all of this is in the business
—only £2747 is sunk in plant, fittings, etc. In
other words, all but that small amount is available
tor the protection of suppliers; and a guarantee
fund of that size is a handy thing- for the supplier to
see about the premises of the people to whom he en-
trusts his produce on perhaps a month’s credit.

The general use of artificial fertilisers has
led to the establishment of a big new industry in
Australia. Superphosphates are mostly in demand;
and when they began to be used, the supply came
from foreign parts. A large proportion of the trade,
m N.S.W. especially, was done, by A. H. Hasell, who
lately came to the sensible conclusion that the time
had arrived when the import trade should be stopped
and Australia should furnish its own requirements
He has,'' therefore, projected a co. to establish a fac-
tory. To a great extent the scheme is on a co-opera-
tive basis, large numbers of farmers having come
into it. The co.—the Farmers’ Fertilisers Corpora-
tion. Ltd.—makes a first issue of 71,000 shares to
the public—46,ooo ordinary and 25,000 6 p.c. cumu-
lative pref. Another 24,000 are reserved for direct
consumers; and 5000 go to the vendor (A. H.
Hasell) for his business connection, trade marks, etc.-
(Most of the direct consumers’ shares have been al-
ready applied for.) No cash is to be paid to the
vendor. The superphosphate business is understood’
Li very profitable, and there cannot be anv doubt
whatever about an increased demand as the farming
business extends, as it is bound to extend with the
closer settlement of the country. Applications for
snares may be made through any Exchange broker.
Victoria has made a quiet start with a
State Fire Insurance Office. The necessary powder
was supplied by an Act which probably, to the House
of Rich Landlords, didn’t look loaded—the Special
Funds Act of 1910. The Victorian Government, like
most others, long ago established a fund to indem-
nify the State in the event of any misdescription of
land titles. This fund became very bloated ; and
authority was given to the Government to dip into it.
Willie Watt, evidently anticipating deputations from
tax-dodging shires, took the first dip himself in the
shape of an appropriation to establish a Closer Set-
tlement Fire Insurance Fund. Thus, at last, a start has
been made with a State Fire Office—certainly a very
small start, and as yet hardly visible to the naked
eye, but a start nevertheless. At present the pro-
visions enable only persons holding- areas under the
Closer Settlement Acts to insure their buildings and
lencing with the Government; but if they can get
the benefit of cheap rates, there will soon be a shout
other producers, who won’t see why they also
shouldn t get insurance without providing anvbodv
with fat dividends.
The boom in company promoting in
X.SAV. has moved Sydney Telegraph to tears because
the A.SAY. Parliament has neglected to pass a Com-
panies Act like \ ictoria. The investing public is at'
the mercy of the unscrupulous promoter who can
write an alluring prospectus. All the States, the
lelegraph admits, are behindhand except Victoria,
and there are loud cries for the six States to pa-s
six Acts identical with the British Companies Act, in
order to block the shark. It doesn’t strike the Tele-
graph (or if it does the paper suppresses the fact)
that if the Referendum had been carried, the Federal
1 arliament by passing one Act could have achieved
what the six separate Parliaments of 12 Houses can
only do by six separate Acts. And the chances are
that when the 12 Houses have finished their job—if
they ever attempt it—there will be just enough dif-
ferences in the measures to be a pitfall to the un-
wary and the cause of much expensive litigation.
The smelting business in Australia is in a
very bad way, owing to the keen competition of
European buyers. The oldest established works in
the Commonwealth—those at Waratah —are idle,
being unable to pay as good a price for ore as the
Kuropean buyers are offering, in order to grab the
custom. A week or so ago the N.S.W. Acting-
Premier was asked whether he knew of the serious
condition of the industry. He said he didn’t know,
and, moreover, didn’t see how it could be in the con-
dition alleged, seeing that the Mines Department’s
figures showed an increase in the N.S.W. output of
smelted copper. That conclusion shows how dan-
gerous a little learning is. The fact, well known to
every metal man, is that during the past couple of
years big works have been established at Port Kembla,
primarily to handle the output of Mount Morgan
(<?■) ’ knd subsequently an arrangement was made
with Mount byel] (Tas.). In nine cases out of 10
the smelting business fails because it hasn’t got a
mine behind it to supply it with fodder when it
can t buy any from the public. The Mount Morgan
contract took the place of a mine owned by the co. ;
and contracts of this kind, and of the kind made
subsequently with Mount Lyell, yield of themselves
either no profit at all or none worth mentioning,
l’he increase in the N.S.W. export of smelted and re-
fined copper is, therefore, wholly misleading so far
as it suggests a more prosperous state of the in-
dustry.
Vegetable Creek, Emmaville (N.S.W;), has
proved its deep lead beneath the basalt for some
800 ft. West of the shaft the old co. blocked out
a fair slice of ground, and obtained sluicing returns
equal to lcwt. per cubic yard. Unfortunately this
(lulu t pay, because of the difficulty and expense of
securing the cubic yard. The tin scratcher’s troubles
are many, among them a heavy flow of water, de-
pressions in the bottom, boulders, drift that con-
tains mighty little tin, and over-burden so heavy
that it is difficult to secure. Sometimes, as in
tins case, he is also troubled with a ground landlord,
who holds the freehold. The crosscut at the eastern
end of the workings is 338 ft. east of the shaft, and
has exposed 100 ft. of washdirt, averaging more than
2ft. in thickness, and estimated to be worth 1401 b.
per cubic yard. Of course, boulder wash, indicative
of the main channel, is just what the co. is looking
for, and driving to the east is to be continued in the
hope that at last the main channel has been dis-
covered. The prospects are distinctly cheerful.
Apropos the eucalyptus oil which the
Australian metal flotation cos. are now using. The
Minerals Separation 00. appears to have been the
first to apply it; previously, residual oil (mineral)
had been employed. Lavers, who has been working
experimentally on the flotation processes ever since the
first small plant was put in at Broken Hill Block
14, is credited with discovering its efficacy. The
oil isn t the eucalyptus oil that Billjim pours into
himself when he thinks he has a cold. It is the dis-
tilled juice of the peppermint, or amygdalina, and
can be bought, f.0.b., almost any Australian port,
tor about 7<d. or Bd. per lb. The older eucalyptus
oil costs about lid., and is practically valueless for
flotation purposes. The difference between the two
is that the one oil (amygdalina) is extraordinarily
emulsive, while the other has that quality in only a
small degree. It doesn’t take much to a' ton of'ore
about 21b.—but, even so, the distilling industry is
becoming quite sizeable on account of the vast ton-
nages put through by the flotation processes.
Great Britain Sluicing Co. (N.S.W.) is
licking its lips over the boom price of tin. Last
half-year the co. paid a monthly div. ( £3500 on
a total paid capital of £5000), and, besides writing
£1538 off leases and plant, added £485 to the un-
divided balance. The co, has also been improving
its water supply. In fact, all through its career
the management has kept a watchful eye on that
very important feature of the business, and has
dodged stoppages from drought exceedingly well.
Mount Copeland (N.S.W.) has been pro-
nounced upon by W. A. T. Davies, who does a good
deal of sniffing round for 8., M. and Co. He savs
the formation is “a gash vein, enclosed in a massive-
grained, light-grey diorite.” The gashes in share-
holders pockets promise to deepen, in view of the
dissimilarity between the assay values related at
the time of the float and those now revealed by
Davies. The formation itself has been proved for
its greatest length in No. 1 tunnel, where over
1000 ft. of lode material averages 2ft. 7in. in width.
Of this 835 ft. pans out at s%dwt., or 245. 3d. per
ton, and the whole length 235. Bd. Total reserves
are 06,000 tons. Computing an 85 p.c. extraction,
the profit is to be £552 per month on a tonnage
of 2500 tons. This is 4s. 4d. per ton. To get it,
£6OOO must be spent on top of the stuff that has
already been poured out of shareholders’ “gash
veins.” Deducting that outlay, the profit in sight
is about £ll,OOO. There is some talk of originals
being asked to part up some of the stuff that came
out of the float.

“(’ulex”: I want to blow up these lying
mining reports. I’ve just spent three weeks at Boo-
dalin (W.A.). The daily papers published reports
that alluvial had been found in quantities fromdwts.
to ozs. When there were 100 men on the field they
said there were 200. Later it was reported that most
of the men were on gold. (There were just four men
on the metal.) Then it was said that the alluvial
was on the leases which had been approved. (Right
—as to one lease.) Then the men were said to be
rushing the leases, and trouble was threatening. As
to that a few men pegged on the lease, but when
they found that the law was against them they
quietly left it alone. If 500 men had turned up, and
were a bit annoyed about it, and showed their an-
noyance, the papers would at once yell for Law n -
order, quite overlooking the fact that they would
be the cause of the commotion. The practice is for
any sort of correspondent to have a yarn with some
storekeeper, and base a report on the remarks sup-
plied by that gent., and the plan is one to be severely
discouraged. Wherefore I heave out this junk of
gospel. Surely we have had a sufficiency of boom at
Bullfinch —poor old Bullfinch !
Oroya Exploration (W.A) has thrown up
its option on the Ingliston Consols at Meekatharra,
and its occasional partner, Oroya Junks, has followed
suit by turning down the Gimlet at Ora Banda.
Too stiff a price is the reason assigned for busi-
ness not being done in the former case; but as
regards the Gimlet, the Oroya Links has probably,
for. the reasons given in B. 4/5/’ll, made room for
some less critical buyer.

Central and West Boulder shareholders
foregathered in London on May 10 and brooded over
a 17 months’ statement of accounts. This showed
a credit balance of £3812; but as the total in-
cluded the co.’s share of 12 months’ profits which
its working partner —Oroya Links—refuses to stump
up because of a counter-claim for alleged encroach-
ment by Central Boulder tributors on the Links’
Oroya South block, the alleged surplus really has
no right to be shown at all. The cos. are arguing
the claim and the counter-claim, and litigation is
threatened. Since the Oroya Links gave up working
the property, tributors have earned £5OO a month
in royalty for Central Boulder. In view of this, and
of the pressing need for more funds to carry on
with, the meeting decided to try to raise an addi-
tional £25,000 by forming a new co. in 250,000
10s. shares, with an assessment of 2s. per share.
Corinthian, at Southern Cross (W.A.), has
joined the list of local producers. This is the property
which D. L. Uoolette found impossible to sell to J.
Bull, and is a next-door neighbor to the Corinthian
North, for which the Bull Ant Co. is now said to
have a sneaking regard. Maybe the Ant crowd will
make a bid for both shows, and, if successful, will
work them as one property.
Rowan’s Find (W.A.) has given its Bull-
finch delusion best, and, like so many other Tolet-
ville cos., is now on the look-out for another prop-
erty. It is only six months since Rowan’s Find
was floated on the reports of two persons. One of
these declared that it was “the best show outside
Bullfinch,” and added that with a battery (which
he recommended should be got without delay) the
working of the property would be accompanied by
“regular distribution of good profits.” The other
had “no hesitation in recommending the property
as a first-class mining investment,” A golden
mirage must have filled the eyes of those two ex-
perts when they inspected the property.
Cue Consolidated, a London speculative
concern, which to its financial sorrow holds Lance-
fields and other unprofitable things in the West, is
now on a new lay. As intermediate vendor it is
busy with others engineering a Porcupine flota-
tion with a Capital of £600,000, to work a number
of claims, including the Hollinger and Dome. Out
of this deal the co. hopes to collar about £13,000
cash, and a com. of 15 p.c. in cash and shares on
150,000 which it has managed to place. It is in
this venture that the Sons of Gwalia has put
£25,000. ;

Ida 11. shareholders met in London on
May 9, and unanimously agreed to the proposed in-
crease in capital. As already stated, the extra
money is to be utilised in cheapening the present
cost of working, and for further developing the
mine at depth. Regarding the deeper levels there
is a marked discrepancy between the estimate of
values by the late manager Campbell Shaw and that
of R. A. Varden, who recently made an independent
inspection. Shaw gave the value of the ore as
30dwt., while Varden reported it as 97dwt.—a differ-
ence of £l4 per ton. Shaw says he took his sam-
ples 4ft. apart to a depth of 36ft. below 1000 ft.,
whereas Varden’s results were obtained from sam-
ples which Shaw claims were not a fair average.
After all, the best and only convincing test is the
mill; and that will probably show' the retiring
boss’s estimate to be nearer the mark than Var-
den’s.
“Oof Bird’’ (W.A.) talks about Meeka-
tharra’s best knovrn mines in the following strain:
“Marmont.—Crushing has been resumed after months
of development. There is plenty of ore available
for treatment above the present bottom level at
300 ft. Fenian.—Cream skimming is still practised
here, and with the extra gold obtained from the en-
larged mill and the newly-erected slimes plant, re-
turns are certain to be ‘bigger than ever’ for a time.
Some of the stopes are wide and the grade high;
but there are stacks of low-grade stuff, which the
spirit will probably move the owners to treat when
they get over their present ore-picking craze. Ing-
liston Consols.- —Most striking feature about this
mine is the size of its stopes, which in places show
a width of from 12ft. to 20ft., over a length of 200 ft.
Only a bite or two of ore has been taken out be-
tween 300 ft. and 500 ft., and the 10-head mill is
much too small to meet present requirements. Ing-
liston Extended. —This show has a big area and two
apparently permanent lodes. Over 50,000 tons of
30s. ore were extracted from one of these bodies in
the oxidised workings, and, like the other Meeka-
tharra shoots that have been followed down, it is
richer in the sulphide zone. Hopes are entertained
of shareholders making a lot of money some day.
Commodore. —A good deal of gold was obtained by
the prospectors down to 100 ft., and tlie work done
by the co. now in possession also panned out well.
So far the 300 ft. level has been disappointing, but
values are expected to right themselves in deeper
ground.”
“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : Have got my second
wind, so that “R.B.’s” uncharitable hope has drawn a
blank. His rigmarole in B. 25/5/’ll only leaves
one point to settle —whether he is a fool or the
other thing. On the published statements as to
what Grut had reported, and what some other fel-
lows said he had reported, “R. 8.” alleges be bought
shares and made a loss. He “never heard that Doo-
lette had made a report,” but he did read what
Crabbe had to say, and he knew “after the middle
of December” that the Bull Ant wasn’t a mine.
Yet in B. 12/l/’ll he attacked a Bulletin writer
and puffed the show as a good thing, and until
stirred up three months later he never thought it
worth while saying anything more about it. If
“R. 8.” knew about the valuelessness of the property
“after the middle of December,” why did he write
what he did near the middle of January ?

The same old story, as told by extracts
from the Golden Horseshoe manager’s last three
annual reports Feb. 13, 1909: “In view of the
satisfactory state of the mine generally and the large
ore reserves, also the increased prospective value of
the mine on account of the recent important develop-
ments at the 2000 ft. level, I consider the outlook of
the mine to be increasingly satisfactory.” . . . Feb.
16, 1910: “In view of the splendid ore bodies proved
at the 2000 ft. level, and the very important develop-
ments at the 2600 ft. level on our eastern boundary,
and the increased ore reserves promised by these de-
velopments, special arrangements have been made to
push ahead with the opening up of our ore bodies in
the lower levels as rapidly as possible, and at the same
time increase the capacity of our treatment plant, so
as to bring its efficiency to a point equal to 200 head
of stamps under existing conditions. . . . Taking
all the foregoing into consideration, 1 consider the
future prospects of the mine are exceedingly good;
in fact, the outlook of the mine has never looked
better.” Feb. 18, 1911: “I regret the operations
for the 12 months have been so unsatisfactory and
disappointing. . . . The bad year which we have
had is not a true indication of the position and pos-
sibilities of the mine, which you will see from this
report and an examination of the mine plans are
very promising indeed, and in my opinion should
improve.” Nevertheless, since the end of 1908 the
estimated value of the ore reserves has been cut
down by over a million sterling! For a mine with
prospects “never better” at the end of 1909, this
ought to be something like a record.
* * * *
William Orr, trying to get the Vic. Cham-
ber of Mines to “recommend the State Government to
take into consideration the advisability of establish-
ing iron and steel works,” tried to warm up the un-
imaginative crowd with a few Yankee figures. “The
magnitude of the United States Steel Corporation,
owned by 80,000 shareholders, was emphasised by
the fact that it employed 180,000 workmen, and more
than a million American people were dependent on it
for a livelihood. The co. pays £25,600,000 in wages
per annum, and possesses 30,000 cars and 700 loco-
motives and owns a fleet of 100 ore ships. It has
93 blast furnaces running day and night, and makes
44 p.c. of the pig iron of the United States. The
co. also owns 50 ironstone mines, which produce
16,000,000 tons of ore per annum. Huge quantities
of this ore are conveyed from Minnesota, Lake Supe-
rior district, to Pittsburg, Pennsylvania, a distance
of 800 miles. This corporation paid Mr. J. D. Rocke-
feller £16,000,000 for his iron ore mines. The
capital of the corporation exceeds £276,000,000,
an'd the net annual earnings exceed £21,000,000.”
And then he tried to dazzle them with some Austra-
lian figures, showing the consumption of iron and
steel goods (say, 4jt millions sterling a year), and
the millions of tons of unused raw material. But it
was no good. The Chamber gasped “Socialism!”
and decided that it would rather have nothing than
share something with the crowd.
Cathcart Central, Ararat (Vic.), is now
running level with the Cathcart in favor. For a
mine with the shaft half full of water, with plant
not yet up, with a drive to go out to the lead, and
with the gutter to be drained, th.s means that the
baby is going to step into the old man’s shoes.
What flips Central shares forward is that the Cath-
cart has got gold down the lead in the deep
ground. The old co. has only been saved from sink-
ing a new shaft before by the fact that the run of
goid has kept high on the northern bank of the gut-
ter. The sample got in the pot-hole sunk under
the level went 3oz. to the fathom. A big effort
will be made to reach the gold without a shaft;
for it would be risky to go to the expense when only
about 1000 ft. of lead remains to be worked. Critics of
the Dodds-Bryant party are turning up files to re-read
the famous declaration that the Cathcart lead could
not go into the Central, into which it certainly is
now making. McCrorey smiles at this, feeling that
his little investment in the Central isn’t so rocky
now that some of his old opponents are sidling into
his paddock for feed.
W. 11. Cundy, of Bendigo, appears as a
Northern Territory expert. Three and a-half years
ago he was missed from the Beehive. Only his inti-
mates knew that he was chasing copper in the Gulf
of Carpentaria. The bread cast away has returned
after many days in the form of notes on one of the
formations that prospectors Nelson and King have
picked up for the Melbourne-Northern Territory syn-
dicate. Cundy cuts the width of the lode down to
loft., and instead of representing it as the biggest
galena formation in the world, describes it as “segre-
gated masses,” which means anything. Of course
it isn’t certain that his notes fit the lodes found
by the prospectors. Assuming that they do, there
isn’t much on which to build a rush to Borooloola.
His picture of the creek cutting the lode falls far
short of the story heard here. The worst little de-
tail is that a shafthas been sunk to 25ft. or 30ft., and
no one in the wide world apparently can tell who
did the work, or what has become of the ore. This
is enough to raise some dizzy doubt, for if there
had been much 80 p.c. lead ore knocking round some
of the old Maroomba birds would surely have had a
peck at it when the boom was on.
* * * *
The smelting works at Aldershot (Q.),
which came to grief partly through bad management
and partly through not having a mine behind them,
are in process of being dismantled. The whole outfit
has been sold and the furnaces have been pulled
down, no doubt to recover any metals that may have
got into the bricks.
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers,
crushed for the month 4420 tons for £B6OO ; cyan-
iding (3200 tons tailings) gave £1411; total re-
venue, just over £lO,OOO. Costs have been screwed
down, so the profit is about £2700, which is the
best for some time past. On the strength of this im-
provement and its accumulated profit, the Extended
pays a sixpenny dividend. Unfortunately the deepest
level (No. 20) is disappointing, and the reserves are
being eaten up.
East Mexican, Charters Towers, is crush-
ing more stone, and the fortnightly output has in-
creased to 590 tons ; but the grade is lower by a few
shillings. A dividend has been declared, payable
July 10. East Mexican got a good deal of help from
its neighbor, the New Brilliant Freehold, but has
now given some return by proving a new make of
stone above one of the Freehold’s blank areas. The
latter, taking the , hint, will put another rise up to-
wards the Mexican boundary. Like its neighbors,
the Mexican has to battle through blanks, which ac-
counts for the recent depression.
* * * *
“Quidam”: The idea that Charters Towers
is “done,” an opinion held by many people at vari-
ous times since the field started, is a pessimistic
groan not likely to be warranted for many a
long day to come. The April returns were
£41,000 odd, and a field that can produce that
amount in 30 days, after having been “done” so
many times, will probably live to smile again. On
the Towers every mine, like the proverbial mongrel,
has its day, and each year sees some battler emerge
from obscurity and become a star. Each successive
year, like each Melbourne Cup, immortalises some
new name; there has been the year of the Brilliant,
and of the Day Dawn, and of the Brilliant and St.
George, of the Day Dawn Block and Wyndham, and
the Brilliant Central, of the Brilliant Ex-
tended, and the Queen Cross, of the Mills
United, and the Brilliant Freehold, and now the
East Mexican has its run. One important feature
of the field is the wide expanse of auriferous country
surrounding the comparatively small area in wh'ch
most of the mining has been done ; nevertheless until
very recently it was easier to get cash in Charters
Towers to prospect an auriferous proposition in
Sahara than to develop a promising show a few
miles outside the town. Now the tendency is the
other way ; and Towers people are beginning to rea-
lise that at their back door there are quite as at-
tractive propositions as in, say, Papua, or at the
South Pole.
Oaks Rush (Q.) is putting the finishing
touches to its plant. Some of the machinery is old,
and was bought che.ap, but the gas producer and
engine, dynamo, electric pumps on the river, and a
pipe-line to the mill, make it an up-to-date equip-
ment. At this parent mine, development work at
the 100 ft. level has been in hand for some time, and
a big formation has been exposed. Most of it is
poor, though the quality is variable. Tt is a case
where the battery is left —-with very considerable
confidence—to tell its own tale.
Last year the ancient Wallaroo and Moon-
ta (S.A.) produced copper at a cost of £54 11s. 2d.,
so the profit was scanty. With these high costs the
average grade of the vein-stuff is of great importance.
It is, therefore, a happy bit of news that recently
some rich ore, assaying up to 25 p.c., has been dis-
covered near Warmington’s, in a part of the work-
ings that some years since produced high-grade stuff.
The lode is 12ft. wide, and enough ore is being ob-
tained to sweeten up the returns.
Mount Lyell, helped by the enrichment in
its deepest level, is obtaining an extraction of slightly
over 2i p.c. Just now, with the metal quoted at
£56, the value of 2| units of copper is 28s. At
this average 40 tons of crude ore run a ton of red
metal, so the working cost on the latter (taking the
present cost on crude ore at 17s. Bd.) totals £35
6s. Bd. per ton of blister —a remarkablv low figure.
Mount Lyell isn’t likely to improve on that; on the
other hand, costs are more likely to get above 18s.
than to keep below it. The tendency is almost bound
to be upwards, and, with a tonnage like Lyell’s, it
doesn’t take many pence to eat a big hole in the
profits.
Mount Bischoff (Tas.), with only 12,000
shares and a monthlj' output of 90 tons of tin oxide,
wears feathers in its hat in consequence of the pre-
sent boom in the white metal. Shareholders are get-
ting £3600 per month (6s. per share), which repre-
sents a profit of £6O per ton on the metal coming
out of the mine; and the co. can do this very easily.
Last half-year the average return per ton of dirt was
a shade over 101 b. tin oxide, containing 71b. of
metal. Basing a calculation on this, the nearly pure
tin costs £96 per ton; all over that is profit. The
expenses are so low that a ton of metal can be taken
out of 320 tons of dirt at a substantial profit,
with the metal at half its present price. Bischoff is
also doing well just now out of its customs trade, so
if the high prices last shareholders can look forward
to a bonus.
Captain Miles, ex-Minister of Lands and
Works, is back in Tasmania from Siam, and reports
of the Tongkah property that everything in the
garden is lovely. The co. has five dredges working
on good tin, the old manager has been replaced, the
co. has bought itself out of its dock and channel
agreement, boring and dredging tests prove that the
tin “does not decrease in quantity or quality as we
go seaward,” Nos. 4 and 5 dredges last month won
70 tons of tin from ground 1 i miles from shore, and,
though “there are occasional patches of poor ground,
the poorest is highly payable.” In Tasmania, despite
the fact that a decade ago Miles made political life
a bit too warm, there are many shrewd folks who
reckon that he is a lucky man "to hunt with —a re-
sourceful person who always plays to win.
* * * *
From Maoriland : There has been a mild
flutter in Thames stocks owing to the May Queen,
now developing at 1000 ft., striking strong dabs of
gold in one of its reefs at that depth. Talisman,
Karangahake, reports £17,850 from 4230 tons for
last crushing, and all is looking well. Ohinemuri
shares have had a temporary set-back on account of
the new demands of the Waihi Miners’ Union and
the fact that the Karangahake men have voted to
leave the Thames body and affiliate with the Waihi
Union and Federation of Labor. The Waihi Union
wants 9s. for surface men instead of 7s. 6d., and
corresponding increases for miners, with revised pro-
visions as to hours and overtime. The parties have
met in conference, but haven’t been able to come
to terms, and the outlook is pretty glum.
The Rand gold output for April was
the rate of 8,000,0000 z. a year ! Be-
tween 1880 and 1890 the average annual production
for the whole world was under 6,000,0000 z.
“8.E.” : A few facts re the mono-rail and
other trains, of which “Leo Lear” (B. 4/5/’ll)
seems unaware. For Brennan’s mono-train power is
provided by a petrol engine, which is not cheaper
than steam per b.h.p. developed. The petrol engine
drives an electric generator, from which separate
motors on each vehicle obtain their power. Gyro-
scopes on each vehicle have each their own separate
motor, but these are of small power, and for that
reason of comparatively insignificant weight. There
is not less friction with one rail than with two;
although only half as many wheels, each wheel of
necessity carries double the weight. The reason that
Brennan’s train can mount steeper grades depends,
not on the gyroscopes or the one rail (as “Leo,” in
common with a great many other laymen, appears to
think), but on the fact that each of the wheels, not
Dnlv on the loco., but on the trucks as well, is a
driving-wheel. This is no new idea, and has already
been applied to electric trains and trams. The mono-
rail train could not climb a steeper grade than a
Sydney tramcar if each of the wheels of the latter
were connected to the motors. Does “Leo” wish one
to infer that the mono-train will go round a three-
chain curve at 120 miles per hour? Such a train
would be inclined at 11 degrees to the horizontal,
and would exert a side-thrust on the rails equal to
five times its own weight! The track for such a
train should be well worth seeing. I will admit
that, under other circumstances, a high speed could
be obtained with the mono, but so it can on two
rails. Express trains have put up speeds in trials of
120 miles per hour and over in Germany. The mono
certainly has its advantages, chief of which are:
Less cost for rails (more than half, however), for
sleepers (because shorter), and cheapness and rapidity
of track-construction. The rolling-stock, however,
would be vastly more expensive; not only does the
cost of gyroscopes and motors enter into considera-
tion, but the undercarriages are much more compli-
cated and costly. The advantages as to grade, speed,
etc., so often cited, are equally applicable to a two-
rail track, and are by no means confined to Brennan’s
or anyone else’s mono-rail. The above is not meant
to be in any way derogatory to this invention, the
chief use of which, in the writer’s opinion, will for
some time to come be for military and other pur-
poses, which call for the laying of a track in
minimum time. What it will lead to in the future,
of course, remains to be seen.
Every engineer in charge of factories and mills
should give close attention to the power wasted in
transmission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller
Bearings save 20 per cent.' power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturally for
engineers to direct their special attention to these
bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are
in Continuous use in Cement Works, Ore Dressing
Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bushings oi
flexible rollers may be applied to loose pulleys or
truck wheels, etc. Should engineers require further
details, they can apply to the Australian Agents,
W. & J. Lempriere, Melbourne; or E. W. Hurst-
house and Co., of Wellington, the agents in New
Zealand.*

Tbe Bigelow Patent Rock Breaker commends
itself to mine managers who welcome an effective
machine which is cheaper to maintain and repair
than Gyratory and other crushers. This machine is
of the well-known Blake type, but has some ad-
ditional improvements which have justified in prac-
tice all the claims advanced by the patentee. The
work of crushing is done on the down-stroke of the
pitman instead of the up-stroke; there is no strain
on the pitman cap ; two large side rods resist the
thrust of the toggles, and enable the jaws to be
regulated within a large range; the back toggle is
so made as to resist all legitimate strains of crush-
ing, but will shear under exceptional conditions and
save the machine. The shaft and eccentric bearings
are self-lubricating; the jaws are convex and con-
cave, and with the cheek plates are made of steel
and are reversible. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have installed a great
number of these machines, and hold the sole manu-
facturing rights for the Commonwealth.* [3

Extract from the Saturday Review, January 7,
1911.—“ Insurance—Ae —A Colonial Mutual Departure:
Policies, combining life or endowment assurance
with other benefits are gradually coining into favor.
Their popularity seems to be justified. Why should
one be compelled to take out two or more policies
when several risks can just as easily be covered under
a single contract? It was a consideration of this
kind that some short time ago impelled the Board of
The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society to intro-
duce in Australasia a combined Life, Accident and
Sickness policy, and as the experiment was attended by
a most welcome increase in New Business, it was re-
cently decided to extend the issue to the United
Kingdom. There were evidently sound reasons for
the departure. Neither life nor endowment assurance
policies afford quite the full protection needed b.v
persons, because no provision is made for acci-
dents or sickness that do not end fatally. . . . Re-
garded as a whole, this new policy possesses many
attractions. It is decidedly cheap, and will appeal
to those who cannot afford to disburse large sums
yearly in the form of insurance premiums.”*

It’s not all sunshine being a
tea-room girl what, between
the “rot” the boys talk, and
the impertinent way some of the
young girls give their orders,
and the grumpy old men and
the grumbling old women, there’s
a lot to put up with still,
there’s a bright side too, thank
goodness for some of the
customers are really nice. If
people only knew how hard we
try to please everybody they’d
all treat us nicely.
For instance, if a girl takes
care when she gets an order for
a pot of tea that it is properly
wetted, and not allowed to stand
for more than 5 or 6 minutes,
don’t you think people might just
say “thank you, l enjoyed that
cup of tea ” —it wouldn’t cost
anything, and a kind word would
give a girl a lot of pleasure.
Talking about pleasing people
—do you know, I’ve heard more
nice things said about Robur
tea than anything else I’ve ever
had to serve—everybody seems
to like it —and a good many
take the trouble to say they do —
l must say 1 like it better than
any other tea myself—and I’m
sure there isn’t any tea more
saving to use.
The Wa tress.
