A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, June 19, 1911.
My Dear Arxni, —
When this reaches you Sydney will be
just starting to coronate with crackers,
electric bulbs, flags and other holiday trim-
mings ; and wedged in somewhere will be the
brilliant ball to be given by Mayor Allen
Taylor. “G.R.” is getting into working order
in large, fat-bodied letters on public build-
ings, and the Divorce Court is going to
blaze “God Save the King,” instead of its
favorite legend, “Who Shall Separate Us?”
“The Messiah,” the Sheffield Choir’s first
attack on the musical microbe in this town,
was remarkable for the fine quality of its
choral voices, and the mediocre and color-
Isss singing of the soloists. Nevertheless,
Miss Jennie Taggart, the soprano, carried
conviction in one of her solos:—
Shout, O daughter of Jerusalem !
Shout! Shout, O daughter !
Robert Charlesworth, the bass, was the
star. An old pupil of Dr. Coward’s, he
had no rivals amongst the principals. His
voice, before leaving Fogland, was men-
tioned by one of the choir, in a burst of
enthusiasm, as “magnificent”; but too
much travel is said to have tired its big
ding-dong tones.
The Yorkshire drawl of the visiting
Choir spread itself over Hawkesbury Col-
lege one day last week. Newcasfle-on-
Tyne, besides growing coal and machine-
guns, is also a vocal forcing-bed, which
produces choral singing in all its vagaries.
It sent a big contingent of singers to this
famous world’s scamper of the Ilarriss
team. Dr. Coward’s own Newcastle Choral
Society contributed 30 or more members.
The Yorkshire speaking voice—with ex-
ceptions, of course —was heard to great ad-
vantage at the pig-pens: “Doan’t he luik er
foine li-ittle pi-ig, and so fa-a-t!” One in-
fant pig might have been made into “baked
sucking” before 30 or 40 Yorkshire admirers
had got to the end of their “fa-a-t.”
We x-eached the trim lawns of the Hawkes-
bury College in a cloud of fine white dust.
Principal Potts extended the hand of hos-
pitality and got into his little pulpit in
the lecture hall at 11.20 a.m. The choir
found literature, relating to the art of early
rising on the land, hard to avoid. It was
liberally laid on to every desk. Mr. Potts
trimmed up his solid information with a
dry North-Countree wit that lent a keen
edge to bacon, butter and oats.
The mosquito served up in formalin—
uotably the Scots’ Grey with the military
band on his legs—gave Yorkshire food for
thought. The cicada with a pin through
his waist was another surprise; and flowers
growing in the open, in mid-winter, made
Sheffield stand up and ask questions.
The Choir sang on a full stomach immedi-
ately after lunch, in the green square of
the College quadrangle. “They’ll sing any'-
where, any time,” said Mr. Coward, junior.
His sisters were amongst the throng—two
youthful damsels, whose ruddy complexions
had the quite-well appearance which the
older novelists severely described as “rude
health.”
Hawkesbury students were polite guides
and walking directories to piggeries, silos,
cow-bails, ostriches and oranges. Four
thousand acres were skimmed of their chief
interest in about two hours’ pedestrian tour,
on a day that was unseasonably warm.
Sheffield mopped its hot face, and tried to
cool off under the piggery roof. There was
rather a surfeit of bacon, and the Voices
got into the cowsheds with alacrity. “Ah
doan’t believe there’s such things as mi-i-ilk-
ing ma-a-achines, so there!” The York-
shire _ lass, confronted with the milking-
machine in full blast, meekly admitted that
she’d thought it “im-pos-sible.” The cows,
brought in for purposes of demonstration,
had a harrowing half-hour. Principal
Potts kept his eye on one contingent, and
implored it to stand away from the
milkers’ heads. Then another personally-
conducted tour would wave exclamatory
British arms in front of startled beasts
with their heads fixed in a wooden slot, their
hearts in their throats, and their eyes di-
lated with terror. Tea at 3 p.m., and a
dusty drive back to the township, which
looks as it had a past, and Yorkshire ad-
mitted it was thankful to rest its legs in
the train.
Under-Secretary Anderson, with Dr. Cow-
ard and some of the Sheffield soloists, motored
up to the College later in the day. Lady
Norah Noel, a very average amateur singer
and one of the minor disappointments—the
choir being a major success—was not
amongst the party. Intelligence Department
McMillan was the genial conductor of the
sprint.
The Sydney Domestic Science College is
within coo-ee, thanks to the persistence of
a little band of women who have been wear-
ing out their shoe-leather on Public Instruc-
tion stairs for the last two or three years.
Minister Beeby arrived at the Education De-
partment with an open mind, into which new
ideas can be poured ; and if ancient prece-
dent is broken in the process, so much the
worse for precedent. Mrs. David, who was
amongst the first to crystallise the domestic-
college idea, can now sit down and get her
breath.
In spite of prayers, entreaties, good wages,
liberal holidays, and an unlimited supply of
followers, the modern goddess of the broom
and dust-pan is as hard to snare as a city
sparrow. The bad old tradition of slavery,
with an indifferent kind of kennel to sleep
in, still damns domestic service. What it
was rather than what it is makes the call-
ing of “general servant” anathema to in-
telligent women. There is only one rem-
edy—to raise domestic work to its proper
place as a business which will have no room
for the incompetent drone. This has been
hammered at, till it evolved the shadowy out-
line of a Sydney training college for hired
help. The deputating ladies who, the other
day, extracted a Ministerial promise of aid
in this matter included Mrs. David, Mrs.
Leonard, Mrs. Holman, Miss Matthews, Mrs.
Hazelton, and Miss M. Hall.
On the eight-hour shift for domestic
work, Mr. Beeby spoke as a man. This pro-
mises to be the hardest nut for the house-
mother to crack. There is nothing on earth
so difficult to work by rule of thumb as the
domestic gods. They sit down to rest at odd
half hours during the day; they tumble over
saucepans and stoke the stove just when an
eight-hour time-limit would send off the at-
tendant priestess to walk out with a smart
young beau. Some men are punctual;
others are —variegated. The butcher, also,
has his moods. To-day he leaves a heart or
liver at the back entrance at the right time.
To-morrow, he won’t show the ghost of a
top-side till the siren of the stove —generally
the housemother- —is goaded to despair by a
hungry family. The chop on the grill, the
humble potato on the boil, the master of the
house on his high horse—-the clock strikes !
Everything ceases to go on happening. Eight-
hours are up. The master of the house, sup-
ported by a good conscience, dines off his
evening paper.
Two shifts of domestic help, as sketched
by Mr. Beeby, pre-supposes that evex-y house-
holder will have not less than £5OO a year.
The oi'dinary woman who has handled the
wages and up-keep (board and residence,
etc.) of one relay of domestic help is at pre-
sent scratching her experience for ideas not
linked up with impossible schemes. The
Saturday or Wednesday half-holiday, in ad-
dition to the Sunday jaunt, is here offered
as a better suggestion. It is more likely to
meet the Great Necessity—till tabloid meals
and the millennium arrive—than the eight-
hour drop-plates-and-saucepans proposal.
That there are young women in abundance
who want to give some kind of domestic
help and live by it, a Sydney man pi-oved
the other day. He advertised for a com-
panion for his one daughter—a young
woman to do just what a daughter would
be expected to do in a “middle-class” house
with “other help kept.” The mail poured
in upwards of 140 offers. A couple of years
ago a similar advt. produced over 200.
In spite of stories to the effect that she
was introducing something quite new, Queen
Mary has merely gone back to the Adelaide
manner in having her Coronation finery
waited on by some of Britain’s best-looking
girls. Macaulay, in a letter to his favorite
sister, Hannah, says :
Her (Adelaide’s) train of purple and ermine was
borne by six fair creatures. ... The Queen be-
haved admirably. The King (William IV.) very
awkwardly. . . . The Duke of Devonshire looked
as if he came to be crowned instead of his master.
The Chancellor looked like Mephistopheles behind
Margaret in the church. The ceremony was much
too long, and some parts of it were carelessly per-
formed. The Archbishop mumbled. .. . Above
all, the bearing of the King made the foolish parts
of the ritual appear monstrously ridiculous, and de-
prived many of the better parts of their proper
effect. The moment of the crowning was extremely
fine.
Besides the train-bearers, Adelaide had
“a crowd of beautiful women and girls” as
attendants. There’s no mention of any
male ornament of the Court being near her
person. The Dean and Prebendaries of
Westminster were just in front, clad in
copes of cloth of gold and other gorgeous
ecclesiastical millinery. The 1831 pageant
is apparently responsible for some of the de-
tails in the royal drapery display at West-
minster on June 22 —an item worth resus-
citating because Mary’s desire to suri’ound
herself with feminine charms has been so
persistently refei'red to as a novelty of this
1911 Coi’onation.
“The Balkan Princess” is a well-dressed
musical play which embraces no special nov-
elties, except a Duke who drops into jour-
nalism with inflammatory headlines, and
sits on dinner-plates in his spare moments.
Florence Young, Princess of Balai’ia, beset
by eligible Dukes, gets off her throne for one
act, and sees life at a Bohemian restaurant
in a fascinating blue drape with an Arabian
Nights’ cap en suite. This gives Duke Ser-
gius (Frank Greene), who is handsomely
upholstered in mole velvet and tights, his op-
portunity to make love. He does it joyfully,
sitting all over the food supply. The Un-
known Princess has no appetite for the steak
—not because a nice young man appears to
be sitting on the bread, but because it’s her
cue to sing.
Olive Godwin, as Sofia, gets every ounce
of atti’action out of a black-satin gown.
She breaks her heart, later on, in apricot and
cream, an elegant environment only spoilt
by a freak cut-away skirt, with a siding in
the middle of its front hem. All the clothes
are fresh and pretty in this piece. The
Princess of Balaria rules over her people for
quite half an hour, clad in a charming shim-
mer of blush pink satin under a silvery
gauze tunic with a long, quivering fringe
like spear points of light.
Miss Monie stage-managed a farewell tea-
party to Mrs. Percy Hunter the other day.
A handsome presentation bag, with a gold-
lettered front and a tame crocodile back,
was the excuse for a small ceremonial,
wedged in between ice-creams and closing
time. Mrs. Holman, as official referee for
those present, handed over the bag to Mrs.
Hunter, who wore a filmy non-explosive
gown of gunpowder blue. Ethel Turner
joined the elect at a table which included
Mrs. Trefle and the pink plumes of Dame
Carruthers’s good-looking hat. Miss Olga
Monie, one of this season’s debutantes, in
fire-brigade red with black furs, wore the
warm color that gives a benediction to a
bleak grey day. There were several musical
crumbs, vocal and otherwise, that didn’t in-
terrupt the conversation. When Percy Hun-
ter has assuaged his thirst for information
in U.S.A. he will be joined in London by his
wife and two boys.
Two ancient families met at church at
Peel, a sleepy village on the road from Bath-
urst to Sofala, the other day. One principal
party was Frederick Cox, a descendant of
that Captain William Cox, paymaster of the
102nd Regiment, who arrived in the Minerva
in 1800; the bride was Eileen, a daughter of
the Herbert Suttors of Bruccdale, and a
descendant of the George Sutter (sic) who
arrived in H.M.S. Porpoise in 1800. Bruce-
dale was the first home of the Suttors be-
yond the Mountains; its owner was William
Henry Suttor, who was the father of the
present President of the Legislative Coun-
cil. The officiating clergyman was Arch-
deacon Oakes, a grandson of Francis Oakes,
an early chief constable of Parramatta.
Brucedale was once a place of much con-
sequence. There, 60 years ago, Governor
Fitzroy met “a party of 40 gentlemen” at
dinner, and in the evening there was a dance,
at which “the very first families” were repre-
se«t»d. There is an account of it all in the
“Reminiscences” of Colonel Mundy, who was
waited upon by a soldier who had been
tried by court-martial for deserting the Col-
onel’s own regiment during the Canadian
rebellion, and who, after being branded with
the letter “D,” was transported for life.
The Hayes baronetcy—she was a daughter
of the late Judge Wilkinson of this city—
is still liable to go hunting for an heir to the
title, which carries with it nearly 23,000
acres in County Donegal, Ireland. Lady
Hayes, married in 1901, has still no son.
Her brother, Dr. Camac Wilkinson, who
worried the tuberculosis germ in N.S.W., is
now 1 on the top rail—which is to say, he
owns a brougham and house in Harley-
street, London.
By last English mail came a menu salved
from a London Suffrage dinner to Vida Gold-
stein. This menu has several features that
pick it out from the ordinary dinner card.
It scatters its colons —thus it’s Miss : Vida :
Goldstein —and gets off the common old blazed
trail of punctuation. And it alludes with de-
ference to the chairman as the Hon. W.
Pember Reeves, and gives him a tail nearly
two yards long. “Director of the London
School of Economics, and late Agent-
General t for the Commonwealth of New
Zealand.” Isn’t Maoriland sitting up in
front when Woman’s Vote is served with
the wine and walnuts'?
Miss Una Piggin, daughter of Mr. F. C.
Piggin, of Corowa, who is going to marry
Mr. Alan Buchan when the early wattle
shows its gold, is connected with the well-
known Ramsay family, of Narrow Plains
station, in the Riverina. The Ramsays
were once something considerable in wool,
but met bad seasons with less luck than
their neighbors-in-drought. Mrs. Ramsay,
sen., still a handsome old lady, was a Vic-
torian Miss Macdonald. The Ramsay hos-
pitality, 20 years ago or thereabouts, kept
the homestead latch always on the click.
Conceiming the occasional failure of the
Australian woolgrower, I fancy Rolf
Boldrewood has used a dramatic incident in
the story of the N.S.W. Desailly family. A
daughter of the late Mr. Desailly (he was
once a big station-owner out in the Far West)
was a small child at the time, and too young
to recall the exodus,_ but she tells the story
as her mother told it to her. After a long-
drought the station was mortgaged to the
banks. The drought dragged on till the
payment of interest became impossible. Mr.
Desailly surrendered to the mortgagee, and
with wife and children drove away from
the homestead. Before they reached the
first slin-rail they were drenched by the
heavy rain that saved the station—for the
banks.
During the epidemic of dressy concerts in
the Town Hall, the wildness of female
head ornaments has excited the sighs of the
person who sits behind them. The tame,
speckled domestic woman sticks a comb, a
small buckle, or a highly-respectable black
velvet bow in some eyrie amongst her locks.
But the untamed heathen craze for a spiky
row of stiff feathers, wide glittering bands of
dazzling color and various forms of head-
dress chic in the Solomon Islands, is getting
on the nerves of plain persons who can’t ask
the lady in front to “kindly remove her hair.”
With warmer weather our new Silk Cul-
ture Society will start hatching worms. The
Silkworm ladies meet every other Thursday
in the Botanic Gardens at 11.30. By their
mulberry leaves do ye know them. Curator
Maiden is mentioned as extending a friendly
finger to sericulture in this State.
Gustav V. of Sweden celebrated his 53rd
birthday in Pitt-street at the Swedish Con-
sulate. He is a poet, amongst other things,
and in 1881 married Victoria of Baden, who
has had a better-cushioned throne than most
of her agitated ancestors. The Consul-
General for Australasia, Mr. Von Goes,
played first fiddle at the local birthday party;
and vessels flying the Swedish flag in the
harbor made quite a brave color-show.
Ella Wheeler Wilcox, the U.S.A. poetess
most generally admired by the Girl, recently
scraped the surface of Japan, China, Burma
and India; and presently we may have
another tripper’s impressions. Meantime, Ella
lias shown that naive ingenuity of Woman
which has hitherto not found its" right outlet,
the Law. W. T. Stead took part in a
luncheon to announce the arrival of the
American lady who never makes speeches
and rather preens her feathers on this form
of total abstinence. Stead remarked that
though Ella might not be a woman suffra-
gist, one of her poems had been adopted as
a -war song of that cause. This broke the
Wilcox silence with a loud crack. She re-
plied in a Machiavellian sentence: “Though
my Muse may be a suffragist, I am not one.”
(N.B. —Can the Muse be sent to Holloway
for tearing a policeman’s uniform?)
The suffering rich have had another
game invented for their leisure moments —
those moments not filled with billiards, gclf,
bridge, _ etc. The new relaxation is called
“Solos,” and can be played anywhere. Here
is a list of the appliances: Two smooth gun-
metal discs with a hole in the centre; two
3ft. leather straps; two small white pegs
fitted into steel plates, and a measuring tape
sft. long. The Times says it can be played
on any piece of decent turf, even though it be
too narrow for law r n tennis. It might even
be played on Sunday.
Australian artists in London gave a party
on May 30, in honor of the visit of the
Coronators. If they didn’t give it, they
meant to, for a programme came by post
last week. Historic episodes, from the
discovery of this big garden plot till the
Commonwealth took charge, were to be
represented in the junketing. The com-
mittee had secured the help of Geo. Coates,
G. W. Lambert. Henry Fullwood, Fred
Leist, Bertram Mackennal, B. E. Minns.
Tom Roberts, Derwent Wood and Arthur
Streeton.
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORIAN.

When the Dashwoods visit the city the fun begins, for Old John
and Young Peter seem incapable of keeping out of mischief, and
manage somehow to entangle the whole family in their ludicrous
escapades until mother and the girls begin to long for home. For

“DAN KELLY, OUTLAW,”
BEING THE MEMOIRS OF DANIEL KELLY (BROTHER OF NED KELLY, LEADER OF THE
KELLY GANG).
Edited by AMBROSE PRATT.
WITH FOUR FULL-PAGE ILLUSTRATIONS BY PERCY SPENCE.

In the whole history of Australian bushranging no gang was more notorious than that captained
by Ned Kelly. Various accounts of the doings of this gang have appeared, but none bears hall-
mark of truth so pointedly as these leaves from a dead man’s diary, as edited by Mr. Pratt, who has
presented the story so convincingly that, for tense interest and exciting events, it has few, if any,
equals.
Dan Kelly was the only member of the gang who escaped the clutches of the law, and his account
of the last stand at Glenrowan is a veritable human document.

“THAT DROLL LADY,”
By TIIOS. E. SPENCEK, author of “How McDougall Topped the Score,” “The
Surprising Adventures of Mrs. Bridget MeSweeney,” etc. With 13 full-

PRICE, Is. POSTAGE, Id.
As Mrs. Bridget McSweenev is the droll lady referred to, the reader may
rest assured that he will get full value for his money, for our genial Irish friend
is funnier than ever. Her further adventures are brimful of delightful humor,
and lend themselves to lovers of good readings or recitations. As a healthful
tonic, Bridget is irresistible. Don’t hesitate about seeking an introduction to
her. You’ll never regret it.
“GAMBLER’S GOLD,”
By ARTHUR WRIGHT, Author of “A Rogue’s Luck,” etc.
WITH FOUR FULL-PAGE ILLUSTRATIONS.
Price, Is. Postage, Id.
Gamblers of all kinds have a place in this book —the card-player at the club, the punter at the
racecourse, and the larrikin at the two-up school. The scenes at the various places are depicted with
this author’s usual power and picturesqueness, the characters being splendidly drawn and the incidents
thrilling in the extreme. There is a description of a fight worthy of Conan Doyle; a callous murder
for which an innocent man is blamed; a night at a two-up school as vividly portrayed as on a cine-
matograph screen; and a welshing incident at the racecourse. Side by side with all this there is a
dramatic love story which ends happily.

FORMERLY music was the one art most neglected in Australian homes.
To-day foreign musicians visiting these shores after an absence of
several years exclaim at the growth of musical taste in this country.
THE PIANOLA, MORE THAN ANY OTHER FACTOR, IS
MAKING US A NATION OF MUSIC LOVERS.
It is not because the Pianola merely “ Plays the Piano ” that it has
achieved such world-wide success, but because it plays so artistically —
because it allows the personality of the performer such free scope.
There were “ piano-playing machines ” before the Pianola, and scores
of them have followed upon the Pianola’s success.
But the great musicians and authorities throughout the world recognise
but one piano-player as worthy of serious attention. As Scharwenka has
said : “The real difference between the Pianola and other piano-players is
in the expression which can be given to the playing.”
And then there is the STECK PIANOLA PIANO —the piano with
the Pianola built inside its case, as shown in the illustration.
A NOTED writer on musical subjects says the STECK PIANOLA
PIANO is apt to be purchased and used at first for amusement
only. Later, it becomes a wonderful educator, with the element of
amusement left in. And the amusement is of a higher order.
You probably don’t realise now how much you miss by not understand-
ing music better. If you follow the lead of so many others, and purchase
a STECK PIANOLA PIANO, you will experience a great awakening.
Light, catchy music is well enough in its way, but it is a very small and
relatively unimportant part of musical literature.
With the facility the STECK PIANOLA PIANO offers for playing
ALL music, you and your family may soon become familiar with music of
the better class. Then you could converse intelligently about music, and
appreciate a good opera or concert when you heard it.
There are not many things that supply both culture and amusement.
Is it not time you knew more about tbe STECK PIANOLA PIANO ?
Wouldn’t it be worth your while to investigate this instrument which has given to so
many others a liberal education in music and a thorough enjoyment of the art ? Let us
send you a catalogue of the STECK PIANOLA PIANO. We will send it tiee if you
will write and ask for Catalogue C.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, June 19, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
A well wrapped-up multitude turned out
to watch the unveiling of the ilopetoun
statue last Thursday; some hundreds waited
for an hour or more in the cold wind before
a vestige of gold lace appeared on the bleak
horizon. The statue, covered in flapping
eanvas, looked like a giant sheik, witn the
thin brown face peering out from the hood
and the hem of the robe flapping round thin
legs. (The legs belonged to the horse ; but,
from a distance, they looked like the thin
understandings of an Arab.) The stony
quadruped has its plot sown with grass; but
the herbage lias not yet .had time to spring,
and the pedestal rises from a bare patch of
earth surrounded by paths of new red gravel.
The stand of the unveilers was carpeted
with red and swathed with blue and gold—
lhe Ilopetoun colors. The sanctuary within
the railings was reserved for the most ele-
vated in Parliamentary circles, and the gate
was kept by a splendidly-garbed aide, held
together by a fine scarlet sash. Two rows
of galvanised soldiers acted as guard, and a
military band tried valiantly to keep the
function warm with spirited music.
The State Government House party ar-
rived in relays. First of all came Lady
Fuller, with the new Marchioness, Miss
Phipps ; and an aide. Gentleman-help Aw-
drey next arrived, with three small blue-eyed
Fullers holding somehow to his two pale-
bull' hands. The quartet circled the gravel
paths in a lost manner, and were presently
beckoned over to the stand. The entry of
Madden, C.J., with his lady and Acting-Pre-
mier Watt, provided the next excitement. At
iast, after a theatrically effective wait of
.several minutes, the band rolled forth Gor-
ave, and Governor Fuller drove up in his
astraehan collar, attended by his secretary.
The Marquis became visible about the same
time, and was tendered an impressive welcome
by all the uniforms present. He is a mascu-
line portrait of his mother, and looks about
twice the size of his late father.
The party and the crowd then stood and
politely shivered in the icy wind while
speeches were reeled off. So rude was the
blast that to the majority of the listeners
the makers of polite remarks were merely
human semaphores. Occasionally a violent
gust would attempt to do the unveiling, and
get the business over. At last the Governor
pulled the string, and everyone tried to dis-
locate his or her neck by looking straight up
at the revealed bronze, while a lowly person
at the back rolled up the discarded canvas
into a big bundle and departed.
The statue is a fine portrait of a cocked
hat and a spirited horse with a very small
Ilopetoun sandwiched between. The horse
prances on a mountainous pedestal just out-
side the gates of Federal Government House,
and snorts brazenly at the Victoria Barracks
opposite.
Colonel Campbell represented General
Hoad at the ceremony, the General being a
very sick warrior just now.
The young Marchioness of Linlithgow is
nearly as tall as her husband, and she added
to her height by wearing a long, upstanding
aigrette. Her coat was a gorgeous thing of
baby seal, with more seal in ropes round the
hem. Over it she wore a scarf of ermine
with a sable-edged stole. Lady Fuller was
also in a winter armory of furs. For this.
occasion she extracted from its camphor-bag
a full coat of sable, which enveloped her from
neck to heel. Mrs. Davy was in black, with
furs ; and the Madden lady wore soft mouse-
color squirrel.
The crowd that watched from beyond the
barriers included many of the Naicest.
Motors circled round early in the proceed-
ings, and took up points of vantage. One
stilled its throbbing heart in the gateway of
the adjacent Homoeopathic Hospital, which
flew its Red Cross flag for the occasion. Just
as the show ended the rain came down, and
the crowd dispersed under a mushroom crop
of umbrellas.
Immense preparations are being stealthily
made for the Gaud Mayoral Ball to be given
on Coronation Night. Mrs. Rumor is quite
certain that, it will be the most gorgeous
thing of its kind the Yarra villagers have ever
been bidden to. The feckless old lady fur-
thermore asserts that a daring group of
Sassiety’s youngest daughters have agreed
to take the floor arrayed in Pasha gar-
ments. She may be right this time. Cer-
tainly a leading chiff'onery is building quite
a lot of jupe-culotte dresses ; and everybody’s
newest garment is to be displayed at this
dance.
The Westralian Forrests, with two nieces,
are netrolling London in a fine new car.
Bigjohn has hardly yet recovered from the
glory of tacking up his knightly banner in
Westminster Abbey—the rite without which,
I am informed, no knight can be regarded
as a complete example to democratic dark-
ness. One of the dreams of Bigjohn’s old
age was to hang up the bit of haberdashery
in the Abbey and have it there at a Corona-
tion.
Just at present, an interesting point is
being .threshed out in the afternoon tea-
barns. A paragraph floated out from Fog-
land to the: effect that “Lady Grey-Egerton
had been commanded to appear at the Draw-
ing-room at Buckingham Palace.” This,
taken in conjunction with the news that
Queen Mary would not countenance any
person who had been through the Divorce
Court, started the crowd guessing. The
curious want to know if S’Rupert’s former
spouse has been made a shining exception
to the severe rule, or whether it was the
dowager of that name who had been bidden
to Court. The papers record the Dow-
ager's dress as being of amber silk ; but the
most painstaking cannot trace anv descrip-
tion of the younger lady’s garments. And
if no dress was there, argue the gossips, the
lady was almost sure to have been some-
where else too.
On a recent Saturday night, the Cale-
donian Society snread itself in a real Scot-
tish welcome to the Linlithgow Marquis at
the Grand Hotel. President Boyd was
host-in-chief. The clans sat down—with
a snrig of' heather beside each plate
—at 7.30, and rose some time in the sma'
hours. In the supper room, surrounded
with flags and hangings of blue and gold,
there hung a portrait of the dead Governor-
General. “We dinua forget, ye ken,” was
written below it.
The small, select council of the Bush
Nursing Association gathered last week
round a vast mahogany table in the Town
Hall council chamber. The City Council’s
cat, stretched luxuriously on the velvet pile
carpet in front of the glowing radiator, gave
a domestic flavor to the affair. Mrs. John
Madden was in the chair; and Lady Fuller,
havirfg been duly initiated, sat at her right
hand, surrounded by furs and closely attend-
ed by the cat and the radiator. Attention
was soon focussed on the Gisborne district,
where, it was pointed out, a medicine angel
was badly needed ; and, as only £5O more is
required to send one there, Gisborne will
probably soon get the nurse it wants.
A couple of kookaburras gave, a vigorous
demonstration at Wonthaggi the other even-
ing. A concert was occurring in a local hall,
and, while some youngsters were worrying
the “Kookaburra Song,” the birds woke up
on their perch outside and joined in the
chorus. The unwonted music woke up
State-Governor Fuller, who listened a mo-
ment and then joined in the chorus.
Quite a lot of Melbourne people are un-
der the impression that they have been
nearly frozen to death. One day a cross-
heading in the Herald assured them that
a thermometer outside the Weather Bureau
registered “two degrees below zero,” and ever
since then there has been a popular feeling of
icebergs in the air. “Zero” in this case was
the Herald’s definition of “freezing point” ;
the little difference of 32 degrees was, so
to speak, frozen out. It's a law of Chris-
tian Science that any citizen who sincerely
believes that he is down to zero must be
able to feel the cold accordingly; so the
Herald is responsible for an outbreak of
chilblains, blue noses, and bitter reflections
on “the weather we’ve been having.”
The first essential of chamber music, es-
pecially in winter time, is that it shall not
be freezing-chamber music, such as happened
at tlie Catnedral iiall the other night. This
was the third concert of a series arranged
by "the committee of the Lady Northcote Per-
manent Orchestra Fund,” and it started a
quarter of an hour late. The belated begin-
ning would have been forgiven if the music
had been calculated to warm the audience’s
toes. But the icy coldness of it! A bit of
Mozart by the string quartet was followed
by a Schumann quintet, with Una Bourne at
the piano. Thirty degrees added to the pre-
vailing temperature might have put the
Schumann matter in a melting mood, but,
as it was, it seemed hard musical fare. A
lot of people failed miserably in the attempt
to look truly thankful for what they had re-
ceived. And there were songs of Schubert
in the programme. Henry Thomas, whose
melodious voice deserved better opportuni-
ties, had been sentenced to sing three of
Schubert’s dullest contributions—“ The Ha-
ven,” “Angel of Beauty,” and “The Counter-
feit.” At no time of year could such things
be chosen by a singer who wanted an encore,
but Schubert is a German god among song-
writers, and Marshall-Hall, who lays down
the laws of music for the committee* seems
to be a less discriminating worshipper of
German gods than ever he was.
The bouquet of parasol dimensions has
been finally condemned ta oblivion by Bonnie
Mary of Buckingham Palace. The manager
of Drury Lane Theatre requested permission
to present the Queen with a bouquet when
she attended the “Coronation” performance
of “Money,” and the Queen sent word that a
bunch of vegetables would be graciously re-
ceived if it were “of a size that can be com-
fortably carried in the hand, as great incon-
venience is caused when a bouquet is too
large for that purpose.” Evidently King
George’s partner has suffered at times from
an unwieldy nosegay which became involved
with her trimmings. A Queen accompanied
from a theatre by floral-tribute bearers must
feel her position keenly. She is so unmis-
takably a public performer returning from
a show.
There was another inter-State knot-tying
last week at All Saints’, St. Ivilda, when
Clive Milne married Mary Cotter. The
Milnes are well-known Adelaide people, and
the bride a Melbourne girl; her mother was
a . Kennedy—which was a name to conjure
with amongst the squattocracy of 50 years
ago, and is still to the fore, though the gilt
has worn off its edges. The new Mrs. Milne,
who is a dimpled beauty, was given away by
uncle Pittendry, a Scotsman with bawbees
who has wandered out to live in ’Stralia.
The Dean of Melbourne put the questions
that are always answered ia the affirmative,
and bride and bridegroom each supplied a
sister to act as bridesmaid.
Miss Merrilies Lorrimer, the sporting
daughter of sportsman George, is to be-
come Mrs. Dudley Rand in August.
The only granddaughter of Marcus Clarke
recently had a christening. The child, who
has been called Marian Margaret, is the off-
spring of Ernest H. Clarke, third son of
the man who invented the “weird melan-
choly” of the Australian bush.
The wandering Carlyle Smythes have set-
tled down with a maid and a dog in an Ar-
cadian nest in Malvern, the suburb of roses
and State Governors.
One of the Kidman girls has been search-
ing Melbourne for trousseau flimsies for her
sister, who is shortly to marry her blue-and-
gold naval officer.
Dr. Booth and Dr. Greig, the two worn™
medicos employed on State medical inspec-
tion work, have been busy of late. The
other day there was a diptheritic outbreak
at Sebastopol, an early-mining quarter
which is now a series of deserted holes in
the ground, surrounded by a scattered and
rather careless population. Dr. Booth de-
scended upon the local school and scientifi-
cally swabbed the throats of the pupils. The
children, previous to her arrival, had been
worked up to fever pitch by the preliminary
business of measuring and weighing, and
most of them looked forward with dread
to the mysterious ordeal. They expected
some terrible medicine-man laden with a bag
of instruments of torture. Instead, there
arrived a sweet-mannered woman who
soothed them to cheerful inquisitiveness, and
soon had them swarming round her in the
friendliest way. She selected 41 imps for
treatment; and only the very last one cried
<>r '. n any way objected to the strange rite.
\\ bile Dr. Booth was busy at Sebastopol.
Dr. Greig was forestalling or killing a
measles epidemic somewhere in the hinter-
land ot Bendigo in the same easy way.
On a recent Wednesday, the Dental Stu-
dents and a lot of the established toothmen
put down the forceps and laid aside the
crown to dance painlessly with their women-
folk in the St. Hilda Town Hall. Smart
grooming seems to be a characteristic of the
craft. John Madden, C.J., was guest-in-
chief. and daughter Beatrice came along to
help with the opening set of lancers. A
round dozen of nice young debutantes twd-
stepped about the room, and not one of them
looked as if she had blossomed before. Bads
in the debutante set too often have a wilted
look; but in this instance they were fresh
from the garden. It is an unkind thought,
but it will obtrude itself —possibly the vet-
erans shun an assemblage of dentists, for
fear the toothsmiths might tell their ages
by their teeth !
Staccato remark overheard in an expen-
sive refreshmentry : “Yes, they're in London
now. And, fancy! They went to Monte
Carlo on their way, and won so much money
that they decided to go on to Nice!" And
the travelled cynic at the next table re-
marked under his breath : “Must have been
cutting it pretty fine in the matter of travel-
ing expenses. Nice is distant about one
tram-fare from the old two-up school."
If St. Judkins is looking for fresh wicked-
ness to conquer, let him attend a few
dances, heavily disguised as a wild young
man, and prowl round the supper rooms,
lie will find that Beelzebub has got into the
ices. The sweet coldness that Miss Sim-
plicity sips after a languorous waltz or a
jolly two-step nowanights is impregnated
with Drink. Absinthe and creme de nienthe
are favorite flavors; but there are others.
The Loreto Convent’s Past Students’ As-
sociation is blossoming into a big affair.
Amongst the members are several well-
known musicians, and they will make mel-
ody at the Austral Salon, on June 27,
when the Association meets for refreshment,
music and gossip.
Manager Wenman is back from skyrocket-
ing round New York, London, and other
places, with “The Chocolate Soldier’’ in his
suit-case. The stage general is a great
barracker for the intelligence of the Austra-
lian gallery. (The rest of the house he
reckons is practically the same everywhere.)
“Our gallery beats the band for sense,” he
remarks emphatically.
The key symbolising the “freedom” of
Malvern, which was presented lately to
Governor Fuller, was a costly bit of jewel-
ler’s work. It was of graven gold with a
big, “black” Queensland opal set in its
handle. In ordering the gew-gaw, at the
ratepayers’ expense, the local council no
doubt reckoned that it was doing itself
proud.
My Adelaide correspondent writes -
i South Australia has for many years been proud
of its Grand Old Woman. Catherine Helen Spence,
and a committee has been formed to establish
some memorial to her and her work. Various
suggestions boiled down to the double-barrelled
ope of a portrait in the Art Gallery and a Univer-
sity Scholarship. . Editor Sowden, of the Regis-
ter, is president .of the committee, and Mrs.
Young i 3 secretary. Both were closely associated
with Miss Spence in her work, and may lie de-
pended upon to throw life into the hunt for sub-
scriptions.

Adelaide is opening a big thing in hotels this
week. The site is that of the old York. The Grand
Central, as it is to be called, has 100 bedrooms, will
take' 150 guests, can seat 600 in the dining-room,
and is fixing up a roof garden so big that a tennis-
court will be merely one feature in the landscape,
so to speak. The place is built round a great glass-
roofed fernery-lounge. The York used to be Ade-
laide’s fashionable hotel, but the South Australian
and the Gresham, being close to rail, Parliament
House and the Theatre, remodelled themselves and
took top place. Lately Foy and Gibson have built
a great drapery emporium close by, and now they
have dovetailed the hotel on to it, the whole making
easily the biggest pile in Adelaide, with a dome
challenging that of the Exhibition. The street front-
age is largely cut into by drapery show-windows.
Great is the power of man’s Beer and woman’s Chif-
fons in alliance!
Rowing men who journeyed here for the inter-State
’Varsity boat-race, can’t have acquired a flattering no-
tion of our waterways. On the day fixed for the
race three shivering crews and a few frozen enthusi-
asts congregated on the banks of the Port River, and
found that amiable stream a seething maelstrom.
After some vituperation of the howling wind and
rain, the race was declared “off,” and, that being so,
the evening dinner was also “off,” for custom de-
mands that the winning crew shall be the hosts, and
there was no winning crew. The oarsmen finally
trailed over the course on Monday. Melbourne
reached the end first, and Adelaide last.
The University Sports Association gave its annual
ball on Thursday night. The fixture is always
hailed with joy by debutantes, and 34 were there
this year. In the past the dance has been held in
the Elder Conservatorium, the best hall this city
boasts for such a purpose; but the Public Enter-
tainments Act stepped in and said that if it hap-
pened again the hall must consider itself a place of
public entertainment, and take out a license. So
the committee fled to the Unley Town Hall, decked
it with scarlet poppies, and entertained their guests
—including the vice-regalities—there.
Inspectors are still investigating food-shops, and
their discoveries are depressing, to put it mildly.
Stables, fowl-yards, food and rats seem inextricably
intermingled, and citizens who live in the districts
concerned are either buying supplies from some un-
exDlored (and, therefore, possibly safe) quarter, or
following in Mr. O’Malley’s footsteps. The dailies
are supplied -in full with disgusting details, but
names are suppressed so that, literally, one nowadays
buys one’s pig in a poke.
The winter racing reason began on Saturday,
with the Cheltenham ■ meeting. It’S • a ' gathering
which only attracts the most sporting of females, and
Saturday, with its cheerless wet and cold, issued
no special invitation to anyone to tramp on wet
lawns. The A.H.C. finished its run on the course,
and the red coats struck the only color note worth
mentioning. . What femininity was there was firmly
garbed in dull and serviceable tweeds.

Captain J. F. Anderson, who formerly
commanded the Orient liner Austral, died a
few days ago. Financially he was reputed
to be one of the “strongest" mariners who
ever paddled in these waters, getting his
start from lucky Mount Lyell speculations;
so a life on the ocean wave had long since
ceased to have any attractions for him.
The Argus ought to he more careful.
For years and years it sobbed that, if the
wicked Labor party was given power, Capi-
tal would he driven out of the country by
Socialistic legislation. Lately, ir has been
lamenting the growth of Collectivism, and
showing incidentally that there ready isn't
anywhere for Capital to fly to. “The
American cities." il walls, “have completely
thrown over the individualistic creed which
was once the strength of the American
nation. They look closely after the health
of their citizens. They inspect their food
and drink. They watjch over their homes
to see that they have enough light, air and
space, and that the sanitary conditions are
as they should he." It is all dreadful;
still the Argus is consoled with the thought
that "the pendulum will swing hack again,"
whereupon the Argus will no doubt he quite
happy in contemplating dirt, squalor, had
air and insanitary conditions once more.
“Dun Ilrown": Why all this persistent
fuss about tlu- remark of the Scotch Com-
missioner that “nowhere had he noticed such
intense antagonism between employed and
employer, and rive versa, as in Australia” !
It was merely the truth. The Commission-
ers during their stay in Australia spent
their time in the company of employers.
They did not'hobnob with Billjim, or sleep
in the men’s quarters. They were at "tin-
house” all the time. And they spoke of
the feelings they saw and heard expressed.
I have been in many countries, hut in none
of them have I heard employers use expres-
sions so heavily loaded .with hatred
of the laboring classes. Judging by
their language, Australian employers in the
.mass have no respect for those they employ;
nevertheless, they are continually lamenting
the lack of respect among those they employ.
If you find the units of an arniy ill-dis-
ciplined and disrespectful towards ' the offi-
cers, whom do you blame? I know
whom the G.O.C. will blame —the offi-
cers. Of course the Scotch Commissioner,
having been the guest of a score or more
employers, could hardly throw mud on their
beards; so he contented himself with de-
scribing the true state of affairs, and said
nothing about which party it is that waters
its antagonism and gets up early to spread
fertiliser around it.

THAT round label on the Chateau Tanunda Brandy bottle is
insignificant enough in appearance, but it has a significant
qpeaning.
Placed there by the Commonwealth Government, it proves, first,
and most important of all, that Chateau Tanunda Brandy is a
PURE AUSTRALIAN STANDARD GRAPE BRANDY.
In these days, when doctors are protesting so vigorously against
the use of raw, fiery alcohols distilled from improper materials, it is
important to know that CHATEAU TANUNDA BRANDY is a
STIMULANT ABOVE SUSPlClON—guaranteed so by the
Government.
Before this label is affixed to the bottle, the Customs officers
have to be satisfied that the contents have been —
U Distilled WHOLLY FROM GRAPE WINE by a pot
still, or similar process, at a strength not exceeding 40
per cent, overproof.
2. Matured by STORAGE IN WOOD for a period of NOT
LESS THAN TWO YEARS.
3. Certified by the Commonwealth officer to be PURE
BRANDY.
GOVERNMENT GUARANTEED.

NOT ENOUGH BLOOD
TO KEEP GOING.
This Woman Fell away to a
Shadow*
Weak and Miserable; Heart
Palpitation, Headaches, In-
digestion; Cured by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills.
How good Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are in
cases of Anaemia or bloodlessness is shown
in the case of Mrs. M. Bell, 43 Belmore-
street, St. Peters, Sydney. She was typi-
cally anaemic, and had all the worst symp-
toms. She was attended by doctors but
obtained no benefit and continued to suffer
for years. Mrs. Bell says that Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills increased her blood supply
and quite cured her. The following state-
ment was made by Mrs. Bell: —
“I showed signs of anaemia at about four-
teen. My appetite failed completely. All
my strength and brightness ebbed away.
My flesh wasted till I was terribly thin.
My. blood was so watery that my lips and
cheeks and gums were like chalk. The veins
would not bleed at all if I cut my finger. My
feet and hands were always cold and
clammy. My ankles and wrists would puff
no very much. My head would ache so
distractingly, I had to get glasses, as my
eves were affected. I would feel utterly
done up all the time; I took no pleasure or
interest in anything. I dare not walk
quickly for my breath would go and my
heart would thump so furiously. I rarely
got a good night’s rest; I would just toss
about for hours and wake up after a doze
quite unfit to get through the day. The
doctor said I was anaemic, and I had a
good deal of advice and medicine, but found
no benefit whatever. I remained in this
state for some years. It was not till I
tried Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills that my
health came back. Some days I could not
keep my food down, and in any case I never
relished it. It had no taste to me unless it
had an acid flavor. I would get such queer
faint turns too, as if I was about to swoon.
I would long to sit about all day, and not
lift a finger. I started taking three pills a
day, as my mother, who had been cured of
Rheumatism by them, begged me to try
them, and after a few doses I fancied I was
a little brighter. My mother got some
more, and presently I could feel my strength
returning. I began to look forward to meal
time. My blood began to turn richer and
the color tinged my lips and face and showed
in my eyelids. The headaches yielded and
I took an interest in what was going on
around me. I began to fill out and in time
every symptom wore away.”
Remember, only the genuine Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills cure. Don’t be gulled into
taking something said to be just as good.
Of all dealers at 2s. 9d. per box, 6 boxes
15s. 3d., or from the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Co. of Australasia Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Senator McGregor, Acting-Minister of De-
fence, has found it necessary to give a hint
to some patriots he has met lately. His de-
partment, having asked a number of munici-
pal councils for the use of halls, rooms or
reserves for drilling cadets, was given cheer-
ful permission in most cases ; but in a few
the huckstering spirit was so strong that
they demanded rent for the usfe of the people’s
property by the people. In such instances,
McGregor has now gently intimated that the
Federal Government has full powers to take
any land it needs for defence purposes, and
if these bodies are too —-er —thrifty, it may
have to annex a shire hall or a public park
bodily.
THE STATE MEMBERS' TOPHET-
MUSICAL CHAIRS.
The Right Thinking person usually holds
it as a self-evident proposition that no coun-
try can prosper unless it takes as its wedded
wife a High Court sufficiently strong to de-
clare the voice of the people from time to
time bogus and invalid. Mr. P. Ilinderprise
goes further, and maintains that, unless a
Democracy is shackled by the decisions of a
substantially non-representative body, the
country will slowly subside beneath Pacific
waves. Yet Switzerland still remains as
much as 15,000 ft. above higb-water mark, in
spite of the fact that Lloyd and Hobson
(“Swiss Democracy”) find room for these
remarks regarding the Swiss High Court: —
Not merely are its members elected by the Fed-
eral Assembly for a period of six years only, but
tile Assembly has severely circumscribed its juris-
diction, removing from its purview a large list of
subjects relating to the administration of Federal
laws, especially those dealing with commerce, edu-
cation and finance. The Federal Tribunal, being
bound by the Constitution to administer every Act
passed by the Federal Assembly, or popular vote,
is precluded from declaring any Act to be uncon-
stitutional. The competency of the Assembly and
tlie people to pass and enforce laws is absolute.
Despite this dreadful state of affairs, Swit-
zerland still provides P. Hinderprise with
three square meals and a decent bed, and the
subsidence of the Republic isn’t noticeable
as .vet.
A CHILBLAIN CURE.
Mr. R. W. Jones, storekeeper, Carrum, Vic., says
that Chamberlain’s Pain Balm cured his chilblains
and wants everyone to know it. “I had one chil-
blain on my foot that was so big that I could not
wear my boot. In fact I could only wear my slipper
by slitting it along the front. I used Chamberlain’s
Pain Balm one night, and the next day was agree-
ably surprised to find that I could wear my boot
With comfort.”*
The S. Aus. Government is thinking about
State brickworks. There is a local “horrible
understanding” in the brick trade, and prices
for the limited output are so shocking that
the Government’s patience is just about ex-
hausted. The State has numerous buildings
in hand, so there would be plenty of orders
for the works to go on with.
At Brighton (Vic.), where Snowball of
Orange holds the fort for Conservatism, the
price of milk has been raised from 4d. to sd.
per quart, on the strength of an advance in
carters’ wages. Now, the milk-deliverers are
only benefiting to the tune of about lOd. a
day; a small daily delivery is 100 quarts;
and IOOd. is Bs. 4d. Thus the advance in
carters’ wages puts lOd. into the pocket of
the carter, and the boss-vendor grabs the
chance to put the other 7s. 6d. into his own
pocket. Then he sits down and writes a
letter to the Argus deploring the dreadful
greed of the working classes of Australia.
Yass and Queanbeyan are the census dis-
tricts within the alleged Paradise chosen
as the site of the Federal Capital of Aus-
tralia ; and some idea of the attractiveness of
the Eden can be dug out of the fact that
the population of Queanbeyan and district has
actually shrivelled 6 per cent, in the past 10
years, while Yass has only managed to put
on 2 per cent. Either Queanbeyan and
Yass have left off having young Australians,
or the young Australians who arrived earlier
have reckoned, after practical experience,
that Kink O’Malley’s enchanted garden is
a good patch for some other, greener person
to try to cultivate.
The suburban council of Essendon (Vic.)
has taken a step towards a Greater Mel-
bourne, by asking other councils to
a conference which should arrange the
creation of a central body to con-
trol matters relating to water supply,
fire extinction, sewerage, lighting, tram-
ways, etc. Unfortunately, Melbourne’s
stodgy City Council has the perverted idea
that it is the wheel and the others mere flies,
and its one idea is absorption. Concerted
action on the part of the suburban councils
could, however, shift even the city aider-
men, fat and heavy though they mostly are.
“Noman”: A few days ago I was in the
Bank of New Zealand, here in Queen-street,
Auckland. A lad was paying in money. The
teller tossed back a sixpence. “Can’t take
that—it’s an Australian sixpence. Tell your
boss, he will understand.” When the lad had
gone, I inquired: “Don’t you take Aus-
tralian silver?” “No,” he said, and added
that all the business firms, the Railway De-
partment, and the tram officials had decided
not to take the stuff. Next day I saw a man
tender an Australian florin to a tram conduc-
tor, who promptly refused to take it. It
seems this is Maoriland’s retaliation for not
being granted certain concessions in the cir-
culation of the Australian silver; and if
that’s so, it’s up to Andrew Fisher and lou
Ward to have a “pitch” about it on the way
back from the royal millinery parade, and
arrange matters. The present piffling ar-
rangement, besides causing heaps of annoy-
ance, is liable to earn for both peoples the
ridicule of Zulus, Choctaws and other com-
paratively civilised people.

The McGowen Cabinet is threatened with
trouble over the Conversion of Land Tenures
Bill which it promised in the Governor’s
Speech. David Storey’s Independent party
has hinted at its active hostility, and one or
two at least of the country Labor members
are alleged to be wavering. It looks as
though the Government will quietly shelve the
measure, and it looks more like that because
the Minister for Lands has introduced his
Declaratory Land Bill, which is to pave the
way for the Land Acts Consolidation Bill.
It would clearly be putting the cart before
the quadruped to consolidate the laws relat-
ing to land tenure first and introduce the
Conversion Bill, repealing a lot of the Con-
solidated Acts, second.
The workers’ rep. in the Westralian Arbi-
tration Court lately mentioned one more
beautiful result of State Frights—which
some foolish people mistake for Home Rule.
The State has an Arbitration Act, but it has
been de-horned and de-hooved and dis-em-
bowelled by the law courts, on appeal, until
little of the original animal remains, and it
is hard to say exactly what the remnant
stands for. The Act has never been con-
sidered by the Australian High Court, and
the law which governs the Westralian Su-
preme Court is the law laid down by the
High Court while interpreting some dis-
carded N.S.W. legislation. The result, as
the workers’ rep. put it, is that “although
we are supposed to be working with a living
Act passed by our own Parliament, we are
subject at all times to a N.S.W. law, and a
dead law at that.” In other words, the
Westralian workers have to fight not only
the Tories in their own Parliament, but the
Tories in the N.S.W. Parliament as well.
No wonder the Westralian workers carried
the Referenda, and want a uniform indus-
trial law throughout the Commonwealth.
The Irish envoys are getting good cash re-
sults in Maoriland. Subscriptions were well
ahead of the 1 >evlin-Donovan total before
half the Dominion was stumped. The present
crusade has brought out from the tall grass
and manuka scrub quite a host of unsus-
pected sympathisers. Home Rule is now
supposed to be a winning ticket, and there is
the usual rush to be on the favorite. The
dividend is expected to be drawn from the
good graces of the prosperous and influential
O’Gormans, O’Hagans, and O’Anythings.
A GOVERNMENT HOUSE TO THE
ACRE.
The Sydney press is horrified at the suggestion
that the' Governor-General, when travelling outside
the Federal capital area, should engage apartments
at hotels.
Impressive Person : “Go away, boy. I'm
not a circus agent. I'm advance-builder to
His Excellency, and these represent the
sites of vice-regal domiciles erected, or to be
erected, for His Excellency."
The census papers have had their first
overhaul, and, according to the preliminary
count, the Ma State has 1217 people for
every 1000 it had 10 years ago. The de-
plorable part of the business is that the big
increase is in the bloated metropolis. Out-
side the suburbs the' only three districts
which hav-e gained any population to speak
of are the North Coast, where the Cow
calls; part of the Riverina, where t;he
big squatter has been moved on by the
wheat cocky; and the Maitland coalfield,
whither some of the barons have gone, after
rooting out the handiest of the combustible
mineral at Newcastle. In numerous old-
established districts, like Bathurst, Orange
and the South Coast, there are actually
fewer people than there were 10 years ago.
These are the parts where the grip of the
big land-grabber hasn’t been relaxed; and in
most cases he will have to be hit by some-
thing heavier than the present land tax be-
fore he will let go.
The Greater Sydney movement got a
further lift last week when another subur-
ban council —the one that looks after the
perfumes of Botany—signed a 10 years’ con-
tract with the Sydney City Council for elec-
tric light and power. Botany has hitherto
got its public lighting from the Gas, Co., but
it figures out that it can save money by
patronising works that don’t need to make
15 per cent, dividends for shareholders, and
it also gets an undertaking that, while it
will never be charged more than the price
now agreed upon, it shall be given the bene-
fit of any reductions that the City Council
may see its way clear to make. And when
a few more municipalities come in to share
administration and other expenses, and the
cost creeps down lower and lower, even the
Right Thinking propputy-owner will begin
to see virtues in the union of the present
municipalities, if only on strictly business
grounds.
Judging by his recent remarks, Captain
Chambers, the imported expert who is to
boss the Commonwealth’s Naval College,
isn’t likely to be offered a staff job by the
Sydney papers:—
I am of opinion that a naval college should not be
close to a great city. In England the two great
naval colleges are removed from city influences. The
one at Osborne, in the Isle of Wight, is situated at
least a mile from the tiny Old World town of
Cowes, which, for the greatest part of the year, is
dead. The other is at Dartmouth, alongside miles of
uncultivated land.
That was one barrel for the Congesters, who
want the Commonwealth’s naval establish-
ment —lock, stock and barrel —placed in Syd-
ney, to go on with. Captain Chambers didn’t
give the Congesters the other, probably be-
cause it wasn’t his business — viz., that Bri-
tain, which carefully keeps its naval cadets
in the background, also keeps its naval sta-
tions in the same place, as far as possible.
At all events, it doesn’t moor them to the
gangway of its chief commercial city.
The anti-Labor (which is always the anti-
Federal) crowd continues to heave bricks at
the Government for its “mismanagement” in
closing the post offices at 6 p.m., which, any-
way, is three hours later than Private Enter-
prise keeps open its banks and insurance
offices. The most recent clod came from the
municipal council of Oakleigh (Vic.) ; and
when it had arrived, P.G.M. Thomas gently
passed Oakleigh a memo, showing that in. the
fortnight preceding the early-closing edict
an average of five-twelfths of a telegram had
been lodged per night at the local post-office,
and an average of lfd. had been received.
During the same period only one telegram
was lodged for despatch b'tween 6 and
7 p.m. After which, it was scarcely
necessary for him to add that he wasn’t
going to keep any office open for a suburb that
had a rush of business like that —it would
be cruelty to the staff.
On present appearances, there is going to
be a fight in the South Aus. Assembly next
session over the new railway offices. Pre-
mier Verran wants to put them behind the
city baths ; and other members of the Labor
party, led by architect T. H. Sineaton,
mean to stop him. If the new offices are put
where Verran wants them, they will block
the extension of the present railway system,
eastward. Adelaide trains all run into a
dead end, and there is no escape for the en-
gines that bring a train in until another
comes along and drags them out again.
Some day the lines may be taken past the
present station; but if Verran’s short-sighted
ideas are carried out, they will only be able
to do it by climbing or boring.
PATRIOTIC SONGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the Coronation Pageant of Empire all the
best patriotic English songs are to be sung, as well
us the National Anthem. — News Item.
Samples of popular variants appended hereto. — Me.
When England first, at Heaven’s command,
(I don’t think)
Arose from out the azure main,
(It never did, but we must stand for that dashed
perjury again)
Fool Britannia, that empty comfort craves;
Britain, ever, ever, ever sweats the slaves.
(Oh! the East Londoners with the cliilblained
faces.)
Britannia has no bulwarks,
No castles on her steep,
For Cobden gave her trade away,
And Justice fell asleep;
But Noise may save a nation
As it ruined Jericho!
And the starving creep through Starvation’s deep,
While the windy Jingoes blow.
(That is the law of national indigestion —the
poor are empty, and the rich flatulent.)
Another one!
Sons of the slum, all British born,
Starving British workmen out, and laughing
them to scorn ;
France has crowded factories,
And thinks it knows the game,
But they can’t drink beer,
Like us boys here,
Who add to England’s shame.
Finalmente!
God help our gracious King,
Let’s give him everything,
God help the King!
While we, with stomach cramp,
Sleeping in fog and damp,
Starve, crown our Rubber Stamp!
God save the King!
VICTORIA.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
THE LEANER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The lifter and the leaner sat,
And talked concerning this and that. . . .
“And so,” the lifter said, “we’re all
Lifters or leaners, great or small.”
The leaner, in the corner-seat,
With shiny shoes upon his feet,
And fancy-work across his vest,
Giggled and guffawed: “Leaning’s best.”
“Lifters?” he burbled, scornfully—•
“They work for men with brains, like me.”
He sucked his stout cigar, and sniffed:
“1 lean! It is the fools who lift!”
The train proceeded on its way;
The lifter raised his voice to slay
The leaner’s fatted self-regard
With facts and logic by the yard.
Yet, ere he spoke, the leaner went
Red in the face and wildly rent
His morning sheet in pieces twain,
And cast the fragments from the train.
“Monstrous!” he puffed tremendously.
“Another filthy tax on me ! —
Taking away me lawful rights—
Loafers!” he howled, “and parasites!”
VICTORIA.
