SPORTING
NOTIONS
Frank Thorn v. Bill Elliott, a Sydney-
sider, was a profitable battle of “feathers” at Melb.
Athletic Club’s room last week. Thorn had several
pounds in bone and thew to the good, and came out
(in top despite Elliott’s marked cleverness. The
Scrap went the full 60 minutes, exclusive of inter-
vals, and Thorn made his little pile of points in the
earlier stages. Elliott did not lose a too-great re-
spect for his enemy till 15 rounds had gone. After
that he broke loose, and began to wade in, letting
fly both hands with uncommon smartness; but his
spurt of audacity had come too late. He was easily
the top tyke in the last two, and pasted Thorn
gaily, without evening up on Thorn’s well-earned
increment. Thorn was thus declared the winner, on
points. This judgment should be reversed when
Elliott gets at him again, if William learns wisdom
by experiments.
Broke or not broke, champion pug John-
son managed to arrive safely in one piece in London,
and got into print immediately. His first effort on
arrival was to rig himself out in a frock-coat and
tall hat. Then, as usual, he found himself in-
side a huge new “buzz-cart,” as the learned stousher
calls motor-ears. He got loose down Whitechapel
way, and that was where he struck trouble in the
shape of two other vehicles. The affair developed
into a large-sized smash ; but the coon kept cool, and
remarked to the man in blue who took the names:
“I wasn’t speeding, but I guess my, reputation must
have travelled ahead of me.” Meantime there is
nothing definite about future fight-engagements.
* * * *
Clarence Weber, when refereeing a wrest-
ling match between Ryugoro Shima, who played jiu-
jitsu, and a Scotchman who played catch-as-catehee-
can, said he would tussle with the winner. There
was only one throw, and the Jap secured it with a
strangle hold. Now Clarence and Ryugoro are hag-
gling over the boodle, Clarence asking for a bigger
share of the gate because he is' the draw. “Not on
your life,” snarls Ryugoro ; “I’m just as big a draw
as you, any time. Let the winner of the match take
the lion’s share of the money—that’s good enough
for me.” And, certainly, it’s fair enough also.
The well-known S’African buiser, Arthur
Douglas, scored a decisive victory over Sydney Sulli-
van last week. Three days later he was in Sydney
Hospital, minus his appendix, and holering between
life and death.
Saturday afternoon in Sydney was, if any-
thing, on the warm side for football; but that, any-
how, was a welcome change. In the Rugby League
Competition, which is noted for the surprises it
springs on the cocksure propliet-barrackers, the re-
sults were in order on form, and the prophets were
jubilant. At the Agricultural Ground, before a
crowd of 5000, South Sydney unwound a back
machine which rolled over Balmain in the last half
like a juggernaut. The game up to half-time prom-
ised to be close, as there was only a difference of
one point in the scores, South leading by 6 to 5.
But evidently the Southern brand is the better, for
in the last ’ 40 minutes the men from the dusty
suburbs rattled up 18 points, while Balmain in
great travail added only five, so the game finished
with South on top—24 to 10. Eastern and
Western Suburbs had a mild encounter at the Sports
Ground, before a fair crowd, and the Orientals
proved too good by seven points —22 to 15. Mes-
senger the Marvellous, as usual, was the star, and
was well supported by Frawley on the winning
side, while Courtney made a mighty stand on the
side of defeat. Newtown, at Metters’s Ground,
did prettv well as it liked with North Sydney.
Shortly after the start Morton booted a goal for
Norths; but it was their only score, and the board
at the finish showed Newtown 15, North Sydney
2 The Glebe-Annandale collision happened at
Wentworth Park, where the red-garbed Glebeites
got home with 19 points to 10. Things were
t'airlv even till the last 20 minutes, and then the
’Balers just crumpled right up. All the same, An-
namlale, the baby team, is much improved, and it
is going to make a mark somewhere soon.
Union Rugby meandered on its way, and
as usual, was watched by the younger brother and the
maiden aunt of the players. But the aunt seems to
fetch along a niece now and again, and a young man
conies along after the niece occasionally. So thingi
are not looking altogether hopeless. The chief bump
on Saturday was between Glebe and Newtown at
University Oval. Up to half-time it looked like a
cakewalk for Newtown, which ended the first lesson
with eight to the good. Then Glebe pulled itself to-
gether, oiled its machinery, primed itself and went off
bang. It piled up nine points without having a soli-
tary one scored against it; and Newtown, the pre-
mier club last season, was thus pipped on the post
by one point—l 2 to 11. At Sydney Cricket Ground,
Western Suburbs let loose on the gentlemen from
St. George, and the Saints were humiliated by 27 to
5. An awful smashing at Union Rugby! University
and North Sydney had a desperate struggle at North
Sydney’s Oval, and out of the fray the battered and
broken Uni. crept with a lead of two points—l 4 to
12.' Manly met Eastern Suburbs at Rushcutters Bay,
and belted it fairly heavily, winning by 8 to 3.
South Sydney managed to score its second win during
the season at the expense of Balmain —14 points to 5.
In the League competition South Sydney
is still on top with 11 competition points, but it is
hard pressed by Glebe with 10. Newtown has nine,
and Eastern Suburbs eight. The Jonah club this
season is Balmain, which has lost the whole six
matches played. In the Union competition, Univer-
sity is top with 12 points, and Western Suburbs and
Manly are close behind with 11 each. Newtown has
10, and below that are all single figures, which are
uninteresting.
* * * *
Soccer football is spread out in Sydney
these days, and no less than three practically first-
grade competitions are being played off simultane-
ously. There are the Rawson Cup, the Association
Cup, and the Gardiner Cup. In the struggle for the
memento of old Bluff Harry, the well-beloved,
H.M.S. Powerful is on top with 10 points, the
nearest rival being Balmain with eight. In the
Association Cup contest Balmain leads with nine
points, and West Sydney is second with eight. As
for the Gardiner Cup—the Lord knows how it stands,
but the team from the Pahf’l seems to have a lien on
that utensil too,.
* * * *
Details of the football carnival to be held
in Adelaide in August have been fixed up. S.A. votes
towards the expenses £235, Victoria, £175; Tas-
mania, £265; Westralia, £350; total, £1025.
These votes will be a first ■ charge on the gate re-
ceipts, naturally. The carnival will be opened by
his Ex. of S.A. on August 2, when Victoria will play
N.S.W., and 1 after that State teams will encounter
State teams till August 12. It will be a magnificent
10 days—an all-Australian football Armageddon, for
the supremacy of the continent.
In the League scrimmage on Saturday
“the same old” Essendon came to grief at the feet
of South Melbourne, which kicked up 44 points to
41. It wasn’t an overwhelming victory, but, like the
sword-gash, it was enough. Carlton,
which won with difficulty from ’Wood last week, on
Saturday had a fairly soft thing in Melbourne, and
walked out a comfortable winner by 89 points to
56. The spectacle of Geelong scoring 72 to Univer-
sity’s 60 filled the crowds with wonder. Collingwood
succeeded in bringing off the “ ’Wood finish” against
St. Kilda —85 to 32; and Fitzroy upset Richmond
by 38 points to 36 —which was cutting things fairly
fine, and suggests that an accident either way would
have made history.
In t.h% Association rumpus Essendon was
in luck, and downed North Melbourne , by 72 points
to 44. P ? ran by 108 squelched Preston, which could
tot up only a poor little 48, though even that is more
than one expects from Preston nowadays. Footseray
and Port Melbourne were both blushing violets that
did not desire to do anything startling, and the gentle
little game came to an end with ’Scray on top—-54
to 33. Northcote smashed, up Williamstown with
48 to 32, and Brunswick Annihilated Brighton with
77 to 11. Mention it not in Gath, and forget the
horrible affair as quickly as possible.
* * * *
N.S.W. Lawn Tennis Association will again
wrestle with the question Of Sunday tennis,
and the members are summoned next week to once
more decide whether “Sunday practice” shall be
allowed or not on Double Bay courts. The Asso-
ciation is in a very rocky state financially, and it
is believed that the opening of the courts on the
Sabbath will result in a rush of new members. The
alternative is a visit to a Christian gentleman who
lends his own money, with liquidation to follow.
South Africa dropped out of the Davis
Cup lawn tennis argument last week, and thereby
cleared the way for the match between England and
America to see who incurs the expense of the long
trip to Maoriland, at the end of the year. If Aus-
tralasia pulls off the Cup this time it will possibly
have to place it in care of uncle, as the roaring from
beyond .the seas is a certain indication that the
othersiders are getting 1 tired of doing all the trav-
elling, and are beginning to feel that it is a long
way to come to get licked.
* * * *
In spite of the war of extermination de-
clared by the League of AVheelmen, the Victorian
Cyclists’ Union continues to make headway. It has
just completed its second year, during which its
membership rose from 50 to 480, and over. £4OO has
been paid away in ; prize-money. The Union is now
breaking into the attractive domain of road-racing,
and will make its maiden effort on July 1, over the
50 miles between Geelong and Melbourne; and on
September 2 there will be a 100-mile run from Ben-
digo to Melbourne. The committee announces that,
although a rebel body, its discipline is of the sever-
est, and evildoers will be wiped out mercilessly; The
Union’s remarkable progress shows that the League
must indeed have lost its punch.
The weather fined up for the June
meeting of the V.A.T.C. at Caulfield on Satur-
dav • but there was no luck about the course,
and ’ the going: was still heavy after the re-
cent rains. The opening event was the loolambool
Hurdle Race, for which eight went out, and the
public socked its money on Bob Gilder. Patrick
said 5 to 4 against,' and considering that Bob duly
won this was a fearsome exhibition of liberality on
the part of Christian. Bob had to be driven very
hard to make him win, and he bungled the last
hurdle badly, but managed to beat Lady Esker by a
length. The sensation of the race was the breaking
of a blood-vessel by Acer, which caused that prad to
drop dead. Another smash took place in the Wanda
Steeple; the top-weight favorite, Corntax, hit the
fence very hard and blundered badly at the next
lump, so his rider pulled him up, and then found
that the animal’s stifle was broken. The pistol gave
the happy release. The race went to Manifold s
Squirm, which wriggled away in great style after
Corntax pulled up. Most interest centred on the
Richmond Cup, which it was hoped would throw
livht on coming events. Riffian was prime favorite,
and got well to the front at the distance, but
there he was challenged by Obi, who will carry list.
51b. in the Grand National Hurdle Race. Obi came
with a magnificent rush, and, overwhelming the
favorite, won by three-quarters of a length. At
the present time there is much argument as to
whether it was a good run or not. Partisans on
both sides are very emphatic ; but writer’s opinion is
that it will be all the same in less than a century.
The Vic. Trotting Association keeps a sharp
axe on its woodheap. Lately it inquired into the cir-
cumstances surrounding the trot at the Marong (Vic.)
races on April 5, and having heard all that every-
body had to say, rolled up its sleeves and got to work.
The local club’s disqualification of E. 11. Ruedin
for three months for suspicious practices was altered
to “for life” ; J. A. Kennedy, driver of Prince of
Wales, was fined £25 for disobedience; A. Williams,
driver of Hazel Direct, was fined £lO for speaking
to strangers after weighing out; and J. Hawkins,
driver of W.S., was fined £5 for disobeying the
starter’s orders. And finally the Marong Club itself
was informed that it was considered a lax body,
badly in need of tidying up.
O. 11. O’Brien had a hard tussle with C.
tt. Reading in the N.S.W. Amateur Golf Champion-
ship final on Saturday, and got out with one up.
In other words, it took him a few less strjies to
biff a small ball over a measured expanse of terri-
tory. It was a most fortunate thing that he didn’t
win by two up, otherwise the Law might have butted
in. Throughout the struggle O’Brien had a num-
ber of close finishes, and his success is said to have
been due to wonderful grit, and that dogged deter-
mination which is so characteristic of the English-
man.
C. J. B. Wood, a Leicestershire (Eng.)
amateur, has created a batting record by going in
first in each innings against Yorkshire, and remaining
not out each time with, respectively, 107 and 117 to
his credit. The nearest to that record was when
Gehrs (S.A.) made 148 (n. 0.) and 100 (n. 0.) against
a Westralian team. But Gehrs didn’t go in first.
Despite Wood’s performance, the choir county came
out on top by five wickets.
* * * *
Widerstrom made small beans of Burton Smith in
the final dust-up'for the N.S.W. Bowls Champion of
Champions at the City (Sydney) Green last week, and
won by 31 to 17. Last year Widerstrom grabbed the
same bauble, and during the last six years he has
scooped in eight medals and various championships
of different dimensions. Unlike the ordinary
sportsman, Mr. Widerstrom is a satisfied individual,
and now drops right out to make way for the
younger generation. He is a wise man who knows
how to retire while his glory is still gilded.
Petersham continues with an unbroken re-
cord in Sydney lacrosse premiership, and at the
wind-up of the first round on Saturday led the crowd
with 14 points for seven straight-out wins. Sydney
and Stanmore struggle along together with 10 points,
and Mosman and Balmain come next with eight
points each; while Waverley, University and Manly
are practically pushed out of sight.
* * * *
Sydney baseball is providing a mighty
struggle this season. Prior to Saturday last four
clubs out of eight were dead equal in competition
points. But Saturday’s upheaval altered the posi-
tion, and left Waverley and Lane Cove on top with
eight points each, and Leichhardt and Petersham
pressing behind with six each. The rest of the
clubs were in the soup. #
Two French doctors, who have been mak-
ing* a study of the effects of aeroplaning, state that
high flying weakens the action of the heart. Most
branches of high flying do. On the other band,
swift and sudden descents produce delirium and hal-
lucinations, and any sort of flying at all is liable to
produce aerial sickness, compared with which sea-sick-
ness is said to be a dream of opium-like delight. A
theory is gaining ground that a lot of the smashes
are not due to natural causes affecting the machine,
but to the vagaries of the heart of the ’planer under
the unusual conditions.
* * * *
Peter Nelson, who trained Dick Arnst for
two of his championship races, is now tuning up
Harry Pearce. Curiouslv enough, the first time Arnst
defeated Pearce, the champion was trained bv
Peter Nelson, and people who see omens are trying
to peer through the mist. It was also Peter who
trained Arnst when he beat Webb the first time on
the Wanganui. Much wonder is expressed at the
departure of Fogwell and Jack Arnst under the cir-
cumstances, but it is obvious they reckoned Richard
was quite well able to take care of himself. Alt
Felton is to be the champion’s pacemaker, and that
Nestor of the game, Harry Floyd, will be mentor and
friend —so Arnst’s friends have nothing to worry
about on that score. Pearce’s pacemaker, by the
way, is his brother Sidney.
The chief sin in motoring is mis-alignment.
’Tis bad alignment or improper fitting together of
parts that causes broken lugs, leaky radiators, and
general Sheol. It is the mis-alignment of the front
wheels that causes the tyres to perish like Jonah’s
gourd. It is the mis-alignment caused by worn
bushes, or bad adjustment in the steering-knuckles,
which makes the turning of the steering wheel a job
to be undertaken only by a wrestler in training, in-
stead of being done by two fingers. It is mis-align-
ment between the clutch and the fly-wheel of the
motor which causes the clutch to drag, like a lady
going to the lock-up on a policeman’s arm, and toe
change-speed gears to grind like the mills of God,
and breaks off a tooth or two which jamb the gears
and wreck the show. It is mis-alignment between the
main and counter shafts of a gear-box which makes
the gear roar like the bulls of Bashan. It is mis-
alignment of the bevel pinion and wheel bearings
which causes excessive wear on the axle and ruins
the bearings, wearing them out in half their proper
time ; and the same thing produces smashes by throw-
ing the brake-surfaces out of line, and rendering them
ineffective. Mis-alignment is Original Sin and Pro-
destination rolled into one. #
“Kaitangata”: In obituarising Carrie
Nation, the window-smashing enemy of Derrmk,
The Bulletin (l/6/’ll) stated (not in its sporting
columns) that John L. Sullivan had fought Slavin,
but from Mrs. Nation he fled and hid himself in the
kitchen. Wrong! Johnel took care not to meet Slavin
after he saw the horrid mess Francis Patrick made
of Jake Kilrain in nine rounds, with gloves, whereas
he himself had taken 79 rounds and three hours odd
to beat Jake with the raw ’uni. Also, Johnel side-
stepped Peter Jackson on the color line, and would
have nothing to do with Joe Goddard when Joe
challenged him while he was “acting” in “The
Honest Blacksmith,” in Melbourne. But what a
fight that pair would have put up could they have
met at their prime!
Along the N.S.W. western line they’re
determined to put down professionalism with an iron
fist. A sizable school held its annual sports. The
boys are not a rich lot, so the money scraped to-
gether was small, and there not being enough to buy
decent trophies, they just divided the cash. One
youth won a footrace and marched off with half a
crown. The following week a local sports meeting
took place. The winner of the half-crown entered
and beat the local champion. The champion’s ad-
mirers were most upset till they remembered hear-
ing of the half-crown. Then they promptly dis-
qualified the winner as a professional.
A favorite relaxation of Equity Judge
Topp, of Victoria, is shark-killing. His Honoris
method is automatic and simple. He tightly corks
an empty bottle, ties round its neck a few yards pf
line with a big baited wire-snoozed hook attached,
throws it into the water, and “lets it go Gallagher” ;
in fact, he has half a dozen similarly-geared bottles
working at once. The shark swallows the bait and
swims off, with its movements hampered by the
bottle, which ultimately kills it, the resistance of
the “dead marine,” when pulled many feet below
the surface, being enormous and ever-increasing. The
Toppian bottle is a death-buoy.
“Ajacks" : Some early N.S.W. ped. prizes
were valuable. Devenport and Farnell, in 1854, ran
125yds. for £5OO. Dev. took the money by a whis-
ker. Following year Devenport beat Dalton over
150yds. for £2OO. At Penrith, in ’62, Single beat
Webster over 100yds. for £4OO. On the Sydney
Domain, in the late ’so’s, two giddy young kippers
of 60 (Robinson and Black) settled a dispute as to
who was the better runner. Robinson won the argu-
ment and £lOO. But there was at least one man
who padded the 'uof for pleasure. That was William
Francis King, the “flying pieman.” King set out to
be a parson, but his genial spirit and fondness for
sport led him away from the collection-plate. He
worked in the gloomy Tower of London, then came to
Sydney and was in his element. At first a school-
master, he eventually became barman at the Hope
and Anchor Hotel, at the corner of King and Pitt
streets. He once walked 1634 miles in 39 days
(only nine of "them fine) just for fun. He carried a
heavy pole, and even then beat the coach from Bris-
bane to Ipswich by one hour. For six consecutive
days he walked from Sydney to Parramatta twice a
day. He carried a live goat, weighing 921 b., and
121 b. dead weight from Sydney to Parramatta in
6hr. 48min. King did most of these things for his
health and not for his wealth, and appears to have
achieved his object, lie lived to be 81, and died in
the Liverpool (N.S.W.) Asylum.
KING SOLOMON’S BRUISER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Then King Solomon swore, saying, Adonijah shall
be put to ;death this day. And King Solomon sent
by the hand of Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada ; and he
fell upon him that he died.
\nd it was told King Solomon that Joab was fled
unto the tabernacle. So Benaiah, the son of Je-
hoiada, went up, and fell upon him, and slew him.
The King said, moreover, to Shimei, “The Lord
shall return thy wickedness upon thine own head.”
So the King commanded Benaiah, the son of Jehoi-
ada ; which went out, and fell upon him, that he died.
And the kingdom was established in the hand of
Solomon. „ ’
—From the First Book of Kings.
King Solomon sat on a priceless rug.
There was wisdom in even his shoulder-shrug,
He had temples builded, and gold mines dug;
But his proudest boast was the champion pug—
Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada !
For he was the man with the dazzling left,
And the swinging right, and the footwork deft,
Whose foemen lay with an eyebrow cleft,
Face to the sky, and of senses reft —
Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada!
And so the King had a golden chair.
And an army of wives on whom to stare,
With some —lady friends—who were also fair ;
But who was the hustler who helped him there?
Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada!
King Solomon lived in a style serene,
With gold-bags bigger than Solomon Green ;
Swapped jewels and winks with Sheba’s queen;
But somebody else had a punch more clean —
Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada !
King Solomon’s mines they have petered out,
And some of his wisdom -we’ve dared to flout;
But the world still sees, with a gasping doubt,
The invincible punch and the terrible shout
Of Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada !
If you read the Book (as you ought to do),
’Tis Solomon, Solomon, pages through ;
But I like this brief and vanishing view
Of the smiter in First Kings, Chapter Two,
Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada !
“The kingdom was made by Solomon’s hand,”
His palace was rich and his temple grand ;
Yet I rather think that they played the band
For the man who biffed at the King’s command —-
Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada !
When the blow fell ngmph ! on some Man of Sin,
The children of Judah ran out and ih,
Saying, “Praised be Solomon and his kin,
For good old Benny is at it again—
Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada!”
Who is the man whom the world admires ?
Splendiferous Dudley or William Squires,
King Solomon getting his heart’s desires,
Or Ben with the biceps of bunched steel wires?
Benaiah, the son of Jehoiada !

Assessments totalling £178,000 have been
made under the new Victorian State Land
Tax Act —the Act which gave benefits to the
big landlord, and put burdens on the small
land-owner. Watt estimated the revenue
from the tax at between £240,000 and
£250,000. But, while Fisher was castigated
for cautiously estimating the Federal land-
tax revenue at £200,000 under the actual
amount, Willie’s much less-successful esti-
mate has only been made the subject of a
jocular reference in the Sluggish press. Aus-
tralia’s financiers are only financiers when
they don’t belong to the Labor party.
George Garson, of Melbourne, who died
the other day, brought his hard head from
the Orkney Islands. Coming to Victoria in
1888, he was appointed State engineer in
connection with the Macorna and Ivow
Swamp water schemes. After that he was
on the Echuca and Waranga Trust, and
then became the State’s Deputy. Chief En-
gmeer for> Water Supply, and a member of
the Water Supply Commission. All his life
he had lived by Vater, and in the end he
perished, suddenly after taking an evening
bath. A brother of Carson’s is the distin-
guished Egyptologist at the British Museum.

New EDISON
Records for July
The new Edison Records for July will be on sale at all dealers on Friday, the 23rd of June.
The lists comprise a number of exceptionally good Records, for which we anticipate a
heavy demand. The most prominent among these are the selections by the famous
Sousa Band (at present in Australia), Marie Narelle, Alexander Prince, Ada [ones,
Peter Dawson, and other popular artists. Special attention Is drawn to the Record
by the world-famous actress, Sara Bernhardt. This number is a histrionic triumph.
Call on the nearest dealer and ask him to play over some of these Records for you.
If you are not aware of the address of a dealer, write to us direct, and we will tell you
that of the nearest. We will also send catalogue and lists of Edison Phonographs
and Records.

(of any State) accompany.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CONTRIBUTORS.
Genius: Nothing very striking about them.... The
Chief: Can’t see a picture in it....V.D.: “Types-
The East” received. Judging by the distance the
head is raised from the body, thought at first it was
a humorous drawing, entitled “The Yeast.”
The Wade Ministers were a poor lot in the
way of nomenclature, about the best they could
produce being Samuel Wilkinson Moore and Charles
Gregory himself. On the other hand, observe
the blooms in the M ;Gowen collection—James
Sinclair Taylor McGowen, Nieis Rasmus Wilson
Nielsen, George Stephenson Beeby, an i Ambrose
Campbell Carmichael. If Alf Edden’s father had
only called him Rowland Geoffrey, and Donald
Macdonell had been christened Godolphus Leich-
hardt, the list would have balanced more evenly
and the visitors’ book at Government House could
have defiantly thrown itself open to the critical
eye of Society.
The London Corporation has decided to
build a new bridge over the Thames, in a
line with St. Paul’s, at a cost of £1,600,000.
There is an outcry led by the Times, be-
cause the bridge won’t give a good view of
St. Paul’s, owing to a bend on the South-
wark side of the river. The Times and its
supporters want a long, straight cut from the
heart of Southwark, right to the middle of
the south front of St. Paul’s, which would
give a magnificent view. But the corpora-
tion says'that would cost a million more,
and it can’t see value for the money.
“P. Knutt”: In introducing penny, post-
age throughout the Commonwealth the
Fisher Government showed a lack of con-
sideration for the feelings of a certain sec-
tion of the community that brands it as a
class legislator of the worst type. I refer
to the Prodigal Sons, who occasionally bor-
row a stamp wherewith to write to a
widowed and dying mother. Ere the foot
of Labor was on this country’s neck, only
three such stamps were needed; but now the
suffering Prodigal must obtain six, particu-
larly beyond the. cities where all drinks are
fid. Private Enterprise has got to do a per-
ish in Australia, L can see.

DARLING DOWNS, QUEENSLAND
For 25 Years the home of the late HON. JAS. TYSON, M.L.C., the
place which he called “THE GARDEN OF AUSTRALIA,” situated
close to the Sydney-Brisbane Railway Line, 18 miles South of Toowoomba
Comprising' 34.500 Acres Freehold
WILL BE SOLD BY AUCTION AT
CAMBOOYA, on THURSDAY, 29th JUNE, 1911
At 1 p.m., on arrival of the Brisbane Mail Train.
In blocks of from 80 to 3,000 Acres (which includes the Homestead Portion).
Southbrook Railway Station only 3 miles from the Estate, Broxburn Rail-
way Station 4 miles, Pittsworth Railway Station 5 miles, Cambooya Railway
Station 6 miles, Greenmount Railway Station 4 miles, Nobby Railway Station
6 miles, and Clifton Railway Station 10 miles.
Cheese and Butter Factories at Pittsworth and Southbrook. Butter Factory
at Clifton, and Cheese Factories at Cambooya and Greenmount.
This is, without doubt, the best property on the Darling Downs, and was one
of the earliest properties made Freehold by the late Hon. Jas. Tyson when he
had the pick of the whole of the Country,
The whole of the Estate is admirably adapted for Dairying, LUCERNE
GROWING, Maize, Wheat, Barley, Oats and Potatoes, and a large area
of the country is ready for the plough.
Ideal Fattening, Breeding, and Lamb-raising Country.
Watered by Hodgson’s Creek, a never failing stream running through the
centre of the Property.
Subartesian Water easily obtained all over the Property at from 30 to 80 feet.
Rainfall averages over 30 inches per annum.

“Pera”: If “Hamer” intends taking the station
bookkeepers’ case to a Wages Hoard (B. 25/5/’ll)
he’d better not rely too much on the amount of
work they do, because, as a whole, they haven’t bad
times as far as work goes. Suppose they do attend
the store, milk the cows, kill the sheep, etc. —it’s
onlv a matter of two cows a day and one sheep;
and as for the store, it’s only a matter of selling
two or three bobs’ worth a week. The two or three
stations where the children have to be taught are
not worth calling stations ; and even with the teach-
ing thrown in, the average station bookkeeper would
not put in eight hours. So “Hamer” had better
base his claim on low wages, and the fact that the
bookkeepers are not allowed to get married. 1 can
give him plenty names of men who only get £1 a
week and no trip to Sydney ; also the names of some
who have been presented with the sack for wanting
to get married.
The great Panama Canal scheme, which means a
big access of influence to the ports on the Pacific —
Sydney and ’Frisco, for example—is marvellous en-
gineeering. To get to the canal proper from the
two oceans, the Atlantic and the Pacific, ships will
have to go up a number of steps in the form of
locks. On the Atlantic side there will be three
locks, forming a stairway to the canal level; on the
Pacific side there will also be three. The surface-
level of the canal will be 85ft. above the surface-
level of the sea. Entering the canal from the At-
lantic at Colon, the dredged-out beginning of the
canal is shown by buoys. Ahead of the ship will be
the huge monoliths of the Gatun dam and locks.
The dam has bottled up the waters in the valley of
the Chagrcs River into a lake called Gatun Lake, 32
miles long. When the ship is hoisted by the locks
to the lake level, it will have a straight run over
the lake waters to the Pedro Miguel locks, where it
will take one step down, and then to the Mira Flores
locks, where it will take two steps down to the
Pacific Ocean at Panama. As the ships pass through
the lock gates will be lowered after them. The con-
struction work is employing nearly 40,000 men for
10 years, at a cost of £60,000,000 sterling. One
cutting—the Culebra —is nine miles long and 312 ft.
deep at its shallowest point. Truly, Uncle Sam can
talk about his boundaries being the Aurora Borealis,
the Equator, the Procession of the Equinoxes and
the Day of Judgment!
“Ajacks”: Adelaide to-day and 12 years ago. In
’99, aged 15, I got my first .job. College education,
good typist, and had a medal for 200 words a minute
at shorthand. One of 80 applicants who answered
the two-line advt., I was engaged 7s. 6d. a week.
Average hours, 9 till 6.30, and 8.45 till 2 p.m. on
Saturdays. To-day I read this; in the original it
is a two-inch daily paper advt.: —
LOOK, BOYS, READ!
I am in need of
AN OFFICE BOY.
Now is your opportunity to get into first-rate
employment in an office that will encourage
you. SPLENDID OPPORTUNITY. NO EX-
PERIENCE NECESSARY. Apply immediately,
B—- and .
At 15| I threw up a “real good job” (still 7s. 6d.),
and the boss didn’t care. I believe one of the eight
boys waiting on the mat to get my billet was killed
in the struggle to push in first. I got the same money
in a land agent’s office where the hours were easier.
I collected rents and put “To Let” notices on houses.
Daily we had applications for reductions of rentals.
To-day I read this advt.: —
£2. willingly given to anybody for right
rental 3- or 4-roomed cottage; rent 10s. or 12s.
“Riew,” this , office.
A lot of crackers were fired in Sydney Chinadom
a few weeks ago when the Full Court, on the appeal
of an opium “fence,” decided that, although the
poppy drug was in illegal possession, its theft was
punishable with the utmost indignation of the law.
An irate Chow who had been rooked of his money
and opium by the “fence,” did the very thing that
was least expected—he took the chance of being
convicted himself by hollering for the police. And
the “fence” went up for three years on a charge of
larceny. The confirmation of the sentence will go
a long way towards breaking up a notorious gang
that has been making big money by robbing the
harassed smuggler on the supposition that he
wouldn’t squeal, and that the Law wouldn’t take any
notice even if he did.
“Petramos”: He was a small man, and kept a
small newsagency where he did a small business. All
day he had been worried by people who wanted to
buy stamps, and he had no stamps. He said so
politely, but firmly ; and finally he made it clear to
the polished gentleman in the plug hat. “No, sir;
we do not keep stamps, but we have a fine brand of
hair-oil and a splendid acetylene gas plant. We can
supply you with a ton of hay or roll you home a ripe
water-melon. We can procure you a shipment of
Angora goats or sell you a sausage-roll, delivered at
your door, for tuppence. But we don’t sell stamps.”
The polished gentleman stared vacantly at the little
man. “Could you tell me where I could buy
stamps?’’ he asked. The little ‘man clasped his brow
as ’ agitatedly as a spiritualist getting a message
from Mars. “You might get them at the iron-
monger’s,” he said at length. “Or, then, again, the
ham shop will probably keep them. The brewery
round the corner is almost sure to have them, but
the managing-director is away. Of course there is
the marine store dealer’s, apd the ammunition com-
pany gives one away to every purchaser of a barrel
of gunpowder. But if I might be permitted to advise
you, I’d say ‘Go to the Devil.’ ”
“Hamer” : By investigation I have found that the
moving spirits in about 90 per cent, of the business
concerns which label themselves with names imply-
ing that they are more or less American are Aus-
tralians born and bred. If an Australian wants the
public to know that he is going to hustle and be
up-to-date, he considers that the best way to do it
is to stvle his concern the American Blankiness Co.
or the New York Meatless Meat Co., as the case may
be. Two leading “American” teeth merchants, for
instance, come, one from Young (N.S.W.) and the
other from Maoriland. Isn’t it about time that the
Australian who can hustle gave his country the
credit of producing him ? And isn’t it about time
that the Australian passed him a hint that, if he
gives Australia a back-hander in his present fashion,
it may lead to anaemia, and a sinking feeling in the
stomach, and chest troubles?
romance is dead.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The ovster-haunt is like a dream
Wherein hlest souls may fare,
And from its rose-lit precincts stream
Rich scents and savors rare;
And as I gaze in joyaunce glad,
1 see, all framed in light,
Full many a noble Grecian, clad
In shirt-sleeves wondrous white!
As one that enters Heaven’s zone,
Bare-browed 1 pierce the shrine,
And sit me at a marble throne
ltich-stained with ruby wine.
Then, like some god from cloud o’erhead,
I thunder, “Kindly bring
Sauce, coffee, oysters, wine, and bread,
Butter, and everything!”
Swift at the word her venom pours
Fate, on my soul accurst:
I hear a great harsh voice that roars
“Pay up da monna first!”
I slowlv rise; I pause perchance
A moment ere I part
To send one searing, scorching glance
Straight to that Dago’s heart:
Then stalk forth, fuming, fit to bust,
And shake, with mental hoots,
That scabrous cookshop’s foetid dust
From off my scornful boots!
What! save at grimy shekel’s cost,
Shall Genius ne’er be fed?
Ah, nay ! The Golden Age is lost!
Ah, Woe ! Romance is dead !
N.S.NV.

"THE CHILDREN'S FOOD.”
The S. M. Herald (15/6/T1) was so
worried by the disproportionate increase of
the metropolitan population, as revealed by
the census, that it went to the trouble of
evolving a remedy for the evil: —
An adequate immigration policy must be planned,
and people brought out for the raising of food, meat,
wheat and butter —-not the raising of luxuries, which
need a Customs barrier to enable them to hold their
own.
The Herald certainly might have men-
tioned land monopoly and the mad habit of
dragging everything to the capitals; still, we
ought to be obliged to it for having defined
a “luxury.” A luxury, it seems, is any-
thing protected by a Customs duty. Soap
for instance, or boots, or shirts and hats, or
trousers. And since luxuries are things one
can do without on a pinch, the Herald pre-
sumably wouldn’t be shocked in its motherly
soul if it saw its food-producing immigrants
chasing the early cow clothed in a wheat-
sheaf.

WILLIAM ALMOND.
BY an Order of tlie Chancery Court of
the County Palatine of Durham (Eng-
land) made on the 17tli day of October
1906. In the matter of the Trusts of the
Will of JOHN ALMOND of the Queen’s
Head Inn West Auckland in the County
of Durham Innkeeper deceased an inquiry
was directed whether WILLIAM ALMOND
hereinafter named survived the above-named
John Almond and whether he is now living
or dead and if dead when he died and who
are his legal personal representatives.
Information is requested as to the where-
abouts of the said William Almond if alive
or if dead as to when and where he died and
who are his legal personal representatives.
The said William Almond or any person
or persons claiming under him are person-
ally or by their Solicitors on or before the
80th day of October 1911 to come in and
as to the said William Almond establish
his identity and as to any other claimants
prove their claims at the Chambers of the
Registrar of the said Court No. 19, Elvet
Bridge in the City of Durham, England, or
in default thereof such order will be made
in the premises as the Justice of the Case
mav require.
Friday the 10th day of November 1911
at half-past 11 o’clock in the forenoon at the
said Chambers is appointed for hearing and
adjudicating upon the Claims.
Dated the 12th day of April 1911.
A. O. SMITH,
Registrar.
The said William Almond is believed to
have been born at Smethwich near Birming-
ham in England about 57 years ago, and
was a nephew of the said John Almond. He
was a Forgeman and lived for some time at
Lemington-on-Tyne and Consett both in
England and he went to Australia about
1891 and may have left there for South
Africa about seven years ago. If he was
alive on the 26th day of November 1903
he became entitled to a sum of upwards of
£3OO as part of the residuary estate of the
said John Almond.
GEO. WM. JENNINGS,
10, Market Place,
Bishop Auckland (England),
Solicitor for the Plaintiff.

CAIRNS
is picturesquely situated, has a healthy
climate, and the temperature in winter
seldom falls below 50°. First-class
hotel accommodation at Cairns and
Kuranda (Barron Falls) can be
obtained at a reasonable tariff, and the
fishing at Cairns is excellent. A trip
to the Barron Falls and gorgeous
scenery about that part of North
Queensland should not be missed.

BARRON GORGE.
MR. DONALD McDONALD (the well-known writer)
describes the trip as follows: —
“ T~''OR the greater part of the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs between the
f™ 1 Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest of the sea trips of Aus-
tralasia. The route is dotted with picturesque islands, covered with foliage strange to
southern eyes, and the boat is ever rounding headlands, streaming into fine bays and estuaries. The
famous Whitsunday Passage, between Mackay and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.”

Children Thrive v
on ANGIER’S.
There is nothing better for delicate children than a
course of Angier’s Emulsion. Bland and pleasant,
soothing alike to throat, lungs, stomach and intestines,
an aid to appetite and digestion, and a splendid tonic and
builder, it is the ideal remedy for children's ailments, and
no mother should be without a bottle in the house. The
medical profession prescribe Angier’s Emulsion not only
for coughs, bronchitis, whooping cough and all lung
affections, but also for scrofula, rickets and all wasting
diseases. Of Chemists, 1/3, 2/9 and 4/6.
“THE CHANGE IN HER IS WONDERFUL.”
. .8 Hampden Street, North Sydney, Aus.
Dear Sirs, —My little girl has been a constant anxiety owing to
loss of appetite and a very trying cough. I was so pleased to see
her relish her food after she had had a sample bottle of Angier’s
that I got a large (4/6) bottle, which she has just finished. The
change in her is wonderful; the cough is quite gone, she has put on
firm flesh and has a good colour and appetite. To have accomplished
this with a really palatable mixture is a great cause of thankfulness
to me and you have my full consent to publish this letter in case it
may persuade other mothers to try Angier's Emulsion.
(Signed) Mrs. K. WILKINS.

IN BANANA LAND
The Denham Cabinet, not content with
passing lines last session to the tune of ten
millions, announces a few more for next
session. As to finance —well, it got £l4O
from fining a few gamblers the other day,
and now feels fit to tackle anything.
The Scottish Commission deals Fat a
heavy one by “warning Australian employ-
ers to be less insistent on employees being
men without wives or families.” The pluto-
cratic rags that have been quoting the
Scotch Commission so abundantly have
somehow overlooked that item.
The boom in land values is felt even in
the far, far North. At Inisfail (Gerald-
ton), on June 13, one William Varney sold
50 acres of sugar land at the modest price
or £6O per acre! And this is away up
where the population doesn’t average one
to every three square miles.
“Hermit”: The northern fraction of the
Australian military forces has been in camp
at Townsville, and the officers’ mess has had
the joy of entertaining Lord Dudley. The
G.-G. may, however, not be the only dis-
tinguished visitor: the cooks of the officers'
mess were Japs, and, for all I know, may
be generals in disguise. Anyhow, what-
ever they are, what has the Commonwealth
Government got to say about the employ-
ment of Japs in Australian military camps
in any capacity whatever?
Died in Bx-isbane, aged 52, E. M. Lilley,
K.C., eldest son of Charles Lilley, Banana
land’s second Chief Justice. He was edu-
cated at the Brisbane Grammar School, and
was articled to E. J. C. Browne, of Little
and Browne. He then “read” with G. R.
Harding, and, when Harding went on the
Bench, with S. W. Griffith, now Chief Jus-
tice of Australia. Called to the Bar in
1882, he soon made his mark, and was in
most big Brisbane cases during the last eight
or 10 years.
The Queensland cane-cocky is beginning to
wonder if Pirate Ilinderprise is quite the
goods it is cracked up to be. Said 11. A.
Cattermull, president of the Cane Growers’
Association, speaking at a big growers’ meet-
ing the other day at Bundaberg:—
As a matter of fact, we cannot tell the worker
that the profits to enable all sections to do well are
not in the industry; all we can tell him is that, as
growers, we are not getting our due share of these
profits. For instance, according to the balance-sheet
of the C.S.R. Co., that monopoly shows a profit for
the past year of £404,142. Think of it, gentlemen.
This is equal to £1 out of every £8 earned from
i the industry in Australia going to swell dividends
for the shareholders of the C.S.R. Co. dearly that
is the end of the industry at which the mischief is
being done; that is the end of the industry at which
the profits are so large that grower and worker alike
are suffering from so unfair a distribution.
Nevertheless, when a recent deputation sug-
gested a State refinery as a probable method
of reconciling decent wages with a fair profit
for the growers, Digby Denham side-stepped
the idea as though it were a footpad.
Canon Jones, of Indooroopilly (Brisbane),
celebrated the fiftieth anniversary of his ar-
rival as a full-blown parson the other day.
He came out from the Cold Country with
Bishop Tufnell, the first Anglican Gaiters
of Brisbane, in 1859, and was the first An-
glican pilot licensed to practise in Banana-
land.
“Old Salt” : lie Bulletin pars reporting
that a Queensland jelly-fish the other day
killed a boy who was bathing up North.
One—l don’t expect it was the same one—
stunned the steamer Guthrie for three hours
on her last trip to Singapore. It hap-
pened in the Brisbane River, where the jelly-
fish swarm. Not only were the engines
thrown out of commission for the three
hours, but when the vessel got to Towns-
ville a part of the machinery had to be sent
ashore for repair.
MRS. MANGLES : “Now yer up
in the world., Mrs. Oldham,
I expects yer'll be dressin ’
fer dinner
MRS. OLDHAM (who has come in for wealth) Lor love, yer, Mrs. Mangles, we dresses
fer breakfast."
Bananqland's Railway Commissioner has
hitherto had £2250 a year; but, though
Charles Evans should be as good a man as
any of them, his appointment is at the cut
rate qf £1750.
Banqnaland’s new Railway Commissioner
Evans is giving the railway men of the
northern State a clean sheet as from July 1.
The old record book is to be done away with,
and a new system is to be introduced. Each
employee will be given a sheet upon which
there is a large space for particulars as to
service, promotions, commendations, etc.,
and a small space for fines, disratements
and cautions ; but while the large space will
he filled in with his record up to date, the
small one will start off in virgin purity.
Only future offences will he entered there.
The Bananaland Government is going to
give its first-class prisoners a few luxuries.
Their cells are to be supplied with a locker,
a table, a chair, pegs for hanging pictures
on, and a vase for flowers. Also, after being
locked up and bedded down for the night,
W. Sikes will be handed a book (selected)
and a light to read it by—in strict modera-
tion.
Bananaland, prolific in nearly everything,
is specially prolific in pests. The cane-beetle
has lately assumed the dimensions of Jack
Johnson, and the Government is on the look
out for an entomologist capable of knocking
the trouble out. Up north, at Mackay,
Mossman, Herbert and Cairns, local funds
are being subscribed, and the State is adding
its lOs.-in-the-f subsidy to the stake-money.
“Sunbeam’’: The women needn’t be given
all the blame for supporting the cheap and
nasty alien. Up here in Mt. Elliott
where the copper grows, and the A.W.A.
takes all kinds of care that there is no
loophole for the non-unionist or “scab”
white man, two Chows are doing flourishing
business, to the detriment of Australian
traders. And most of the flourish is made
possible by the miners who cross the hand
of the Yellow Agony with silver in return
for the welli ni cabbagee and all li’ fluit
ripened under John’s virtuous couch and
hawked to the doors of the canvas resi-
dences.
“Rough Coal”: “Geofred” is right when
he says that the “big-gun” shearer isn’t
worth considerng as champion unless he
combines quantity and quality from start to
cut-out. What sort of a real champion is
the “one-day sprinter” who tomahawks a
brag tally and spends the next day recuper-
ating? That’s where Ewen Cameron
proved invincible. His tallies seldom
varied, unless he found a “gun” trying
to force the pace, and then we knew what
to expect. In 1897 I saw him go through
Mutooroo, in the nor’-east of South Aus.,
where we shore 92,000 sheep, and he averaged
throughout within a trifle of 200 per day,
and the cut would have satisfied the most
avaricious cockie. Old man as he is now
(over 60), he would make things sultry if
he would come from his retirement on his
Queensland farm, and Zimmerle’s admirers
would not lack wagers over a six weeks’
run, among rough, wrinkled wethers, un-
der expert judges.
The Strife of the Street.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We laugh at the voice of sweet Pity,
What time we press on in the street;
In the cold, selfish lust of the city
We stride with our merciless feet;
And they who go down in the battle
Are trampled and mangled as cattle;
No ear can we spare for the cry or the prayer
In the strife of the pitiless street.
We heed not the plea of the tender,
When trumpets are loud in the street;
The gentle find ne’er a defender,
When furies with furies compete.
We crush with a curse what is nearest,
We stamp in the dust on the dearest;
With claw and with tooth do we rend without ruth
In the strife of the pitiless street.
With ruin, red ruin and ravage,
Goes on the mad strife of the street;
No cave-dwelling, blood-drinking savage
To match with our warriors were meet;
The blood of mad Roma’s arena,
The tearing of wolf and hyena,
Gome back like a dream when we hark to the
scream
Of the strife in the pitiless street!
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, June 17, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
We are in the throes of Coronation pre-
liminaries, and Mr. and Mrs. Hayseed, with
all the little Spear-grasses, are blowing into
town, to be put up by their friends or taken
down by the hotelkeeper. Some of them
had a peep at the dress-rehearsals, in the Ex-
hibition Grounds on Friday, of the children’s
pageant, where a correct-to-card representa-
tion of the Coronation ceremony was given
by a multitude of miniature dukes and duch-
esses, counts and countesses, standard bear-
ers, knights of the privy seal and the public
walrus, earls and girls, and all the other trade
marks of Royalty as blown on the bottle of
history.
Announced that our Agent-General is
about to relinquish the pursuit of the shy and
elusive emigrant, and. take a trip out here,
possibly to collect a daughter and son-in-law
who are honeymooning in the vicinity. I
hope he isn’t coming in one of the six vessels
which are to arrive or, anyhow, are to start
with Denham’s foreign railway navvies. No-
body seems to know where those boats came
from, but for some mysterious reason there
are several people who are prepared to take
a shade of odds as to where they are most
likely to so. During A.-G. Robinson’s ab-
sence, ex-Gov. Lamington will be civil to the
State’s pawnbroker in Fogdom.
The death of Edwyn Lilley, K.C., half-
masted most of the flags in the city on
Thursday. He never rallied from the paralytic
seizure that struck him down 10 days ago,
and passed on to wherever decent men go,
amidst general regret.
Dr. Coe took unto himself a bride! on
Wednesday, in the person of Connie McNab,
daughter of Alex. McNab, bowler and law-
yer. The ceremony was at All Saints’, and
the bridal swathing was of cream satin.
The most striking costume at the gathering
was worn by Mrs. Hunter Laidlaw, an aunt
of the ex-single lady, who draped her hand-
some person in black velvet and lace over
cream satin, and topped it with a Very
French roof.
The Miss Hook Co., or the Arcadians, as
they loved to be called, did fine business here-
abouts, and departed flower-laden and much
supped, particularly a small red-headed
maiden who is so very attractive that Aunt
Eliza says she can’t be nice.
The Brisbane rowing boys framed their
annual hop on Wednesday evening in a red
and black color-scheme. Mrs. Ernie Baynes
headed the ladies’ committee, and Mr. Hughes
was the hard-worked sec. Everybody else
enjoyed it thoroughly.
Singer Lottie Richter will shortly take her
very pure soprano voice to the Old World to
get a specal polish upon the top notes.
They’re worth polishing somewhere.
Word reaches me from a travelling friend
who has been near Melba that the singer has
beep cultivating a quite angelic temper, and
is likely to get through the Australian opera
season in the best of humors.
Miss Tempe Pigott, who has made a hit
with the Plimmer-Denniston co. in “No-
body’s Daughter,” hails from Brisbane, and
was well known for her earlier stage work
with amateurs. In one amateur show she
so fired a character part that the profession-
al “star” was blown out of the limelight, and
the rest of the co. hastily dispersed.
A wandering pearler from the north-west
tells me that “international complications”
are loudly talked in the Dutch East Incjies
over the recent seizure of two pearling luggers
150 miles off Australian shores by the gun-
boat Gayundah. He wants to know where
Australia got more than the recognised
three-mile limit, and I didn’t charge him any-
thing for advising him to wait, when he would
learn some points at law that will be prdfit-
able to all northern pearlers and trepangers.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
A great Australian, a man of brilliantly-
original ideas, is Willie Watt, Acting-Pre-
mier of Victoria. He lias ordered that the
prisoners in Pentridge Gaol shall celebrate
Coronation Day by a “typical British din-
ner of roast beef and plum duff.”
. Within twelve mouths three Anglican
bishops in the Maoriland province (which
includes Melanesia) have resigned—Neligan
of Auckland, Wallis of Wellington, and Wil-
son of Melanesia. Now Bishop Mules, of
Nelson, who is 74, and Bishop Nevill, of
Dunedin, who is the same, are thinking of
doing likewise, and already the cloister and
the hearth are agitated as to whether the
demand for more bishop will be met with
imported article, or whether local industry
is to be given a chance like it had in Wel-
lington recently, when Bishop Sprott was
appointed.
J. W. Allpass, who died in Sydney a few
days ago, was the last of five trained school-
teachers brought out from Britain in 1855,
to help establish the State school system
in Australia. He lived to be head-master
at Fort-street, and inspector at Newcastle,
Bathurst and Sydney.
Bookseller Will Andrade, the other evening, in
Melbourne, was arraigned before an enthusiastic
little shivoo to celebrate his return from a 15 months’
jaunt around the globe. Will has been everywhere
and seen everything, with the result that he ds a
more confirmed Australian that ever. He says he
prefers Bourke-street to Bond-street, and would rather
be governed by Andy Fisher than either Roosevelt,
the Kaiser, or the House of Lords.*

MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mountains, N.S.W.
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made by the new proprietor (Mr. W. H. Burgess), late of the Grand
Hotel Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season.
Medlow Rath is an ideal resort. The crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains, in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of the picturesque. Arrangements can be made at the Hotel for motors,
carriages and riding tours. , ~ .
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office-or your own residence-direct
to the Majestic Hotel. . , .
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views in the mountains are superb, and
hours can be spent in walks through the Hotel grounds. , T , „
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Caves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices, 42 Oastlereagh Street
W. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

A Song of Red Things.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
EED roses ! Nodding to me on the stem
Through scented gloom,
Ye hold all Eden’s fragrance by the
hem
With hands of bloom.
The velvet shadows of your petals’ fold
In wondrous wise
Gather all magic of the new and old —
God’s' painted sighs.
What never breeze or wavelet could express
He breathed in you—
The secret word, wrought in a rose’s dress,
Of all tie knew.
* * * *
Red hair! The splendid flower of woman-
hood,
Rich fold on fold,
Blossoms like burnished copper threads im-
bued
With blood of gold.
Such glory as tall angels learn to spin
On bobbins seven,
Whirring the wheels of rapture, void of sin,
In some chaste heaven.
Such flame as, streaming on the wind of life,
Sets hearts afire,
Sharpening the swords of kings for madder
strife
Than world’s desire.
Red leaves! With what a pomp the drift-
ing year
Falls to its close!
Gone is the poppy’s laughter, and the clear
Hue of the rose.
What flaunting splendors mock the greyer
sky,
The flying cloud—
Brave things condemned that wear, e’en as
they die,
Their liveries proud !
Spent blood of Autumn, sprinkled on the sod
Of a lost day,
Staining the white and fragrant feet of God,
Who walks that way !
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WEIGHT.
The Swimmer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DARKNESS had encompassed Nano-
mea Island, and Watson, the trader
from Nanomaga, was gazing dreamily
at the curving lines of phosphorescent break-
ers as they tumbled over the barrier reef, and
sent even, smooth sheets of snow-white foam
across the flat table of reef into the placid
waters of the shallow lagoon.
For three weeks he had been the guest of
the native teacher, and now he was anxious
to return to Nanomaga, where he had been
buying copra, and for three months had
been awaiting the arrival of his firm’s
schooner to take away his produce; then,
weary of waiting, he took passage in a pass-
ing German vessel to Nanomea, knowing
that the schooner he was expecting would
call there on her way to Nanomaga. He
took up his quarters in the teacher’s house,
much to the anger and disgust of the one
white man on the island —a trader named
Winchcombly—a drink-besotted, unclean
creature, illiterate and violent-tempered.
Watson had paid Winchcombly a brief
visit according to- traders’ etiquette. The
result was a quick quarrel.
“So .you are Watson, are yoq?” he had
said rudely. “A nice sorter white man
you are to put up with a Kanaka mis-
sionary when there’s a white man livin’ here.
It’s the likes o’ you as makes these rotten
natives so saucy to white men.”
Watson eyed the man up and down, from
his bare, dirty feet to his unkempt head
and ragged, tousled beard.
“You may call yourself a white man,” he
said. “I don’t. I daresay, though, that if
you used a bar or two of soap you might
get to resemble one, you dirty ruffian.”
Winchcombly glared at him savagely, and
then began to roll up his shirt sleeves —but
very slowly.
Watson laughed.
“Oh, I’m ready for you, Mr. Winch-
combly—after you have had a wash. You
know where to find me.” Then he walked
away.
As he sat smoking, he was joined by the
burly, good-natured teacher. Then suddenly
there came a chorus of women’s voices from
the back of the mission house: “E mu afii i
Nanomaga!” (“There are fires on Nano-
maga !”)
The two men ran to where a group of
women were standing on a little knoll, from
which a clear view of the ocean to wind-
ward could be obtained, and there, plainly
visible, was the glow of five fires.
“What does it mean, Viliamu?” asked
Watson.
“Trouble,” the teacher replied.
“Aye, but what trouble?”
“Three fires mean, ‘We are coming to see
you, and leave here to-morrow night,’ ” re-
plied the teacher. “Four fires mean, ‘We
shall visit you in a month from to-night.’
Five fires is a call to us for help, for they
say, ‘We are attacked.’ I fear that it is a
war party from Nuitao.”
Nuitao was 60 miles from Nanomaga, and
there had always been bad blood between
the people of the islands, and Watson knew
the native mind and its proneness to yield
to sudden anger for some fancied insult or
injury.
He turned quickly to the teacher.
“Your people here will go to their help,
Viliamu ?”
The teacher looked distressed.
“They dare not —for the people of Nano-
maga have the mai lafa (measles) and no
one man of this island would venture there.”
Watson thought a minute, then made up
his mind.
“Viliamu, I must go to them. They
have been good people to me. Get me a
canoe with food and water. ’Tis but twelve
leagues.”
By the time the canoe was ready he was
on the beach, talking to the teacher. Pres-
ently the surrounding crowd opened out and
made way for a stalwart, dark-skinned man.
clothed only with an aridi, or girdle of grass,
which hung from waist to knees. His long
black hair, save where it was cut squarely
across the broad forehead, fell like a mantle
down his cheeks and upon his brawny shoul-
ders. In the hugely distended right ear-
lobe was a clasp knife, a stick of tobacco
and a pipe. Stuck in the girdle of grass
the haft of a heavy knife showed itself.
Striding up to the white man he greeted
him in the Tokelau dialect:
“White man, lam Tarria of Onotoa. I
will go with thee to Nanomaga. I fear no
man” —he made a contemptuous gesture to-
wards the surrounding natives. “For six
months have I been here; I work for these
people. They pay me in food, yet they
will give me no wife, and so I have been
fined many times for woman-stealing. I am
a fine man, and many women like me.”
Watson smiled. “Then thou wilt help
me to paddle the canoe to Nanomaga? ’Tis
12 leagues. And I will give thee five dol-
lars and a home in my house.”
“Did I ask for money? Give me a mat
to sleep on in thy house, food and tobacco,
and I am content.”
“Good. Let us away quickly.”
Then the canoe passed through the surf
and headed for Nanomaga.
When daylight broke Nanomaga was 16
miles distant, and not visible from the
canoes, it being so low. A sharp rain-squall
came away from the westward; it passed,
and then the wind began to blow with some
force from the north-west —an unusual thing
at that time of the year, and in their favor.
After a drink and a smoke, the men lit
their pipes and set to with their paddles
again, knowing that they would sight the low
dark line of Nanomaga in another two hours.
Ten minutes after they had had the drink
of Hollands, Watson was seized with great
pains. He was a strong man of five-and-
thirty, and hoped that the attack would soon
pass away. But the pains increased; he
ceased paddling, and a groan of agony es-
caped his twitching lips.
“Art thou ill, white man?” asked Tarria.
“Aye, friend, I suffer great pain,” and
the native saw that his bronzed face was
white and drawn.
“I, too, have strange pains—-like those
which people suffer when they eat poison-
fish. But there are no poison-fish at Nano-
mea.”
“Give me more grog, Tarria. My head
swims, and my skin burns.”
The native poured him out a drink
into a cocoanut shell. Watson drank
it neat. Tarria took none, but bent
to his paddle again. The white man es-
sayed to do the same, but his twitching fin-
gers could not close around the haft of the
paddle. For a minute or so he swayed to
and fro on ' the seat; then, without a cry,
toppled backwards into the bottom of the
canoe and lay there unconscious.
For some minutes Tarria waited for him
to come to, but he lay apparently life-
less, except for his twitching lips and fin-
gers. Then the native, himself suffering
pain, pillowed Watson’s head on a basket of
food and took to his paddle once more.
Another two hours passed and Tarria had
made good progress towards the island.
W T atson was now partly conscious, and
raised a feeble hand for water. The wind
had died away, and the canoe made less head-
way. On the eastern sea-rim a low, black
line of cloud was slowly rising, and Tarria
knew that it meant rain and wind —the lat-
ter dead ahead. He paddled vigorously.
The black cloud turned to grey and then
to white as it rose, and the squall was upon
them —fierce tumultuous rain and a savage
hum in the wind. For a quarter of an
hour Tarria had all he could do to keep the
canoe from broaching-to. The squall passed,
the hot sun came out, and the native, after
baling out the half-swamped canoe, filled
his pipe, and, after covering Watson’s face
with his handkerchief, resumed paddling.
Another two miles and the line of coco palms
stood out against the horizon. But every
now and then short, sharp rain-squalls com-
pelled him to bring to.
Gradually Watson regained consciousness
and tried to sit up; but his legs refused
duty and he sank back with a groan. Tar-
ria’s black, deep-set eyes looked at him pity-
ingly as he plunged in his paddle-blade, only
ceasing from his labor when the sick man
called for water.
As the canoe topped the ocean swell Tarria
caught sight of the white beach —six miles
distant. Then came a mishap—Tarria’s
paddle snapped clean across at the head of
the blade. He took up the one used by
Watson and examined it critically. It
was made of light “pua” wood, and he ut-
tered an exclamation of disgust when he
saw that the haft had been broken and
bound loosely with coir cinnet.
For some time a cow blackfish and her
calf had been keeping company with the
canoe, sometimes coming to within a few
yards of it, then sounding and making off
again. Suddenly the mother rose right in
front of the fragile craft, and Tarria, back-
ing water suddenly to avoid running into
the bulky creature, broke the second paddle.
He tried to repair it, but without success,
for this time it was the blade that went on
both sides. Watson heard his muttered
curses and asked feebly what was wrong.
“Friend, I have broken both paddles. Yet
it matters not. The shore is but a little
distance, and I can swim there and get
help.”
“How far?”
“Less than half a league. Nay, try not
to rise and look! Dost doubt my word ?
What is half a league or five leagues to
swimmers like we men of Onotoa! Rest
thee quietly till I return with some of thy
peopleand as he spoke he untied his thick
grass girdle and dropped it in the bottom of
the canoe.
“But,” protested Watson, speaking with
labored breath, “the bakwa!"
Tarria laughed. “Fear not for me, friend
—never yet hath a shark harmed me. And
thou art in great pain, and”— He stopped
suddenly, for he was about to add that the
canoe was still so far from the island that
the people might not notice it.
“And what, Tarria?”
“And the old women doctors of these
motu (islands) have great skill and will soon
cure thee. Now, friend, I go.”
“Stay, first take a strong drink of grog,
and give me some too. I shall drink it by-
and-hye.”

The man poured out a stiff nip and took a
mouthful; then he spat it out.
“What manner of ‘rom’ (grog) is this?
It is bitter to the tongue. It is bad ‘rom.’ ”
“Let me taste,” said Watson, who now
remembered that the Hollands had struck
him as having an unusual taste. Tarria
passed him the cocoanut shell; he sipped a
little. It certainly was slightly bitter, and
a quick suspicion entered his mind.
“Pour it back into the bottle, and press the
cork in tightly. As thou sayest, it hath a
strange taste.”
Then Tarria took the two hafts of the
canoe paddles, laid them athwartships over
Watson’s head, and spread his girdle over
them to protect the white man from the sun.
Then he pressed Watson’s hand.
“Be of good heart, friend. I shall return
soon. Art sleepy?”
“Aye, I am drowsy.”
“That is well. Tiakapo.” (Good-bye.)
And, slipping over the side, he struck out
swiftly for the shore.
At noon Watson was aroused by the sound
of voices, and two canoes came alongside.
He was lifted out, placed aimidships in the
largest canoe, and given a young cocoanut to
drink.
“Where is the man from Onotoa?” he
asked.
“In my house,” replied Akapu, the chief of
die island.
“Is he well?”
“It is well with him.”
“And thou, and thy people? Are there
many sick?”
“Very many.”
Too weak to ask any more questions, Wat-
son closed his eyes. An hour later he was
in his own house. Akapu brought him
nearly half a tumblerful of brandy. He
drank it eagerly, and in a few minutes was
able to sit up.
“Where is the man from Onotoa?” he again
asked.
Akapu placed one hand on Watson’s knee,
and then spoke slowly.
“He is dead.”
“Dead!”
“Aye, he is dead. When he left thee to
swim to the shore no one saw him till he was
close to the reef. A little girl who was fish-
ing was the first to see him. And, as he
placed his hand on a knob of coral to draw
himself out of the water, a shark shot up
from beneath and bit a great piece out of
his side —so great that the bowels came out.
But yet so strong was he that he dragged
himself on to the reef and said to the child,
‘Hasten, little one, to the shore and tell thy
people that their white man is adrift in a
canoe a league and a-half away over there
to the west. Bid them hurry, for he is
sick and helpless.’ And then he asked the
child tQ bring him water and a pipe and to-
bacco. We came to him and he drank the
water, but, as we put a pipe to his mouth, he
lifted his hand and pointed seaward. Then
he died there on the reef.”
* * # *
Three and a-half years had passed, and
Watson found himself in San Francisco.
Standing at the corner of Sansome and Cali-
fornia streets, he gave a sudden start as a
big, burly man passed them. It was Winch-
combly. Watson followed his man, who en-
tered a low drinking saloon and then went
upstairs. Watson ordered a drink, and soon
found out that Winchcombly was boarding
at the place, and was “having a good time,”
also, that he was “a bad man,” and worth
leaving alone. Returning to his lodgings,
he opened his trunk and took out a square
bottle wrapped in brown paper. Half an
hour later he was back in the saloon, which
was crowded with “toughs” of the usual
’Frisco-wharves type. He scanned them
closely, but the man he sought was not
amongst them. At last he heard his voice,
and discovered him in a corner, seated with
two other men at a small round table, drink-
ing.
He went outside, and then re-entered by
another door. Approaching Winchcombly
from behind, he gave him a heavy slap on
his broad back.
“How are you, Mister Winchcombly?” he
said quietly. “I’m glad to see you again.”
Then, ere the man could utter a word,
he slipped round to the other side, took a
chair directly opposite him, and, quickly un-
covering the bottle, placed it upon the table.
Winchcombly half rose from his seat, then
sat down again. The two toughs stared at
Watson, then at the bottle, but said no-
thing.
Watson addressed himself to them. “Gen-
tlemen, glad to meet you. Don’t know your
names, but that doesn’t matter. Let us
have some drinks.” He called a bar at-
tendant, and the drinks were brought.
“What are you having, Mr. Winchcombly ?”
he said with studied politeness.
“Carn’t yer see it’s whisky ?”-was the
surly answer. “Anyway, what does it mat-
ter to you?”
Watson stretched out his arm and quietly
drew Winchcombly’s glass away, and placed
it beside his own.
“It matters a good deal, Mr. Winch-
combly. I don’t want you to drink bad
whisky when I can give you some of the
excellent Hollands gin which you sold me
on Nanomea, three and a-half years ago.
You remember, eh? I only had two drinks
out of the bottle —the rest I have kept for
you.” He threw out the whisky in Winch-
combly’s glass and then half filled it with
liquor from the square bottle, just as Winch-
combly’s right hand was sliding slowly down
to his hip pocket.
Watson was too quick for him. “None
of that. Put your hands on the table,
sharp.”
And Wincheombly promptly obeyed, for
the muzzle of Watson’s pistol was within a
foot of his bloodshot eyes.
“Drink,” said Watson.
“What for? I never did like square-
face.”
“Drink,” reiterated Watson grimly.
Winchcombly looked at the two toughs, but
found no sympathy in their callous faces.
Slowly he stretched out his hand to take
the glass, and then, with a sudden upward
jerk of his right knee, upset the table and
drew his revolver —a second too late, for
Watson’s bullet took him in the forehead.
He dropped on the saw-dusted floor.
Watson looked at him for a moment,
then turned to the crowd that now sur-
rounded him.
“Try that Hollands, you fellows,” he
said, as he turned to go away, “and see
how it agrees with you.”
LOUIS BECKE.
“A Mickle Blow.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HARSH winches clattered in the
night; dark phantoms came and
went—
Their shadows, doubled and grotesque, ’neath
shadow-burdens bent.
Foul flare-lamps flickered o’er the tide; like
lost, complaining souls,
Aloft the winds groaned through the gear
that swayed from bare, black poles.
Grey sunset with a falling glass a coming
change foretold ;
But still the baskets swung inboard, and, in
her hungry hold,
Up-tilted, threw their heavy weight, that
gives the engine soul
And buys the stoker bread and beer —the
world’s black servant, coal.
Night long the hymn of industry uprose, in
sweat and grime
These blackened cornermen of Toil earned
joyous overtime.
They trooped ashore with morning light;
then, wistful to the town,
We turned our dull, repentant eyes, and
laid the hatches down.
Our bridge deck clanged its loud command
unto the wardens who,
By virtue of certificates, in turn command
the screw;
Then slowly swung her bulk around, and
low her plebeian nose
Snuffed seaward like a dog unleashed, that
takes the trail it knows.
The rivets in her ancient plates were bright
—in ’sixty-nine ;
But in her frame she kept alive traditions
of the Tyne;
Infrequent paint her sides had known, and
yet her engineers
Had shipped with curses, and, betimes, had
left with oily tears.
All things about her spake of Use; the marks
of wear and tear
From truck to kelson on her lay, the grit
was everywhere.
She bore the stamp of Common Things;
’twas plain her homely bulk
Familiar lived with tip and crane, the
lighter and the hulk.
No subject for the cultured bard or critic
debonair,
Who “high ideals” cultivate —in regions of
thin air;
For in the social world of ships, where
many grades there be,
She filled the humble functions of a Scul-
lion of the Sea.
So with no waving cambrics out, no music
or display,
We slipped her shore-lines at the dawn
and went our vulgar way ;
And as she swung to face the roll from
shelt’ring heads between,
She laid her scuppers in the trough and
meekly took it —green.
Macßraid upon the bridge remarked to
dour Mac Oil below —
“Ye’ll need full head o’ steam the noo; we’ll
hae a mickle blow!”
The nether Mac with canty eye surveyed
his shafts of steel,
And to the tube laconic growled: “She’s
doin’ vera weel.”
We left the land in mist and haze to make
our offing wide,
From traps of Doom that starboard lay,
where bones of ships abide;
For, gentles in your easy chairs, and clerks
upon the stools,
The lessons of the Seven Seas were never
taught in schools!
And as a fighter treads the ring, to take
and make the play,
With human will and human skill, we faced,
alert, that day
The lean-flanked fighting seas tnat ducked,
and, gathering strength, uprose
And planted on her trembling hull their
dull, repeated blows.
Aloft, in dripping oilskin, swayed the steers-
man at his wheel;
Below they fed their furnace fires and oiled
the joints of steel,
And thus our day to darkness fell, and
groaning through the night
She pounded on, a risen gale and roaring
seas despite.
With ramp and race and leap and dive, with
thud and creak and groan,
She slaved, a Scullion at her task, on God’s
wild sea, alone ;
Her iron decks unwonted clean, fast hosed
by briny streams,
Loud anguish in her ag&d heart of moaning
ties and beams.
And when, long last, the morning brought
Macßraid’s returning watch,
A hoarse voice down the speaking tube in-
quired, in anxious Scotch:
“How fares it wi’ the engine-room? We’ve
had a fearsome nicht?”....
“It’s nae sae bad,” the Depths replied;
“she’s puttin’ through aw-richt!”
“Aw-richt” they trailed her in at length:
and now the Yarra tide
Goes crawling like a yellow snake along her
drab old side.
To-night the wharves are very still; the
watchman goes his round,
And from the streets of Melbourne town glad
• calls of Life resound.
The mate’s gone home to Clifton Hill; the
Old Man’s in his bunk;
A feckless gang of stoker men ashore are
getting drunk.
They’ll drink the Scullion’s health in beer,
for well these godless know
Her worn old plates, ’twixt Death and
them, stood staunch ten hours ago,
When, prisoned in the depths aglare, they
fought that “mickle blow.”
VICTORIA.
E. J. BRADY.
